Hewlett
The Red Page
The manner is still the dignity of
the writing, the Meredittnan pose, but Mau-
rice Hewlett, in his latest novel, Mrs. Lance-
lot (Macmillan), is undeniably getting
middle-aged. He still retains his romantic
attitude toward woman; he can still wor-
ship on his knees ; but beside such a living-
woman as the Marjorie of 11. G. Wells,
Mrs. Lancelot seems a pale abstraction,
strangely strayed from Open Country. The
robust, passionate women he can pictur-
esquely draw, but apparently he has out-
grown his medievalism, and now we have
his cold analysis of more modern passion*-
less ladies. The story introduces us to a
cold fish, the husband of Mrs. Lancelot,
whose ambitions lead him to countenance
the conquest his wife makes of a great duke,
apparently drawn from the Duke of Welling-
ton —the plot is laid in the time of the
Reform Bill in England. The husband prac-
tically sells, her beauty to the duke in re-
turn for political office; but the main
assumption, and rather an incredible one,
Hewlett makes in this “comedy of assump-
tions” is that the duke respected the chas-
tity of liis Egeria; and that, in fact, the
notorious connection, flaunted in the face of
the public, between the duke and the lady
was innocent. It is true that the duke tried
to make it otherwise ; but Mrs. Lancelot’s
innocence disarmed her , gross lover. Indeed,
when an impetuous poet cast eyes on the
lady, and won her love with a swift Hewlet-
tian ardor, the duke himself, seeing no chance
of his own success, helped the lover to his
feast. The husband, in this strange quar-
tette, is pictured for us as revering his curi-
ous benefactor, the duke, and learning, in
his cold-blooded way, to love his lost lady.
All very clever and strange, but built on
assumptions that hardly convince. Yet Hew-
lett tries hard to beguile us, and almost
beguiles. Thus, lie tells us of his duke: —■
He was a joyless man as well as a grief-
less man—bleakly in the mean; a cold-
blooded man; with sentiment instead of
emotion, and appetite instead of pas-
sion. ...A strange personality—as strange,
as any other human crea-
ture in this world, but no stranger. If one
single man among the millions of us knew
in and out one single other man, he would
find that he was not the unique phenomenon
he had always supposed himselL We are
all odd fishes, I believe; and that’s why we
shall never be Socialists. Our nature is in
the way.
And the husband is Similarly apologised
for:—-
He was a morbid man, a sensitive man,
but a _ just one. He could not deny to
himself that he had desired this friendship,
and had done his best to procure it—for his
own ends. He had hoped that Georgiana
would attract the duke, had invited it. Now
that it was done, how could he, in common
justice, blame her or her lover? He knew
that he could not. Lover as he was, he
must go on loving, and see his beloved give
herself to his patron. He could be nothing
but agonised—but he could not cease loving
either of the guilty ones He had been
betrayed by the two persons whom he loved
best in the world—and yet he went on loving
them both. Was ever such a position held
by a man of honor before?
And if you want Charles “to the life,” so
Hewlett assumes, hear the duke’s opinion
of him : :
“lie’ll never talk, for fear of letting him-
self know wliat he thinks of himself.”
And, to show the absolute cleavage there
exists between two foremost novelists like
Wells and Hewlett, let us consider their re-
spective attitudes toward the sexes. W T e
know, from Marriage, that Wells believes
that the woman demands too much of the
man; but from the mouth of Hewlett’s
second self, the romantic poet of this tale,
we have the direct negative:—
“Pray, is a career the prerogative of the
male? May it not be that your business
should be to serve her, rather? Not only is
it possible, but I tell you that it is certain.
She has the plain right to be served by any
man who has the grace to see that she is a
vase of piety, a fountain of honor and truth.
....You are a woman-user What busi-
ness had you to be dull with so lovely a
being at your side? You love! Do you
know what love is? Do you know that it is
a high ecstasy of wonder and service?”
So it is no wonder that the book ends
with the lover’s great cry: “By God, by
God, my Saint, I shall love you on my
knees!”
There you have the romantic attitude in
fiction—a little out-of-date, is it not? But
Hewlett is Hewlett—a feast of fine writing,
but, alack, with the fiery inspiration, the
impetuous elan, a little sobered by middle-
age.

Of immediate interest to Australasians in
the September number of The Round Table,
the quarterly half-crown Imperial review, are
the articles dealing with the necessity for
an understanding on the part of Britain
ihat the enemy of Australia is not Germany,
but Japan, and pointing out that the
acquirement by Germany of colonies would
act as a safety-valve to the otherwise in-
evitable British-German tension. Other
valuable articles deal with the Labor move-
nent in Australia, Closer Relations Between
Maoriland and the Commonwealth, and the
position of India and the Empire. For an
authoritative summing up of the questions
agitating the Empire The Round Table is
to be commended.
Everywoman, According to Wells
In Marriage, 11. G. Wells puts Woman
into lier pigeonhole, first in a men’s discus-
sion, then the wife to her husband, and
finally the husband to his wife: —
“Women,” Sir Roderick Dover said, “are
inferior.... to begin with, they're several
inches shorter, ' several pounds lighter;
they’ve less physical strength in' foot-
pounds.”
“More endurance,” said Buzard.
“Less sensitiveness merely. All those are
demonstrable things—amenable to figures
and apparatus. Then they stand nervous
tensions worse, the breakimr-point comes
sooner. They have weaker inhibitions, and
inhibition is the test of a creature’s position
in the mental scale.”
Buzard glanced at their moral qualities.
“More moral 1” cried Dover, “more self-
restraint ! Not a bit of iff! Their desires
and passions are weaker even than their
controls; that’s all. Weaken restraints), and
they show their quality. A drunken woman
is far worse than a drunken man. And as
for their biological significance”-
“They are the species,” said Buzard, “and
we are the accidents.”
“They are the stolon and we are the
individualised branches. They are the stem
and we are the fruits. Surely it’s better to
exist than just transmit existence. And
that’s a woman’s business, though we’ve
fooled and petted most of ’em into forget-
ting it.”
He proceeded to an attack on the intel-
lectual quality of woman. He scoffed at the
woman artist, at feminine research, at what
he called the joke of feminine philosophy.
“It isn’t a matter of education or oppor-
tunity, or simply that they’re of inferior
capacity; it lies deeper than that. They
don’t want to do these things. Thev’re
different.”
“Precisely,” ejaculated Buzard, as if he
claimed a score.
“They don’t care for these things. They
don’t care for art or philosophy, or litera-
ture or anything except the things that touch
them directly. That’s their peculiar differ-
ence. Hunger they understand, and com-
fort, and personal vanity and desire, furs
and chocolate and husbands, and the ex-
treme importance conferred upon them by
haying babies at infrequent intervals. But
philosophy or beauty for its own sake, or
dreams! Lord! no! The Mahometans
know they haven’t souls, and they say it.
We know, and keep it up that they have. . . .
No woman ever painted for the love of
painting, or sang for the sounds she made,
or philosophised for the sake of wisdom as
men do”
Buzard intervened with instances. “Madame
Curie,” clamored Buzard.
“There was Curie,” said Dover. “No
woman alone has done such things. I don’t
say women aren’t clever,” he insisted.
“They’re too clever. Give them a man’s
track or a man’s intention, marked and de-
fined, they’ll ape him to the lifd”
Buzard renewed his protests, and was un-
derstood to say that women had to care for
something greater titan art or philosophy.
They were custodians of life, the future of
the race— —• ,
"And tliaffs my crowning disappoint-
ment,” . cried _ Dover. “If there was one
thing in which you might think women
would show a sense of some divine nurpose
in life, it is in the matter off children—and
they show about as much care in That
matter, oh!—as rabbits. Yes, rabbits!
Look at the things a nice girl will marry !
Look at the men’s children she will consent
to bring into the world. Cheerfully!
Proudly! For the sake of the home and the
clothes. Nasty little beasts they’ll breed
without turning a hair. All about us we
see girls and women marrying ugly men,
dull and stupid men, ill-tempered, dyspep-
tic wrecks, sickly young fools, human rats !”
. “If ever we get a decent care for Eugenics
it will come from men.” said a white-faced
little man beside Trafford, in the confiden-
tial tone of one who tells a secret....
“Don’t w-e know,” Dover said, “we’ve got
to manage and control ’em —-just as we’ve
got to keep ’em and stand the racket of their
misbehavior? Doesn’t something tell us all
that if we let a woman loose with our
honor and trust, some other man will get
hold of. her? We’ve tried it long enough
now, this theory that a. woman’s a partner
and an equal; and does it work ? A woman’s
a prize, a possession, a responsibility, some-
thing to take care of and be careful about.
....You advanced chaps seem to want to
have the women take care of you. You
seem always to want to force decisions on
them, make them. answerable for things that
you ought to decide and answer f0r....8ut
they’re not helps—that’s a dream—they’re’
distractions, gratifications, anxieties, dan-
gers, undertakings ”
Buzard got in his one effective blow at
tins point. “That’s why you’ve never mar-
ried. Sir Roderick?” he threw out.
m man was checked for a moment.
I r afford wondered what memory lit that
instant s pause. “I’ve had my science,”
said Dover.
The wife to her husband '
“What are we women—half savages, half
pets, unemployed things of greed and desire
and suddenly we want all the rights and
respect of 50u15!....1t isn’t that we can
make you or guide you—l’m not pretending
to be an inspiration—but—but we can re-
lease you. We needn’t press upon you: we
can save you from the instincts and pas-
sions that try to waste you altogether on us.
....Were the responsible sex. And we’ve
forgotten it. We think we’ve done a won-
dei it we ve borne men into the world and
smiled a little, but indeed we’ve cot to bear
them all our lives. A woman has to be
steadier than a man and more self-sacrifi-
cing than a man, because when she plunges
she does more harm than a man. And what
does she achieve if she does plunge? No-
thing—nothing worth counting. Dresses
and carpets and hangings and pjfetty ar-
rangements, excitements and satisfactions
and competition and more excitements. We
can’t do things. We don’t bring things off!
And you, you Monster, you Dream! You
want to stick your hand out of all that is
and make something that isn’t, begin to be!
That’s the man....l’ve found my place.
I’ve been making my master my servant.
We women —we’ve been looting all the good
things in the world and helping nothing.
I’ve been making you fetch and carry for
me, love me, dress me, keep me and my
children, minister to my vanities and
greeds.”
J*
And the husband :
“And the women—dressing ! Perpetually
dressing. You, Marjorie—you’ve done no-
thing but dress since we married. No, let
me abuse you, dear ! You dress your minds
a little to talk amusingly, you spread your
minds out to backgrounds, to households,
picturesque and delightful gardens, nurser-
ies. Think of our tremendously cherished
and educated children! And when they
grow up, what have we got for them ? A feast
of futility... .And we’ve got to stop this
Jackdaw buying of yours, my dear, which is
the essence of all that is wrong with the
world, this snatching at everything, which
loses everything worth having in life....
You’re going to be a non-shopping woman
now. You’ve to come out of Bond-street,
you and your kind, like Israel leaving the
Egyptian flesh-pots.”
The Human Machine
A useful description of the working of the
machinery that we call our bodies is given
by Arthur Keith in The Human Body (The
Home University Library, Williams and
Norgate, Is.)- Man is one of the three
families of that group of mammals termed
the higher primates. The central family is
represented by the great anthropoids ; man
on the one 'side and the gibbon on the other
represent the two other families. All three
families arose from a common ancestral
Stock; but while the gibbon has clung to the
ancestral form, man lias progressively and
aberrantly evolved into his present position.
The great anthropoids, represented by the
gorilla, have kept in the middle course, and
with their coming extinction there will be
a wide gap between us and our nearest liv-
ing allies. The only two mammals that carry
their bodies in a peculiar way both reside in
Australia: the kangaroo and man. The
author shows the magnitude of the changes
implied by this remarkable manner of walk-
ing, which involved a complete revolution
in the organisation of the body. “There is
not a bone, muscle, joint or organ in the
whole human body but must have undergone
a change during the evolution of our pos-
ture.”
Some curious facts are brought to our
attention in this well-written book. Thus,
the face, from an anatomist’s point of view,
is realty part of the apparatus of mastica-
tion. It is an acddent, as it were, that the face
has to accommodate the eyes and the nose.
The facial part of the head is a bony scaf-
folding for the upper and lower jaw, on
which the teeth are set. .. .Legislation in
favor of woman can give the sexes equal op-
portunities of life, but it cannot blot out the
structural differences between man and
woman, for these have taken geological
epochs to produce. There is no possibility
of civilisation in time producing a structural
equality in the two sexes. . . .But for the dis-
covery of America and Australia the long-
headed type of skull (common to the Eng-
lishman), as opposed to the round-headed
type that prevails in Europe, would have
been but sparsely represented in the modern
world. The long-headed type of skull is the
most ancient. . . .With reference to color of
skiu, the evidence points to the stock from
which human races have arisen as having
brown pigmented skins —practically the
color of the Australian aborigines. These
have so many primitive features in the struc-
ture of their bodies that it is also possible
that their skin-color is likewise primitive.
The very black African and the very fair
European races may represent comparatively
recent products in the evolution of modern
races.... The long fingers and slender hand,
so prized as aristocratic, are really a rever-
sion to the hand of the gibbon... .And so on.
The author has some strong words to say
about the physical culture gentlemen who
aim at producing a voluminous chest and a
Herculean musculature. Such an over-de-
velopment, he says, is always bought at a
price. “The lungs are really delicate and
complex structures, and the fact cannot be
over-emphasised that they can be injured by
over-expansion quite as readily as by com-
pression of the chest. So far as concerns
the form of the chest, our ideal must not be
the prominent expanded type so often por-
trayed on the posters of the professors of
physical culture.”
The book is a plainly-written history of
the development of the machine about which
the average man knows practically less than
he does about a bicycle.
4.
A quite ladylike novel is Blinds Down
(Bell, per Sydney Book Club), by Horace
A. Vachell, author of The Hill. There is
nothing to offend the flapper in this quiet
chronicle of a dull English town; and the
symbolism of the house which always kept
its blinds down on the side that looked on a
slum is obvious to the most feminine intel-
ligence. The story of the mother whose
education in this genteel house led her to
wreck her life is paralleled by the story of
her daughter, similarly educated, but saved
by her mother’s experience from her mother’s
mistake; but the analogy is so close that
there is little interest in the end of the tale.
Just the sort of .anaemic, worthy novel that is
ordered in immense quantities by the cir-
culating libraries.
More Verses by James Stephens
Wind and Tree.
A woman is a branchy tree
And man d singing wind,
And from her branches carelessly
He takes wliat he can find:
Then man and wind go far away
While winter comes with loneliness
With cold and rain and slow decay ’
On woman and on tree till they
Droop down unto the ground and be
A withered woman, a withered tree ■
While wind and man woo undismayed
Another tree, another maid.
Mac Diioefl.
I saw them, all,
I could have laughed out loud
To see them at their capers;
That serious, solenimfooted, weiylity
crowd J
Of angels, or say resurrected drapers:
Mach with a, thin flame swinging round his
head.
With lilting wings and eyes of holy dread
And curving ears strained for the great
foot-fall,
And 'not a thought of sin.. ..
I don't know how I kept the laughter in.
For I was there,
Unknown, unguessed at, snug
In a rose-tree's branchy spurt,
With two weeks' tvliisker blackening lua
to lug, J
With tattered breeks and only-half a shirt
Swollen • fit to burst with laughter at the
sight
Of those dull angels drooping left and
right
Along the towering throne, each in a scare
To hear Ills foot advance
Huge from the cloud behind, all in a
trance.
And suddenly,
As silent as a ghost,
I jumped out from the bush,
Went scooting through the glaring, nerve-
less host .
All petrified, all gaping in a hush:
Game to the throne and, nimble as a rat.
Hopped up it, squatted close, and there I
sat,
Squirming with, laughter till I had to cm,
To see Him standing there
Frozen with all His angels in a stare!
He raised His hand.
His hand! 'Tints like a sky!
Gripped me in half a finger,
Flipped me round and sent me spinning
high
Through the hot planets: faith, I didn't
linger
To scratch myself, and then adown I sped
Scraping old moons and twisting heels and
head
A chuckle in the void till.. . .here I stand
As naked as a brick,
I’ll sing the Peeler and the Goat in half a
tick.
Nora Criona.
I have looked him round and looked him
through, • '
Know everything that he will do
In such a case, and such a case.
And when a frown comes on his face
I dream of it, and when a smile
I trace its sources in a while.
He cannot do a thing but I
Peep and find the reason why,
Because I love him, and I seek,
Every evening in the week,
To peep behind his frowning eye
With little query, little pry.
A nd make him, if a woman can,
Happier than any man.
Yesterday he gripped her tight,.
And cut her throat —and serve her right!
From “The Hill of Vision,” by James
Stephens (Maunsel, Dublin).

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Ricardo”: The estimated cost of electrifying the
Melbourne railways is £2,750,000, spread over seven
years; and engineer Merz and his associates receive
a tremendous 5 per cent, of it. First returns
should come in in 1915, and show £20,000 saved,
on present-traffic basis. With an increase of traffic
the saving just leaps along. In 1908 Melbourne
railways carried 39 millions of passengers; in 1912,
90 millions. Still, it is boom time, and there may
be a slump. At present Flinders-street station is
alleged to have more passenger traffic than any other
in the world. Once or twice a day, during busiest
hours, the trains cannot find seating accommodation
for more than 70 per cent, of the passengers. There
is a limit to the number of trains that can be run
safely, or even with moderate safety. The demand,
under the present system,- cannot be met without the
lines being triplicated or even quadruplicated. Elec-
trification provides higher speed, more capacity,
greater punctuality, reduction of dirt, noise, smell
and smoke, improved lighting, elimination of heavy
running, and reduction of wear and tear during times
of slack traffic. And all this it is proposed to accom-
plish by means of cheap brown coal, which is of no
use for steam purposes under present conditions, and
very little use for anything else.
“Silas Snell”: I think the Tatt.’s sweep-winner
lie is the most popular and persistent perjury in
Australia. Every second man you meet at this season
of the year is out to advertise Tatt.’s, with a cir-
cumstantial yarn about a bloke who has just landed
£6OOO in a sweep. I had to run an alleged winner
to earth the other day. I found him officiating as
guardian of the bone-heap at. a city butchery. Our
elevating conversation ran this way: “Is this story
about the Tattersall’s sweep true?” “Well, in a
sorter way it is.” “And did you go and take your
father out of the Benevolent Asylum, as they say ?”
“No, that ain’t true. See, I ain’t got an old man.”
“Was it your uncle, then?” “No; ain’t got no uncle
neither that 1 know of.” “Perhaps it was your
mother?” “No; I never took no one out. o’ the
asylum.” “Oh! And you are keeping on at your job
here after making such a haul?” “1 ain’t made any
haul!” “But you won the sweep?” “No, I didn’t.
I had two tickets, though, and one of ’em was only
two off a prize.” And that’s all there was to it.
“The Ratcatcher's Daughter”: The hardest-wood
scuffle broke out again the other day, this time on
the floor of Australia’s House of Reps. The question
was whether karri or jarrah was more suitable for
sleepers in the construction of the Kalgoorlie-Port
Augusta railway. The Australian Government de-
cided in favor of karri, and the ensuing disturbance
was described bv King O'Malley as “the last stand
of monopoly’s defenders in the' House” —said mono-
poly being the Jarrah Co. of Westralia. King-0
quoted the official report of Millar’s Karri and Jarrah
Forests, Ltd., London, as follows: “This famous
timber (karri) is not only tne best for street paving,
but it.leaves its competitors—stone, asphalt and pine
—far behind.” Petsche, formerly city engineer of
Paris, in a publication of his, stated that karri was
the third hardest wood in the world. He placed the
iron-wood of Borneo first; liem, second ; karri, third ;
teak, fourth; and jarrah, fifth. The Sovereign also
quoted Nisbet Blair, a London engineer, as stating
that in the Strand (London), yellow deal had worn
down 4Jin. in 12 months. This was replaced by
jarrah, which wore 2Jin. in three years, or Jin. per
annum, and. later, by karri, which wore at the rate
of l-sth inch per annum. The King may be wrong :
but for once in a way he has got a heap of solid
evidence at the back of his throne.
“Prickly Pear”: Prime Minister Fisher has a
chance of taking a .fall out of prickly pear. Most
people know a variety of things that could be made
from the curse which has spread over 20 or 30 mil-
lions of acres in Queensland, but the majority of
these things could only be evolved, extracted or
otherwise produced at a loss. Most likely alcohol
could be derived at a profit. The world has just now
a tremendous appetite for alcohol, not merely to make
its head go round, but it turns it’s wheels as well. The
quantity available from ordinary sources is not likely
to meet the growing demand, and the possibilities of
prickly pear in this direction should be thoroughly
tested. A bonus offered bv the Federal Government
for the first quarter of a million gallons, to be fol-
lowed up with a duty which would be really pro-
tective, should start the new industry on a satis-
factory basis. Prickly pear grubbing concessions
could be granted to manufacturers on condition that
the vegetable was removed root and all, and thus em-
ployment might be found for hundreds of men at an
occupation which was directly preparing the way for
closer settlement and primary industry, while adding
to the manufacturing wealth of the country.
“Vigilans”: In one business block in Melbourne
there are four “dummy” auctioneers. They all sell
the same class of shoddy and have the same “gee-
ers.” During a holiday week this class of rascal does
roaring business. These auction rooms are the
spieler's haven of rest. The runners-up or “gee-ers”
are all well-known confidence and three-card men.
One shop employs five, all of whom may be seen
on the racecourse with their implements of trade
whenever fools and horses are gathered together. In
the bogus auction rooms they act the parts of jewel-
lers and dealers, and keep a look out for mugs who
are fleeced for the auctioneer, and later on skinned
for the crook’s own benefit, if their hides are worth
the trouble. The confiding bushfellow is their most
frequent Victim. The Argus recently published an
article dealing with this variety of hawk, and a tide
of letters poured in from people who had suffered.
A week or so back, the writer spent half an hour in
a den where a wall notice says impudently, “Loafers
Please Loaf Outside.” While the seller of shoddy
was spinning his usual yarn some rash listener in-
terrupted with, “Have you any ‘gee-ers’ here?” “Go
and have another beer!” yelled Brum angrily. But,
before he could act on the advice, three crooks
grabbed him by the scruff of the neck and propelled
him swiftly to the beerless gutter. The Bourke-
street police are very tired these times, and the
swindling fraternity congregate round these haunts
unmolested. In fact, Bourke-street is 'pretty well
blocked with birds of prey. I understand the asinine
Law has not the right clause, or something, where-
with to squelch them. So they flourish like the
national debt, and O’Callaghan’s exhausted serfs drift
harmlessly by, day-dreaming about that totally inade-
quate 6d.. the Government lately added to their wages.
WASTE MATERIAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The milking machine will eventually be as in-
fluential in the dairying industry as the cream separa-
tor, and it will not be possible for any dairy farmer
to carry on with hand-milking.
—Victorian expert's opinion.
The farmer stands his cows in rows
Along the milking sheds,
Applies the nozzle ami the hose
To browns and whites and reds.
Each can is in its place below
The unexpectant cow.
He tweaks the lever round, just so,
And turns the tap, and lets her go,
And scores of streams begin to flow.
Observant stranger, that is how the work of milking's
managed now!
But is the dairy farmer glad?
His calculating eye
Is fixed on space, his brow is sad,
For in their beds they lie,
llis kiddies eight. The clock strikes six;
A sigh his breast expands.
He’s in a most perplexing fix.
You see, they are no use ; his chicks
Are eating much, and earning nix.
Those children, as the matter stands', are simply
thrown upon his hands!
VICTORIA.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Retrospect.
Have you ever come to Sydney Town, your
heart brimful of glee.
In the Springtime of the Year.
With its heat and dust and glamor. Oh . its
very dear to me,
Inexpressibly dear!
Have you ever risked your life
’Mid the traffic’s din and strife,
IHlled with fear?
Have you ever seen the Harbor when the
sun is going down
On a Summer’s evening bright.
When the boats are sailing homewards, out-
stretched wings of white and brown,
Bathed in light?
Have you heard the seagull s call,
From the white stone garden wall
Seen their flight?
Have you ever gone a-surfing on a sultry
Summer day
With a dainty little maid?
In a neat Canadian costume, waging war
against the spray,
Unfraid?
Luxuriating in the brine
With the glass at 99
In the shade?
Have you seen the ships departing from a
corner of the Quay
With a smile to hide the tears?
Have you felt that heartsick longing for
your Own Countree
After half a score of years?
Through the churning of the screw,
’Mid the gleam of red and blue,
Heard the cheers?
Oh! hasten down to Sydney Town, you have
been long away !
Come to greet the glad New Year!
With its merry Christmas shopping, it is
always bright end gay
And immeasurably dear!
You will always find a friend
With a welcome to extend
Waiting here!
N.S.W.
ISOBEL RENTOUL.
The Kiss of Spring.
My lover kissed me on tlie lips,
A hearty kiss and free,
And dancing to my finger tips
The warm blood leaped in me.
We rode together down the way
Through paths of shade and light,
And all the dimples of the day
With bird and bloom were bright.
My lover laughed in merry sport.
Oh, it was good to ride
Where blossoms held their noonday court
And earth and sky were wide.
And it was good to feel and know
How strong he was and fair,
Who half one golden hour ago
Had kissed me unaware.
The Spring was at its highest flood,
The world was all a song,
It was the music of my blood
That beat so sweet and strong.
The wild bird carolled in the tree,
It carols gaily still,
And Summer has a kiss for me
Since young Spring worked his will.
Still at the changing of the year
The warm, soft airs aflow
Kiss me, like love, as in the dear
Dead Spring of long ago.
N.S.W.
PEARL SMITH.
Eugenics Up to Date.
No one should be allowed to marry who cannot
show a clean bill of health.
—Latest eugenic dictum.
I’d waited lons for Mary, but her foolish
doting mother
For long refused my offer, and advised me
seek another.
And, when the mother yielded, the Panjan-
drum said the pharynx
Of Mary was defective, and she’d trouble in
her larynx.
I then laid siege to Jessie, who was debonair
and faddy,
And when I’d won her over I’d a battle with
her daddy;
But when she sought her “stifficate” (as
only just and right is),
The College would not issue, for she’d chronic
tonsilitis.
A peach (I thought) without a speck was
pretty little Polly;
Her smile was all enchantment and her
laugh was always jolly;
But her they sternly did taboo—because, be-
neath her roses,
A hint they did discover of the fell tubercu-
losis.
I wooed the haughty Winifred until she did
surrender.
And showed beneath her scornful guise there
beat a bosom tender:
But then the College did decree: “No man
shall ever marry her,
We find when she was twenty-three she was
a typhoid carrier.”
And when, at last, I’d run —I think —
through quite a baker’s dozen,
My choice, at length, despairing fell on Kate,
my gentle cousin;
But having found her free of plague and
gout and meningitis,
They put the damsel out of court for old ap-
pendicitis.
Yet finally I found a maid who calm and
smiling passed them —
Her build was firm; her health superb—and
so the College classed them;
But, when I went to get my girl and proudly
take her home again,
I found Myself disqualified, and started off
to roam again.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

HALLEY Motor Lorries
ARE EASY TO CONTROL.
The “Halley” is cheaper to run per mile than liorse-teams. And
when it is not working it costs no money, and needs the slightest amount
of looking after. And, being from three to four times as fast, it brings
the markets nearer. The roughest roads and most hilly country offer
no impediment to the Halley’s progress. It is constructed by a specialist
in industrial motors, to withstand the hardest conditions and severest
usage. ■
The “Halley” is simple in construction. Anyone soon learns the
way to handle this lorry. The majority of “Halleys” in use are being
controlled by former drivers of horse teams. This fact makes it parti-
cularly valuable to dwellers remote from mechanical assistance.
The Special Award and Gold and Silver Medals of the Eoyal Auto-
mobile Club, for Simplicity, Accessibility and Freedom from Break-
down, have been awarded the “Halley.”
ANY TYPE OF BODY FITTED.
To Carry from 15cwt. to 6 Tons.
DALGETY <& CO. Ltd.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” »*
the proper superscription for “copy.’’ Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
Tfie Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence'’ col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political. Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clivpings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction—i.e., any pic-
ture which has to be re-drawn — : will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 14, M>l2.
HOW OF ARMAGEDDON?
Last week Dr. Long, Bishop of Bathurst
(N.S.W.), voiced the sentiments of the aver-
age casual Australian, in regard to the war
in the Balkans. “I hope,” he said jauntily,
“that the other nations of Europe will be
good enough sports to stand outside and let
them have it out” —an aspiration which was
loudly applauded. One would think from
the cheery, impersonal tone of this pro-
nouncement that Australians, along with
Britishers in general, stood in no peril from
the trouble that is raging on the other side of
the earth. To persons like the Bishop, it
appears in the light of a sort of test match,
or row between alien schoolboys. In point
of fact, as an outcome of this disturbance,
Australia may quite soon be experiencing
those horrors which, hitherto, it has only
read about. The unspeakable person in the
scarlet fez is evidently licked to a standstill.
Hilmi Pasha, Turkish Ambassador at
Vienna, declared, as far back as Thursday
last, that the position of his country was
“hopeless.” At the time of writing. Con-
stantinople—that mighty and most desirable
citadel, whose sphere of influence extends
from the Adriatic to the Persian Gulf, and
from the Danube to the Eastern Mediter-
ranean —appears to be on the verge of capitu-
lating to the enemy. In any event, before
these lines are printed, the Powers may have
landed troops there to preserve order, a pro-
visional military Government may have been
established, and the Turk may be a cipher
in the city that has been the capital of his
Empire for four and a half centuries. What
if, in his humiliation and despair he crossed
over the Bosphorus, bag and baggage, and
abandoned Europe? He could take no more
dreadful revenge on the Powers which have
stood off and watched his agonies in more or
less approving silence. A general conflagra-
tion, a species of European Armageddon,
would be inevitable. It is questionable
whether it isn’t inevitable anyhow. Each
of the great nations is yearning for Constan-
tinople, and none can afford to let the other
have it. The conflict of interests is appar-
ent to anyone who glances at a European
railway map. Germany is vitally interested
in strengthening its position in Asia MinTir,
where it already has concessions to build
the Euphrates Valley railway. Nothing on
earth could more advantage it in this respect
than the possession of the great city on the
Bosphorus. Constantinople would give Rus-
sia an exit to the Mediterranean, a boon it
has been expressly denied for generations.
Russia is in a position to make a powerful
bid for this priceless asset. There is the
French alliance, and there is the fact that
the Serbs and Bulgarians have traditional
and historical racial and religious sympathies
with the Bear Empire. Bulgaria badly
wants an outlet to the ocean on the iEgean
and since neither the Greeks, nor Roumania
(the reputed possessor, by the way, of one of
the most formidable armies of its size in
Europe), nor indeed any of the Balkan na-
tions, would endure the handing over of the
prize to Bulgaria, the latter race of useful
fighters might throw in its lot with Russia
in return for a nnrt. Servia, according to a
cable message which appeared on Monday
last, is clamoring for a port on the Adriatic.
But, apart from the fact that Italy requires
the whole of the Albanian coast. Austria,
one-third of whose people are dissatisfied
Slavs, must risk all rather than submit to
a step which would inevitably lead up to
the establishment of the racial magnet of a
Pan-Slav Empire on its borders. Britain,
so long as it aspires to occupy Egypt, cannot
allow any naval Power to hold Constanti-
nople ; and without a doubt, to avert such a
menace to its interests, it would fight to the
death.
* * * *
A recent message that the Belgian army is
mobilising is full of significance. It means
that the cockpit of Europe sees trouble
ahead. And whatever great Powers fly at
each other’s throats, Britain must immedi-
ately be involved. Assume that either
Russia on the one hand, or Germany on
the other, seizes the splendid booty which
the valor and genius of the Balkans people
has laid at the world’s feet, in a manner of
speaking. War would follow automatically
between Russia and its ally France, on one
side, and Germany on the other, Germany
being aided possibly by Roumania. Britain,
France and Russia are bound by treaty to
preserve the inviolability of Belgium. But
Germany’s road to France and to the
mouth of the Scheldt lies through Belgium.
Treaties apart, it would be as much as
Britain’s national existence was worth to
suffer Germany to gain a footing anywhere
adjacent to the mouth of the Rhine—that is
to say, in a position from which it would
be able to menace, perhaps to command, the
North Sea. It is well known that last July,
at the time of the war scare. Britain was
fully prepared to land its expeditionary force
on the North Coast of France, with the
object of preventing any attack on
Belgium. That landing would occur
inevitably, within a very few days
of the beginning of hostilities between
those Powers which are most likely
to clash in the rush for the Turkish spoil.
In that case, the British Empire would be
at war. not with a few unclothed niggers,
rot with a tiny nation of farmers, but with
the carefully-trained and organised armies
of a combination of huge fighting nations.
* * * *
A question which should be approached in
this country in a spirit of deadly serious-
ness is: Where and how, in that event,
would Australia stand? From the moment
that friendly relations ceased, the Com-
monwealth would be as open to attack as the
county of Kent. An elementary rule of war-
fare lays it down that a policy of aggression
is the best means of defence. How of our
destroyers? Are they prepared, at short
notice, to go out and intercept and seize
foreign merchantmen, to repel the attacks
of foreign war vessels, and in general to
perform the duties which they were built
for? Have cruising districts been mapped out?
Are there coaling stations and oil depots
along the coast, with adequate supplies? And
are our vessels equipped, as regards ammuni-
tion, stores, and so on, for a prolonged
period of trouble? And then there is the
vital problem of our land forces. That we
have a Military Board the whole world
knows. Evidence of its activity is continu-
ally forthcoming, in the form of new designs
in puttees, and fresh ideas in feathers and
gold lace and the like. But as to whether
there exists anywhere a busy, far-seeing and
capable General Staff —the sort of body
whose existence made Japan what it is, and
whose absence has just made Turkey what
it is—no evidence is forthcoming. Sup-
posing it were necessary suddenly to call
out 20,000, or even 10,000, men, for the
purposes of local defence. Has anyone in
authority fixed upon the places at, which
they would be enrolled ? Is there, in the
whole of the Commonwealth, the cadre , as
it is called, or skeleton, of any single regi-
ment? That is to say, are there men who
know where to put their hands, within a
week or a month or - two months, on all the
boots, uniforms, rifles, socks, ammunition,
scarves, cooking utensils, bedding, hats,
water bottles and similar oddments essen-
tial to a regiment that is embarking on
active service? Have arrangements been
made lor the feeding of large bodies of
men, and for the care of the sick and
wounded? Could the authorities ship 5000
troops, within a brief space of time, from
Melbourne to (say) Port Darwin? Or
2000? Or 1000? In fact, have any trans-
port plans whatever been worked out?
It may be that Australia stands on velvet
in the matter of the provision that has been
made by its military heads against hostile
invasion. On the other hand, things may
be all the other way. If they are, there is
nothing surer than that a ghastly experi-
ence awaits it, if not at once, then, inevita-
bly, a little later on. Turkey’s present plight
affords a hideous example of the criminal
folly of not worrying. One of the reasons
why the Ottoman was annihilated at Kirk-
Ivilisse would seem to be that, before en-
gaging his ruthless foe, he had not fed for
three days, no one having troubled to make
proper commissariat arrangements. Wounded
Turks have been dying like flies for weeks
because no provision was made by their
Government to give them medical help. The
clothes and boots of the Turk are in a par-
lous state, for the reason that no one has
taken the trouble to look to these things.
In short, he who for generations bore the
name of the world’s greatest fighting
man owes the loss of his European Empire
and the trailing of his proud reputation in
the mud of defeat, mainly to villainous care-
lessness and lack of foresight on the part of
those whose job it was to organise his army
as a serious fighting machine. There may
be no such carelessness in the Common-
wealth. But if there is, then those respon-
sible for it are the wickedest of traitors.
This country is one of the most glittering
prizes of any white men’s war. Under ex-
isting conditions, it might be called on to
defend its national life within a week or
less. It can do so, provided its forces, small
as they are, are properly organised and
equipped. Are they? It is a question De-
fence Minister Pearce might have to
answer with fearful suddenness.
THE AGE OF REASON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Victorian State Executive recently ordered
the flogging of a sex-maniac, who is under sentence
of imprisonment for life.
Whene’er I read some savage tale
Of punishment devised
By tyrants in an olden day,
When serfs were victimised,
I reverently tell myself
“Thank God, we're civilised !”
Thank God, those idols, grimmer far
Than gods of wood or stone,
Unthinking Hate and brute Revenge,
With all the seeds they’ve sown,
Are cast to earth, and Reason sits
With Mercy on the throne.
Calm Reason sits upon the throne
And fashions righteous laws,
And in our blessed Age of Light
It ever bids us pause
And, ere we plan the remedy,
Unearth the Primal Cause.
It seeks not, in a brutish rage,
To flog the witless fool;
The rack, the pillory are gone,
The witches’ ducking stool ;
And Reason builds no gallows for
Heredity’s poor tool.
“Reform lies not in punishment!”
So saith the modern sage.
“No remedy for evil holds
Blind Hate or savage Rage.
The whipping-post, tllfi darkened cell
Are of a darker age.”
So Reason saith ; so Mercy saith ;
And, having said, withdraw.
(O brothers, in this Noble Age
That there should be a flaw!)
And to the vacant throne there steps
The thing men call the Law.
The Law devised by kings long-dead
And superstitious priests,
Whose code considered but revenge,
With bloody rites and feasts —
The ancient Law, bequeathed by men
Scarce risen from the beasts.
But e’en before such kings and priests
Infested our poor earth,
Long ages ere some bleeding wretch
Excited their loud mirth.
A thing, half man, with crooked brain,
It chanced, was given birth.
And lo! this thing begat him sons,
And their sons sons again,
And on and on. till sturdier .
And cleaner grew tfie strain,
Till in the breed, for many an age,
The taint had dormant lain.
For countless ages it. mayhap,
The fatal taint had missed.
Till, in our day, a babe was born
With some strange mental twist,
A thing for all men’s, sympathy—
A foredoomed atavist.
And that he sinned against our code
And harmed a fellow-man
(Lord knows what Nature is about
To work on such a plan !)
Lo. be is seized on by the Law
And placed beneath the ban !
And what has Reason now to say,
Chief of our modern gods?
And Mercy? “Keep the man apart,
But harm not such noor clods?”
“Nay,” saith the Law, “we’ll truss him
up
And scourge his back with reds !’
And so they take the last poor son
Of all that tainted host.
And try to exorcise the taint
There at the wliinping-post.
This is the Age of Reason, friends !
It is our proudest boast.
And what of those great men on hieii
Who said this thing should be?
Wliat of the Law’s high officers
Who voiced the brute decree?
Shall such ones not become the mark
For scornful obloquy?
Nav, gentle brothers, blame them not-
Blame is the whip of fools —
For here again we mark in them
Heredity’s poor tools.
The eld rings with their sires’ demand,
Calling for ducking stools.
And so. when all is said and done,
We end whero we began.
We must leave Nature to proceed
With her age-honored plan.
E’en I who speak may be t'>e son
Of some strange-fashioned man.
Because he had a twisted form
A man of old was slain :
They flog him in our Age of Light
For his poor twisted brain;
And, ’spite my words, the chances are
They’ll do the same again.
Still, when I read some savage tale
Of punishment devised
By tyrants of an olden day,
When serfs were victimised.
I feel it in my heart to say : —■
“Thank Gon, we're civilised !”
VICTORIA.
DEN.
THE PARTY COMBINE IN RESTRAINT
OF DOING THINGS.
The secretary of the Watchmakers and
Jewellers’ Employees’ Union in Sydney re-
cently complained that in his city one of the
big manufacturers had discharged 30 hands.
Pressed for an explanation, the vice-presi-
dent of the Manufacturers’ Association said
that while his own firm was still reasonably
busy, the importations of foreign goods had
increased. What is true of this one trade is
true of many. Wage increases without in-
creased Protection can only result in driving
work to the countries where work is done
more cheaply, thus throwing the men locally
engaged in that industry into the ranks of
unskilled labor, or forcing them to join the
army of post-leaners and odd-jobbers.
* * * *
Australia always needs a high tariff, but
its present need is much more urgent than
usual. The signs of coming slump are as
plainly marked as the face of a White Hope
after meeting the powers of darkness. Two
big southern factories have already closed,
and more are likely to follow. Importations
are enormous, and the existing Protection is
about as effective as a string of Chinese
crackers would be against a Bulgarian
bayonet charge. Yet nothing is done, and
the tariff-sinkers of both political parties
seem to regard the situation with compla-
cency. Loss of work for Australian hands
means lack of bread for Australian mouths;
yet the men whose first business it should
be to set the crooked matter straight act as
if the thing had less interest for them than
the defeat of the favorite for the Melbourne
Cup, or the question whether a Minister’s
pals should have first claim to employment
on public works. And all this apathy in
face of a gigantic public danger exists in
spite of the fact that the Federal Parliament
has an unquestioned majority in favor of
out-and-out Protection. If the free vote of
the members of the Australian Parliament
could be taken on the question to-morrow,
the adequate Protection for Australian in-
dustries which is needed might become Aus-
tralian law before a week was over, the
growing evil might be checked, and Austra-
lian workers might have secured to them the
right to work and live which is fast slipping
from them. A majority of the people of
Australia are in favor of such Protection,
a majority of their representatives in Par-
liament are in favor of it, the exigencies of
the hour loudly demand it, and yet matters
drift from bad to worse, and nothing at all
is done. Why? Because the curse of Party
Government has us in its thrall, and the
free voice of the people and their represen-
tatives is smothered under the weight of an
absurd and obsolete system. Fxsiiek, the
Protectionist, must yield in matters of
highest principle and urgent reform to
Hughes, the Freetrader, so that he may
have the latter’s support against Deakin,
the Protectionist, who is leagued with Cook,
the Freetrader. Australia may stand still,
or go to the dogs or the devil in
order that the game may be played in the
orthodox way, and a Party, which means
nothing either good or useful, held together
to further defeat the popular will.
■K * * *
Nero, fiddling amid the Roman blaze, had
at least the excuse that he was an artist
and producing suitable music to accompany
the crackling of the flames; but Fisher,
hopeless and helpless in the midst of the
ruin his fiscal sinking is bringing about, has
no such excuse to offer. Party politics, as
played by the leading professionals in the
game, is a fairly remunerative branch of
sport which can provide gentle exercise and
simple luxuries for a few; but the Protec-
tion which the present crisis calls for, but
which party politics prevents, means work
for thousands of Australians, which means
food and shelter and clothing for tens of
thousands of Australians. It will be a dis-
astrous thing for the Labor party if the
people of the Commonwealth should be
driven to take note of the fact that Labor
may be as much the foe of labor as anti-
Labor, or Toryism, or any old thing which
wears the brand of popular contempt and
derision.
Party Government is no fixed and neces-
sary feature of our system of politics. There
is a perfectly simple means of escape from
the tangle which is holding back Australian
effort and preventing reform. Party as it
exists is as much a combine in restraint of
progressive legislation as any Beef or Oil
Trust can be a combine in restraint of trade.
The introduction of a sane system of elec-
tive Ministries would at a single sweep wipe
out the present party lines for ever. Poli-
ticians would still, of course, continue to
form into parties with regard to certain
measures and views, but such combinations
would readjust themselves with every new
proposal brought before Parliament, and no
member would be called upon to vote against
the thing he believed in. To-day’s allies
would be to-morrow’s opponents, and legis-
lation would be passed, not by weight of
party influence., but by force of actual
thought and opinion. Were such a system
in existence in Australia our present tariff
wall would be built up to an effective height
with ordinary business-like promptitude.
But Party Government goes on its lumber-
ing way. irresponsible in its methods as the
rule of Sultan or Czar, doing in six sessions
what should be done in six weeks, and leav-
ing a thousand things undone, because to
attempt them would mean the breaking up
of the party itself. And still the people en-
dure it, and look up hopefully to men of
tied hands and stultified aspirations who
still boast that they are leaders in the fight
for progress! Sometimes luck helps the
nation forward a few steps, sometimes ill-
luck hurls it back, sometimes some of the
people starve, and often most of them suffer
unnecessary hardships. But always the Fat
Man laughs; for Party Government is his
best friend, and he knows it.

PLAIN ENGLISH.
Fiscalism and the U.S. Presidential
Election.
For the first time in 20 years a democrat
is President of the United States, and the
Australian Freetrade press is elated. Syd-
ney Telegraph supplies a fair type of the
general volume of comment: —
While we believe that the selection of the Demo-
crat is by far the best the United States could make
for itself, it is beyond doubt that it means more to
the world outside than the choice of either of the
other candidates would have meant, owing to the
Democrats being pledged to fiscal reform in the
shape of a tariff for revenue only.
This recalls 1892, when Grover Cleveland
had been elected President on the Democra-
tic ticket. He was suspected of Freetrade
leanings, and with a good deal of reason,
having been the author of a spirited attack
on the system whereunder about two-thirds
of the revenue of his uative land was de-
rived from import duties. Australian Free-
traders whooped joyously, under the de-
lusion that in the land of the _ screaming
eagle they had found a champion and a
priceless advt. for their creed. Infuriated
with this idea, George Reid and Edward
Pulsford called a public meeting for the
purpose of expressing gratification at the
honor done to their supposed fiscal ally. It
was a tremendous success. Reid, who had
hitherto opposed Federation “because it
meant Protection,” and who as a result had
narrowly missed losing his seat in East
Sydney, seized on the triumph of Cleve-
land as an excuse to perform an adroit
somersault. He announced that the spec-
tacle of a great country like America throw-
ing off the incubus Protection had heart-
ened him. What America had done Aus-
tralia could do. He no longer felt afraid
of Federation. The thought of one sober
man keeping house with five drunkards had
ceased to appal him. (Deafening ap-
plause.) In collaboration witli Pulsford
he sent a lengthy cable to Cleveland con-
gratulating him on his victory “in the name
of Freetrade.” Cleveland did not so much
as acknowledge the congratulations. He
did not dare. Neither then, nor now, nor
during any of the years intervening, lias it
been possible for a man to live politically
at the top of the tree in the U.S. with the
stigma of Freetrader on him. America
starkly refrained from throwing off the in-
cubus Protection. Years passed, and Reid
himself—the man who had only agreed to
Federation because he meant to convert all
Australia to Freetrade —began to _ glimpse
the light of the Inevitable. Coalescing with
Protectionists, he sank the fiscal issue.
These ancient facts are recalled lest pre-
sent-day Freetraders, if such there are,
should be deluded into acts of foolishness.
America is not going to adopt a tariff for
revenue only, any more than Germany pro-
poses to disarm or Britain to scrap its navy.
For months past Wilson has been repudi-
ating his alleged revenue-tariff predilections.
Here is what he said at Hartford, Connec-
ticut (25/9/T2) :
Do you suppose half the population of the United
States is going to destroy the very foundations of
our economic life by. running amok among* tariff
schedules ?
That is not our programme, and anybody who
says it is simply does not understand the situation
in the United States at all.
There should be no fear of the FREETRADE
BUGABOO, because the Democrats do not propose
to revise the tariff in any other way than will con-
serve the real business interests of the country.
We propose to revise those schedules which give
unfair private advantage to certain interests without
affecting business which is sound and legitimate, and
which we all wish to see promoted.
Neither I nor the Democratic party stand for Free-
trade.
The Freetrader who would claim the pos-
sessor of those sentiments as an ally is
almost too much of the cheerful optimist 10
be at large.

Where the Chronic Jester is Out
of Place.
Melbourne Age remarked in its report of
some recent proceedings in the Australian
Parliament
For upwards of an hour there was debate, most
of it of a bantering kind, on the sum of £3OO set
aside for members’ “writing paper and envelopes.”
It could veraciously make the same com-
ment on about 50 per cent, of the “business”
that is undertaken in this and the other
Legislatures of Australia. Our multitudin-
ous Parliaments labor under the heavy han-
dicap of a glut of wags, real and bogus, in-
tentional and otherwise. The national
pastime of leg-pulling finds its fullest and
freest opportunity in these resorts. There
are legislators in every House of the
Commonwealth whose whole reputations
stand broad-based on a knack of insulting
interjection or repartee, and a capacity for
making their duller associates appear idiotic
and confused. Bloggs, M.P., is returned
regularly for the great and influential con-
stituency of Blight. His party papers and
organisations take him seriously, at any rate
at election times. Yet it is impossible for
his warmest admirers to point to any stage
of his long and strenuous career whereat
he evolved one new and useful idea and
brought it to fruition; or to indicate five
lines of criticism which were in any way
helpful. Long, senseless and irrelevant
questions, framed palpably to raise a pass-
ing laugh; crude personalities of the sort
that the festive bagman frafficks in when in
his cups; ponderous allusions, archly de-
livered, to the enemy’s alleged moral and
other lapses—with these and similar im-
becilities the time which the country pays
for in hard-borrowed cash is frittered away.
The electors have got so habituated to the
practice of regarding their Parliaments as
freakish variety shows, where everything,
from general assault and battery to the
spectacle of an ex-Minister rolling on the
floor amid a cluster of policemen, appears
more or less appropriate, that they can see
nothing reprehensible in the antics of the
legislative humorist. And yet, more especi-
ally in the National House, where there is
not a moment to spare for frivolling, this
type of merry-maker is almost a public
curse. His efforts at wit would not be en-
dured for two minutes in the Board room
of an ordinary business concern. Why they
should be allowed to hamper the business
of a Commonwealth is one of the Party
Government system’s eternal mysteries.
The Law’s Delays.
A Melbourne paper wrote the other day of
the ‘"monstrous fees” and the ‘‘artificial
methods of piling up costs” by which the
“bleeding of litigants” was carried out in
the United Kingdom. It concluded:
In the English courts the “cost of production,
represented by the expense of securing finality m any
dispute of importance, is certainly high enough to
warn thousands of the public that it is better to
bear with tiieir grievances in silence than to run
the risk of being ruined by an attempt to reach the
founts of justice.
But why mention England when the posi-
tion of Australia is far worse V Despite the
smug assumption of probity which is. the
badge of the whole legal tribe, barrister
and attorney alike, a big section of the pub-
lic is agreed, as the outcome of bitter ex-
perience, that the majority of our men of
law are incomparably more predatory than
the members of those professions which lay
no particular claim to saintliness —doctors,
architects and surveyors, for example. The
profitable operation of bleeding the moneyed
litigant may be witnessed almost any _ day
during term time. It is not done, figuratively
speaking, with a scalpel or other fine wea-
pon, but with the axe. When both parties to a
suit sit behind satisfactory banking accounts,
the Law is, indeed, seen in all its pomp and
majesty. It becomes absolutely imperial in
its disregard' of sordid expense. Demurrers,
objections, interlocutory applications, appli-
cations for postponement, and for the taking
of evidence on commission in any old
region from Greenland to Peru are
hurled round by the dozen. Point after fine
legal point is taken on either side and argued
with splendid assiduity. Nothing of real
value accrues from these operations in 999
cases out of a thousand. But they afford
both Bench and Bar a certain amount of
mild intellectual exercise, and they indicate
energy and keen enthusiasm on the part of
counsel. And they keep the profitable game
of litigation going to the satisfaction of all
hands excepting those who are paying for it.
As a result of the advice on evidence —
which is not tendered till an exact under-
standing has been arrived at in regard to
just how much evidence the litigant’s pocket,
is capable of enduring—the precincts of the
court swarm with nervous, shifty-eyed wit-
nesses at so much per head for expenses,
alcohol and so on. Within the Temple of
Justice the merry jest, the diverting anec-
dote and the futile and irrelevant personal
reminiscence are bandied to and fro. Occa-
sionally there is a burst of bogus fury from
one side or the other, but for the most part
all is frank harmony and profit-sharing
among the legal brethren. The responsibility
for this scandalous state of affairs rests
primarily on the Judge who puts up with it.
If every Judge made a point of rigidly bar-
ring jokes, stories grave and gay, and puns;
if he closed down on every scrap of obviously
useless evidence, and sternly rebuked the
professional gentleman or spieler who was
guilty of foisting it on the Court; if he
declined to hear prolonged argument on
purely academic objections and points of
law; and if he devised some means of pun-
ishing the frequent type of barrister who
takes his fee and then omits to turn up at
the hearing and earn it, the business of the
Court would be vastly expedited. Australia
is really much worse off than England as
regards the difficulty of “securing finality in
any legal dispute of importance.” It is
notorious that many jury cases which in
London would be disposed of in a day are
liable, in Melbourne or Sydney, to take any-
thing up to a week. It is largely a matter
of weak Judges. A dreadful curse is the
weak Judge.
—_.—
A City of Rags.
The suggestion of the Parliamentary select
committee which has just reported on the
increase in Sydney house rents, that canvas
dwellings should be erected at places like
Daceyville and Centennial Park to house
the surplus population, has a fine flavor
about it. No doubt a well-pitched tent is
in every way superior to a hovel in a brick
terrace; but the proposal is not made with
a view to improving the public health. It
is made because under existing conditions
the average wage-earner cannot get a house
at a price he can afford to pay. The
natural increase, immigration and the influx
of people from the depopulated rural areas
have worked, as they were expected to work,
right into the hands of the' Sydney land-
lord, who draws upon the people’s earnings
to the last penny and still can keep his
houses full. The committee recommends
cheaper suburban railways, advances by the
Government Savings Bank to artisans for
the erection of dwellings, and the throwing
open of Crown lands in the suburbs for
building purposes ; but it fails to suggest that
the first and foremost step towards a per-
manent improvement of conditions is the
.carrying out of the policy of decentralisa-
tion" to which the Government is solemnly
pledged. Were the tents to be pitched at
Twofold Bay, Port Jervis or other harbors
along the coast, and if railways were being
rushed to them with all decent speed, the
idea might be one full of cheer. It might
be taken as indicating that the McGowen
Ministry had suddenly awakened to the
fact that it had been unjust to its suppor-
ters. and was now in desperate need of re-
pentance in hessian and cinders, or other
substances suitable to a state of sincere
contrition. Unfortunately the notion of
housing the people under temporary shel-
ters is advanced merely with the idea of
making the congestion of Sydney appear a
little less serious and postponing the day of
reckoning. Babylon was built of bricks,
Athens largely of marble, Memphis of gran-
ite. It remains for a N.S.W. Labor Minis-
try to have the glory of constructing a city
of rags.
University Reform.
“Fitzwilliam,” in reply to the N.S.W.
Minister for Education :
Mr. Carmichael spreads himself (B. 7/11/’l2)
on the subject of the University Bill. The article
contains no more than the usual amount of drivel,
and, though it dodges most of the specific objections
to the Bill, is perhaps worthy of an answer.
The existing Senate has done good work. The
number of undergraduates is now three times as
great as it was 13 years ago ; and though mere size
is no gauge of efficiency, this tends to show that
the increase of the University is more than keeping
pace with that of the State. And Sydney lawyers
and doctors and engineers can at least hold their
own in any part of the world they have yet pene-
trated to. I speak subject to correction; but I
think Dr. Cole is a member of the teaching staff at
Harvard, and Elliott Smith Professor of Anatomy
at Manchester. And the Judges both of the High
and Supreme Courts represent Sydney fairly well.
However, most human institutions are susceptible
of improvement, and the majority of University men
are in favor, probably, of a wider franchise and
a shorter tenure for the Senate. Moreover, there can
be no very strong objection to direct departmental
representation, in view of the fact that the Govern-
ment supplies a large proportion of the funds. But I
find it difficult to see how the introduction of two
Parliamentary representatives could help matters
much. 1 am aware that during the discussion in
the Council some gentleman interjected that if a
man was fit for Parliament he was fit for the
Senate. To which I reply—Maybe. It is even pos-
sible that the University Senate might occasionally
be enlivened by a deftly-flung water-bottle.
I note that the Government has abandoned the
proposal for municipal representation—a clause
which probably sneaked in from one of the British
University Acts. - The omission is a good one. Ido
not know what the standard of municipal intelli-
gence may be in Britain. I do know what it is in
N.S.W., where the average alderman is incapable of
managing a Sunday-school picnic.
But the main point in Mr. Carmichael s letter is
the proposed abolition of fees. This is an eminently
sane idea; but if Mr. Carmichael thinks that the
“working-man’s son” will benefit tuppence by it, he
is mistaken, that’s all. As he points out, the pro-
vision already made for poor men by bursaries and
remission of fees is not fully availed of, but he
apparently thinks that if the same prmcple is
applied to the bread-and-butter faculties, it will
have the desired effect. The offer of a free Univer-
sity education to everybody who applies is very
like the offer of a motor-ear on the same terms—a
poor man couldn’t afford the luxury. It isn t the
cost of preparation for a profession that hurts 5
is the five or more unproductive years that are
spent in training, and the other five years or fifty-
that must be endured bv a professional man without
means or influence before he earns even a living
wage. Take the Bar. If a poor man has sufficient
pluck or luck to save up (say) .-€250 to keep him-
self during his five years’ studentship, which is
hardly an extravagant amount, he would have an
infinitely better chance of getting his money back
by spending it all in Tatt.’s. Success in Tatt. s is
luck; success at the Bar is a miracle. The other
professions are not quite so bad. in their results,
though the time for preparation is as long or longer.
A doctor can probably make a fair income from the
jump, but it takes him nearly seven years to qualifv,
counting the necessary hospital experience. It’s no
use saying that a system of evening lectures would
give the necessary chance. To qualify for a pro-
fession nowadays requires steady work both day
and night. An engineer has the same long training,
but he has the advantage when he gets through
of being sure of a job—in another country. In
this continent the Government is the biggest em-
ployer of engineers, and the wages it pays are about
25 per cent, or thereabouts of what are offered in
almost any other part of the world. And it seepis
insane, somehow, to spend good money m breeding
experts for foreign countries where the value of our
engineers is known. ... «
No, the poor man’s son who worries about a profes-
sion is a fool. I don’t mean a common fool, but a
fool of the hero sort. Professional prizes are good,
but scarce. The initial risk is a big one, and for
the average man impossible, as it involves at least five
unproductive years. And failure, the failure of an
prinon f ed mnn, is Tl<klps. Tho man who has
enough to take the chances, takes them; and the
extra £l5O or so for fees, spread over five years,
does not deter him, and their abolition wont en-
courage him, because he doesn’t want encouragement.

The Majesty of the Law and the Mercy
of Holman.
A batch of papers recently laid on the
table of N.S.W. Assembly, and issued by the
Government Printer last week, dragged
from the grave of the Past the case of Alice
Wilkins, a 12-year-old Burwood girl who
in November 19, 1911, smote another eight-
year-old child. When the case came before
the court the prosecution was represented
by a solicitor; the defence was conducted by
the father of the accused, who was too poor
to fee a Blue Pointer, M. PI. Fitzhardinge,
D.S.M., was on the Bench; and haying
decided against the side without any legal as-
sistance, he dealt out a fine of £1 and costs
£1 145.. with the alternative of 14 days
hard labor — lb days’ hard labor for a little
12-year-old girl! Probably M. H. Pitz-
hardinge, D.S.M., thought the child s
father would scrape up the fine somehow,
and let her go. But the father couldn t. He
had a family of five to support on a daily
wage of 7s. 9d.; and at the time it was a
very afflicted family. The eldest child was
an epileptic, another was in a plaster ot
Paris mould owing to some injury; and a
third, a voung baby, was ill. Owing to
financial stress, the mother had to leave
these sick children and go out working to
assist her husband to make ends meet.
Under the circumstances, the father peti-
tioned Holman to remit the savage ciltei-
native and treat the child as a first offender,
and by remitting her to his care, save her
the horror of 14 days’ hard labor. Holman
refused (and will probably sweat blood tor
it before he is through) ; so, after some
delav. the 12-year-old girl was arrested, de-
tained in the cells at Burwood and then
sent to Darlinghurst Police Station to be
taken to the Women’s Penitentiary at Long
Bay. And to Long Bay she would have
gone, but the police rebelled at having to do
such barbarous work, and, opening a sub-
scription list among themselves, collected
enough to pay fine and costs, and sent the
child home. What some member should
now do is call for a list of the penalties im-
posed by M. H. Fitzhardinge, D.S.M., for
assault, battery, wife-beating and stealing,
and see how his treatment of this child
compares with that he deals out to seasoned
sluggers and .offenders against property. And
another might move for a return of penal-
ties remitted by Attorney-General Holman,
and see how they compare with his helpless-
ness to save this little girl from gaol.

A PHIAL of vitriol strays in from the N. Ter-
ritory : ... , ,
Talk of the pride of birth, the sickening hauteur
of Royal Courts and the intolerant swank of Jeames
Yellowplush ! None of these objectionable things
is in it with the antics of some of the drab little
officials whom the Fisher Administration has re-
cently despatched to these parts. We can swallow
an “Excellency,” and a “Suite” and an imitation
Government House atmosphere—a sort of simulacrum
of a shadow as it were. But what think you mf
this? A Pine Creek woman took her seat, the other
day ' in a first-class “ladies only” compartment of
the Darwin to Pine Creek train. She had three
children with her, and one of them was a baby, and
ailing at that. Also, she had paid her fare. Along
came the wife of a high official (not the official,
mark you, but his wife —a person of no authority
or status whatever), and had the Pine Creeker and
infants bundled out, and into the only other first-
class compartment in the tram —which was, inci-
dentally, a smoker crowded with men. Nor is this
the first occurrence of the kind. A few weeks earlier
a girl was similarly requested to spend a hot day
in a carriage filled with men and smoke, or miss the
train. And, as an example of how catching this
disease is, a day or so later the wife of one of
.Toslah Thomas’s new officials inflicted herself on
the occupants of the smoker, because “she wasn’t
o-oing to travel in the same compartment as the wife
of one of her husband’s men —the idea of such people
travelling first-class!” (N.B. —“Such people” travel
first-class up here not because they enioy paying more
money, but because the second-class cars are for
many Vea e ons unendurable to white women.) ,r h"
sort of official that is needed in a sparsely settled
region like this is one who is enpugh of a man of
the world to be quite onsnobbish, ami who displays
horse-sense in his social dealings with those
whom he meets. The manners and customs of such
a one are, as a rule, automatically reflected in his
family; if they aren’t, he usually has a few de-
cisive remarks to make on the subject. It is a
thousand pities that the Fisher Government couldn’t
see its way to supply us with a few Panjandrums of
this description. For one thir.g, it would have made
for peace and harmony, than which nothing is more
essential to a small community. As it isn’t so, I
hereby move that Josiah Thomas immediately pro-
vide a State car for the use of his more specta-
cular officials. Otherwise an enraged citizen, whose
wife has been insulted and done out of that which
she has duly paid for, will be arising shortly and
rudely pushing someone of prominence through the
nearest window.
■
To the mere private citizen, accustomed to
decide between breakfast time and the
boarding of his morning train whether he
will buy laee-up boots or tan shoes, the
question of the uniform railway gauge for
Australia has already passed far be-
yond the stage of argument. But
politicians can’t and don’t do things
in the sudden way in which Mary
buys soap or Tom books seats for the play.
After years of discussion and argument, re-
presentative engineers of the States and
Commonwealth are going to meet together
and discuss and argue some more about the
right gauge. After that there will probably
be a period of rest, during which the en-
gineers’ conclusions will be allowed to ripen,
or simmer, or something, and then the thing
will probably be pigeon-holed or forgotten
until there is a change of Ministries all
round, whereupon the matter will be re-
awakened, and some other engineers will be
called together to discuss and argue some
m6re. Yet the business is urgent. Day by
day and week by week Australian young-
sters are being trained to meet a possible
foe, and the bald fact stands that the Com-
monwealth hasn’t got the machinery to take
them with speed and effectiveness to face
that enemy if he should arrive. What is
wanted at the present moment is not a con-
ference of engineers to decide what should
be done, but an emphatic statement on the
oart of all the Premiers concerned that the
thing which they all know should be done
is going to be begun next week. Even the
farcical conference which is to be held
might be tolerated if it meant getting
straight to business ; but the trouble is that
such a conference is merely a_ cog in the
great wheel of shirk, and that its sole pur-
pose is to provide excuse for further de-
lays, and reason for vexatious talk about
State rights and parish grievances. Mean-
while the other chap is sharpening his gun
and putting nails in his marching boots.

George D. Meudell, from the housetop—
Iceberg Irvine, M.H.R., born in Dundalk, Ireland
—alleged to be a most distressful country—lately
told a mothers’ meeting of the Australian Women’s
National Dorcas Society that Australians suffered
from moral anosmia, and were always looking for a
post, preferably a Government one, to lean against.
That’s a slander which is likely to cost Irvine his
seat next election. For what are the facts ? AVe
Australians are the hardest-working, most skilful,
most intelligent, best made in bone and blood, richest
and most charming people in the world. Ive
travelled 400,Q00 miles in 38 countries, and have
seen all the other peoples and tribes, even Jack
Johnson’s, in their own cities and jungles. Our' men
and women physically are the peers af every other
race, excepting, perhaps, the Norwegians and Swedes.
The prettiest women in the world come from Odessa,
Stockholm and Hobart, in that order, lhe. finest
men come from the llawkesbury River (N.S.W.*),
Christiania (Norwav), and the valley of the South
j.y-k (Scotland). And here follows the* record of
what our fathers and ourselves have done in the
newest, as ft is the oldest and the best country on
earth. The Sydney peopl# have created out of
nothing, which Robert Inqersoll once described as
most unpromising raw material, the strongest private
bank and the most successful mutual life assurance
office in the world. In 50 years they’*; made Sydney
the sixth shipping port for tannage in the greatest
Empire this civilisation bus so far seen. It is
also nearly Dm most beautiful city an earth, bar
ebec and Edinburgh. In 50 years Australia has
produced £.7.50,000,000 of minerals, and by its
gold output, from 1852 to 1872, Australia created
the British Ennrre! It was neither steam, nor Free-
trade, nor Manchester, nor Nelson, nor even, a com-
bination of them, that made Britain Great. It was
good Australian gold. Without Australian gold
to form the basis of her credit, Great Britain would
to-day be a decent little place busy catching and
salting herrings, making a little lace (a trick learnt
from the Huguenots), brewing dashed (rood beer and
stout, because of good local water, and making and
coloring burnous, handkerchiefs, and cummerbunds
for all the colored peoples living within coo-ee of
the Equator. Without our Australian gold, London
could never have become the chief moneylender,
pawnbroker, and, therefore, shipping nation of the
globe For ships were needed to carry' the lent goods
—not gold, mark you—to all the other countries
under Britain’s commercial suzerainty, by reason of
our gold. Australia has the most bank deposits, the
most savings bank depos ts, the best birthrate, the
lowest deatbiate, the finest hind laws, the biggest
wool output and the finest wheat in the world. Aus-
tralia also uroduees the best horses, cattle, meat,
butter, rabhits, copper, sugar and fruits known
to Europe’s merchants. Australia has the greatest
silver mine, the best copper mine, the richest gold
mine and the largest tin mine, as well as the
biggest coalfield, in one spot, on earth.. Our import
and export trade is the largest per head, and so is
our local shipping. Epidemic disease is the smallest,
crime statistics the lowest, milk supply and food the
purest, deaths under 12 months the fewest, and old
age pensions at 10s. a week the highest known to
