A punter contrasts the ways of the Chil-
dren of Israel with those of the Tote :■ —-
The recent improvements at Flemington show
the urgent need for that greatest improve-
ment of all—the Tote. Though the paddock
betting ring has been enlarged, on Cup Day
it was such a seething mass of pushing
backers, that Patrick Moses couldn’t hold out his
fist for the cash of eager punters without encountering
the fingers of a brother Ikey. Women are not al-
lowed to bet in Victoria: but even if they were,
none would dare venture into that mass. On the
Hill a worse pandemonium reigned. The brethren
bad been shifted from their old positions in front of
the Hill Stand to the western slope. This slope
is as steep, and on Derby and Cup Days it was as
slippery, as the road to Gehenna. Also it was as
crowded as that unquiet place is supposed to be.
O’Joshua stood it on Saturday, and got wet and
muddy, and angry; but on Tuesday he defied the
authorities and went where the spirit moved him.
Some of him returned to the old spot, and stayed
there; more of him developed an unseemly habit of
wandering all over the place, so that a man who
backed a winner with some Coiien near the scratch-
ing board might, when he went to collect the cash,
discover him in the middle of the picnic ground—or
he might not. In fact, he might not discover him
at all. Of course, all these Abrahams are regis-
tered and guaranteed, and application at the V.R.C.
office next day would ensure payment of the wager ;
but that was no good to the man who wanted to put
some of his winnings on a prad for the next race.
Also, everybody doesn’t know of this arrangement,
and if Everybody did, it might not suit him to
chase round town after a few shillings. Anyhow, a
number of people were earnestly seeking the elusive
Israelite after the Cup and other races, and many
are still seeking him. Now the Tote doesn’t take
all this space or cause all this bother. It is simply
a number of small windows in a wall, with certain
numbers overhead and telephone wires la d on. Also,
the Tote doesn’t wander about to the annoyance of
successful backers. And it always pays out all it
lakes in, less the usual 10 per cent. Moses doesn’t.
Died last week at Waverley, aged 75,
Obed West, second son of the late Obed and
grandson of Thomas West, who came to
Sydney in 1800. Obed was born in Pitt
Row —now Pitt-street—in 1807, on the spot
where Iloffnung’s Buildings now stand. In
1804 a grant of 75 acres was made to
Thomas West, and the estate, which he
named Barcom Glen, stretched from Oxford-
street to Rushcutter Bay. Thomas estab-
lished a flourmill there, and most of the
Scarce inhabitants of that nart also drew
their water supply from it—for a considera-
tion. Tom’s son, Obed 1., was the winner of
the first gold medal awarded for rifle-shoot-
ing in the colony.
A curious cable appeared in Sydney Sun
one night last week. It was to the effect
that Australian circles in London under-
stood that W. M. Hughes might be appointed
Chairman of the Inter-State Commission, at
£3OOO per annum for seven years. It looks
like the champion case of political kite-flying.
Evidently the brilliant, tricky Hughes is
looking for a comfortable rlace to land when
the time comes to get out. In some respects
it is bad news for Australia; because, though
Hughes is very often wrong, he supplies
more brains to the Federal Labor party
than any other man in it. A party with a
big leader could well do without Hughes’s
trickiness; but unfortunately the Labor
party hasn’t got a big leader.
“Bobstay”: Good old mouldy British
Board of Trade again! The immigrant
steamer Irishman has just arrived at Ade-
laide with a virulent outbreak of measles
among the children. There were 50 infected ;
six had died and several others were landed
at Adelaide in a serious condition. The
Irishman was built for a cargo and cattle
ship, but as “anything is good enough for
immigrants,” the Board of Trade made no
bones about pouching its fee and issuing a
certificate which enabled the ship to herd
human passengers aboard. Other vessels
have reached Australia with the Board of
(Trade certificate, much to the astonishment
of local authorities, but the Irishman, with
its tlirown-together accommodation ’tween
decks (even the hospital was there!) and
its load of stricken children, about takes the
cake. In fact, it takes the whole visible
flour supply.

Bloodshed and devastation raging in the
Balkans, and “The Quaker Girl” worthily
upholding the principles of the Society of
Friends at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) ! There
is an impressive sermon herein if one of
the brethren who make a trade of drawing
moral lessons could but be turned on to the
job. Who ever heard of a Bulgarian
Quaker Girl? No-one! Like every other
peace-propagandist, the Quaker is the pro-
duct of lands which have not felt the prod
of a hostile bayonet for generations. It
would go far to discount the Bulgar’s de-
light at his victory over the hated Turk did
be but realise all he loses in never hav-
ing any chance to know people like

“The Village Blacksmith.”
Blanche Browne and Bertie Wright—repre-
sentative Quakers of the best and brightest
sort. It might also depress him if he appre-
ciated the tragedy of his lot in never being
likely to encounter such charming and tal-
ented folks as Grace Palotta, Jessie Lonnen
and Ivy Schilling—typical members of a
high-class Quaker set. But would it induce
him to turn anti-militarist, even if the full
gloom of his situation were brought home
to him ? When he thinks of the number of
times he has been massacred, perhaps it
wouldn’t.

Randolph Bedford will pluck his “Unseen
Eye” from the Sydney Palace to-morrow
night, and the cavity will be filled on Satur-
day evening by a “Woman of Impulse.” As
per announcement, “the scene of this im-
pressive play is laid in London, and the
characters are types to be found in the high-
est social and diplomatic circles.” Leaders of
Australian diplomatic circles like Peter
Bowling, Harry Coyne (of Brisbane), the
Colonial Sugar Co., and a few others are
cordially invited to attend and give their
brother and sister strategists the glad hand.
“Struck Oil,” the moral melodrama, will
make way at the Sydney Criterion on Sat-
urday for “The Gambler’s Sweetheart,”
whose morals are not announced.
Cripple Creek, which was to have been
drained off from the Adelphi to-morrow
night, has been given seven days’ grace, and
will trickle till the end of next week. Then,
like the Cotter, it will dry up and blow
away.
When Jansen did the egg and bag trick it
was appreciated ; the edge was showing signs
of wear when Margo did it at the Tivoli;
and it had developed into a nuisance by the
time De Bierre came along to the same hall,
and repeated it without anv appreciable
change. But De Bierre has one or two
tricks that haven’t been seen here for at
least two years. With the help of a handy
screen and a ’‘double,” he manages to bring
out some illusions at which the pop-eyed
marvel. The tied-thumb trick and the sculp-
turing illusion are especially clever; so this
paper, while not wildly enthusiastic, doesn’t
quarrel with the management for spilling
much ink over De XXX. Nella Webb,
Mozetto (last lap), D’Arc’s Marionettes
and Marini are still on the paysheet. Next
Saturday, Will Whitburn, the Rallis and
the Wilson trio will join them.
The delay of the English mail upset the
Sydney picture shows on Monday; but they
were able to carry on with their reserve
stock. At the Crystal Palace, the attrac-
tion is “Volto,” who is claimed to be a
human storage-battery, and when he is fully
charged, say with 60,000 volts., he invites
the public to light its cigar on his ear or
his necktie. “Volto” will carry on his pre-
carious job until further notice.

Hedley and Bartlett lead the van at the
National Amphitheatre. They are Musical
Mokes, which, on the authority of the in-
valuble Webster, signifies persons who play
on several instruments. T ; hey also sing
several songs and earn several cheers. Maud
Faning is back, likewise Gus Harris. What
a duet this clarion-voiced pair could render
if they agreed to combine ! Violet Black
is still doing well, and the Elliott sisters
and Boy Rene remain on the bill.

“Nightbirds” has been drawing the festive
racegoer to Her Majesty’s (Melb.) in large
quantities. Altogether this Strauss opera is
a joyous affair; even the bored young noble-
man and the stout prison governor do not
ask to be taken seriously. When this paper
last looked in on “Nightbirds,” Charles
Albert was the Count wlioy would a-wooing
go, and he seemed less droll, but a more suit-
able husband for Flo Young than ex-Count
Cannot. Mention of Miss Young moves this
“And Then I Tickled Her Instep.”
journal to exhort the lingering diner-out to
arrive at the theatre before 8.10, at which
time Flo changes into her pink dressing-
gown, in full view of the limelight man. The
approval with which this incident is received
suggests that a dress-clianging ballet would
prove a welcome circumstance in these
nights of popular education. And a stock-
ing-changing chorus would probably cause
the whole of the ground floor to be reserved
for six weeks in advance.
Wallingford, at the Royal, is getting-rich-
quicker than the local punter has a chance
of doing on the odds offered by Sandy
McCohen. The thin story of the beautifully-
tailored Fiji uncle, who rose to higher things
on stepping-stones of his dead honesty, is
helped along by brisk acting, and the play
keeps a fairly tight grip upon the attention
of the audience until the unnecessary last
act, when the _ dull platitude that turneth
away interest is exploited with irritating
persistence. After three acts devoted to the
glorification of crookedness, a fourth, urging
that honesty pays the best dividends, merely
because a couple of “crooks?’ have come by
comparatively lronest wealth through an ac-
cident of commerce, is a Chadbandish ex-
crescence. It is g sop to conventional mor-
ality, on a par with the beautifully moral
teaching hf “Camille,” to the effect that the
broad path leadeth to consumption. During
the week Miss Josephine Cohan, the expert
typiste, who operates her machine with three
fingers, was out of the cast, and her place
was taken by Beatrice Holloway.
Society will Sin no more at the Princess’s
after Friday night. That is to say, it will
not sin any more on Drury Lane lines,
which are so foreign to Marlow-drama that
these latter-day patrons of the house in
Spring-street have not warmed to the story
of Lady Marion and the diamond ta-ra-ra.
Popular-priced opinion is favorable, as a
rule, to any drama that hinges on a woman’s
honor; but when that honor is preserved
from calumny by being passed along in a
cardboard box to the keeping of a mild
young man who disappears under unaccount-
able circumstances, even the honor of the
leading lady loses the amphitheatre’s in-
terest. So “The Sins of Society” is about
to be withdrawn. On Saturday the same co.
will present “The Girl Without a Home,”
billed as another of Mrs. Morton Powell’s
sensational successes.
At the King’s they are reviving “The
Span of Life” until further notice, and the
rolling ocean continues to roll against the
Collin Rocks where the lighthouse warns
the villain’s heavily-insured vessel of its
imminent peril. As a matter of fact, the
light in the turret has gone out for want
of oil, and the villain, after putting two
bullets into Cyril Mackay’s seat of indiges-
tion, hopes to be present at a shipwreck;
but Mackay somehow contrives to ring a
bell and hang out a danger signal, whereby
the marine disaster is averted. After which,
the whole of the Anderson co. migrates to
Africa for a time, and the villain fails in
some more endeavors. His only notable
success is the poisoning of a bunch of grapes
with a hypodermic syringe. This appears
to send him to the gallows on a charge of
murder with malice aforethought. Mean-
while, three acrobats have furnished the
drama with its title by forming the boneless
bridge that leads to the restoration of the
missing papers.
Margaret Cooper’s stay at the Athemeuin
Hall concludes on Saturday. Till the end of
this month the saucy songstress will flit
around Victoria, ere proceeding to the
West, and departing thence for S’Africa.
Margaret’s entertainment, apart from her
own particular part of it, is brighter than
it was aforetime, and Melbourne will miss
the pungent little lady when she’s gone.
There are no material changes in this

There is as yet no mentiofi of the last nights
of the Three Keltons, biit their end will
surely come. No matter how good or other-
wise a turn may be, its end must come. The
grass witheretli; the moving finger writes;
and even the sun and moon shall pass away.

When Tiie Bulletin visited Melbourne
Majestic last week, Red Fox, the poacher,
was spending some crowded moments with
a donah to the right of him, a donah to the
left of him, and large and breathless keepers
foaming curses in his rear. Between the
women and the keepers, Red Fox gets more
excitement _ than he needs, and in the last
few flicks is downed and outed by the men,
while his best-beloved Lizzie becomes a lone
and splendid corpse. A more tedious drama
was “The Change of Spirit,” showing the
reforming influence of love on a hard-faced
burglar; and “Barriers Burned Away” was
a budget of views illustrating how a Yankee
blizzard became the reconciling influence in
a separated family. Like other picture
houses, this week the Majestic is filming
“The Balkan Blaze,” wherein is featured
the preliminary movements of Abdul the
Dashed.

“My little son occasionally gets so bad with croup
that he cannot get his breath,” says Mrs. N. P.
Lewis, Mile End, Adelaide (S.A.). “I just follow
the directions on the Aiottle of Chamberlain’s Cough
Remedy, and he is soon completely cured. I never
to bed without a bottle of it in the house, for I
never know when my little son will be attacked
with this most dreadful complaint. I always feel
safe with Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy by me.”*

One of the Boohs.
Harry Corson Clarke as
“Blackie” Daw.
Claire Romaine lias arrived at Adelaide
Tivoli. She is a daughter of Edward Solo-
mon, the composer, and is a sort of good-
looking Nellie Earren. As a “male imper-
sonator” she prefers the sac suit and the
boyish air, and mostly presents the preco-
cious type for whom any immature house-
maid is an object of swift pursuit. “I
haven’t told my mother yet,” is the burden
of his song, or “If you can’t have a
cuddle at 17, I’d like to know when you
can.” The lady gets the last ounce out of
her material; and the loud chuckles of the
citizen who has had two drinks at the inter-
val seem to show that he gets even more
than that. At the King’s is “La France.”
She is a slim and pretty Australian girl
who has colored lantern-effects thrown at
her, whereby she appears to have a mer-
maid’s tale, or butterfly’s wings, or to be
standing outside a church robed in furs. The
effects are clever, and the turn is highly
popular; also, it is entirely (and to some
people unexpectedly) Proper.
* * * *
Other Adelaide shows are unusually
numerous. Branscombe’s “Dandies” got a
welcome-back from an Exhibition simply
packed; they have since got out into the
open air, where they rightly belong. At
Henley Beach are “Arcadians,” in the open,
too. Fasola and his miracles are having a
short stay at the Exhibition, and just oppo-
site the Wondergraph pictures have taken
up their summer pitch. This leaves the
Town Hall free, and concert-givers have
rushed it —Glee Club, Bach Society and
others to follow. At the Royal is an Irish
boom, with Allen Doone and Miss Edna
Keeley expounding a gentle, genial Hiber-
nian doctrine that takes no account of Red-
mond and O’Brien factions. Business is
very brisk. .
Major Crouch wrote in the S. M. Herald
last week: —
One of the things which interested me was to
learn that the Japanese War Office regularly receives
eight , of the leading Australian papers, and that all
references to the defence matters of the Common-
wealth are systematically filed. I thought I knew
a little about our navy, but the amount of intimate
information which 1 heard in Japan about our
naval preparations from English-speaking Japanese
was an eye-opener. "
Such sidelights make the Peace Society more
optimistic than usual, fill the anti-militarist
with confidence, and satisfy Bruce Smith
that our splendid ally has no more idea of
forcibly adopting Australia that he has of
burgling the planet Jupiter.
Sydney Daily Telegraph last week ad-
vanced the amazing theory that a Senate
which does not represent State Frights has
failed in its mission and might as well be
wiped out. If the people who elect the Sen-
ate choose men who are opposed to State
Frights, that makes no difference —the fact
that they belong to the Senate which was
created to represent the States makes it im-
perative that they should voice the views of
the anti-Eederal State Governments. It never
occurs to the Telegraph to upend the
thing, and demand that the State Govern-
ments should carry into effect the views of
the wholly-elected Senators.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“J.F.H.” (London): William Moore has not yet
“placed” any of the plays of the Melbourne drama-
tists, but he has done something—he has got the
opinions of some of the leading dramatic critics.
Spence, of the Westminster, devotes a column to
their consideration. The ablest of them is, he says,
Louis Esson’s “Dead Timber,” expressing with vigor
a farmer’s struggle for existence in the Gippsland
bush. It is “vivid and dramatic,” and likely to
affect an audience deeply. “The Woman Tamer,” a
story of Melbourne push society, employs a vocabu-
lary that baffles Spence, but he is clearly of the
opinion that Esson is a “skilful dramatist with ideas
of his own.” Of Moore’s “Tea-Room Girl” he
says: “This is a clever and rather curious experi-
ment.” Spence concludes with the hope that “some
London manager will make an experiment with these
works, or some of them.” He adds: “Though of
small and not quite startling quality, they show
much ability, and prove that Australia will soon to a
great extent free herself from the bondage of the
British touring system, and develop the dramatic
gifts of her children.”
“Screen” : One swallow does not make summer, but
one bush rainbird may indicate a deluge. The
first advt. of the auctioning of’a picture show has
just appeared *in Melbourne. Biographs may be a
joy for ever, but this does not preclude a slump.
Melbourne city has nine every-night picture shows;
the suburbs have 13 very big ones, with a host of
occasional and out-of-doors. All this is a prodigious
strain even for a Capua like Melbourne, taking
account of theatres, half a dozen shows on St. Kilda
beach, etc. As for pictures, a leading manager tells
me that all the combined energies of the providores
cannot furnish Australia with more than a dozen
first-rate pictures every week.
‘^Adelaide”: The par. (B. 31/10/’l2) about Wybert
Reeve’s stage history omits the item in which he
himself took most pride. He was offered the lead
in something good at Manchester, but did not find
it convenient, having more attractive offers else-
where. “There’s a young Irving who could do it,”
he said to the management. “Give him a chance.”
So Henry had his chance, and climbed straight up
from that moment. Wybert himself stood still; but
the more Irving soared above him the more pleasure
he took in the memory of that incident.
“Snell”: I never had any particular respect for
elocution in the home, and on the boards it is a most
unfortunate gift the moment the actor gets down to
actual men and manners. Erie Moxon was dealt
great praise for his elocution in “Ben Ilur,” and
certainly was unobiecfionably theatrical as the ex-
tremely stagey B. Hur. But when he attempted to
play the work-a-day hero in a typical melodrama of
our time, the effect produced was a dream of a very
efficient young curate trying to apply his pulpit at-
titudes and accents to the business of selling whelks.
The beauteous Miss Ethel Warwick, who was in
Australia a while ago, has lately been running a
London theatre with a revival of “Zaza”—which is a /
mighty strong play as long as the heroine remains
giddy, and deplorably weak when she turns good, in
the English translation. Alas! it is the fate of all
English versions of French plays to be Wowserised
in the last act.
The wife of Wallace, the composer, was a Miss
Kelly, of Dublin. She accompanied him to Sydney,
where they separated. They did not meet again,
although she lived on until 1900. A few years be-
fore she died there was a London benefit for her,
under Cardinal Vaughan’s patronage. There was one
son of this marriage; he died in the Charterhouse.
In later years Wallace supplied the provisions for
another lady’s house. They had two sons, both of
whom suicided in America. This is the lady referred
to in some biogranhies as Mrs. Wallace, who was
with the composer when he died in the Pyrenees. 1 *

There has been organised in Sydney a vocal co. of
a type numerous and popular in England, and in
Europe generally; but almost unknown in Aus-
tralia, namely, a professional quartette open to sing
at functions where jollity and chortling are com-
bined with “good taste” and “culchaw.” This new
band of vocal buccaneers has styled itself “The Har-
monists,” and comprises Percy Coward, alto ; Charles
Wright, tenor ; Charles Larsen, baritone; and Harold
Widdop, basso. They have hung out a shingle at.
Nicholson’s, and in announcing serenading as a
specialty, hint that there is no reason why the mid*,
night warble beneath the twinkling stars should be'
confined to Spain and Italy.
“Screen”: A fourth good picture theatre is opened’
with excellent prospects on the south side of Princes;
Bridge (Melbourne). This is the Snowden, facing
Snowden Gardens. The history of shows on the south
side began with Cooper and Bailey. Then followed
Chiarini, Cole, Wijspn and Fillis. Fillis was the
first to build in iron, a device afterwards followed
by the Fitzgeralds. Next Wirths got the site and
constructed Olympia for their circus, and when that
odorous joy had departed, West took the house over.
Later on the circus brethren built the Hippodrome,
whereupon West built his own theatre, Spencer took
Olympia and Taits showed in the Glaciarium. These
three big places are still going strong, notwithstand-
ing that the Majestic is flickering over the river in
Flind»rs-street, while the city is infested with strenu-
ous rivals.
Interviewed by N.Y. Times, Oscar Hammerstein
allows that he produced Lord Howard de Walden’s
puerile “The Children of Don,” at London Opera
House, because the noble author made it worth
while to the extent of £20,000. This relieves the
newspapers’ puzzlement as to why the awful stuff
was produced at all. As the Howard de Waldens
own 292 acres of London, the £20,000 is a mere
“petty cash” item to thp titled librettist.
The long-threatened All Irish show, which starts in
Melbourne early next month, includes a consignment
of the Ould Sod. A lot of earth from each of the
four provinces of the distressful country arrived by
mail steamer the other day, and this is to be worked
into the programme of the picture, song and story
entertainment aforesaid. Cinematograph illustra-
tions of “Ireland To-day” are only part of the appeal.
Colored slide pictures will be used as a setting to the
incidental ballads; also various “exhibits” are to
figure in the proceedings. The circular that was
issued from Dublin some time back promised a show
that should interest even the public outside the
radius of Irish national feeling, and the circular,
seemingly, was more truthful that most publications
of its kind.
From London: “Find the Woman” (which is “The
Third Degree” re-named) has had its 100th perform-
ance, and is going strong. So is “Bunty Pulls the
Strings,” nearly 600 not out, and giving nine per-
formances every week. A music-hall is giving a
comedy, including cycling and boxing turns, played
entirely by educated cocks and hens. Another runs
in some pictures of a recent Melbourne beauty com-
petition, and (in natural colors) of surf-bathing at
Manly. And the Hippodrome has been chosen for the
first production in England of a new opera by Leon-
cavallo, composer of “Pagliacci.” This is the day of
the “Hall.” Drury Lane is going to celebrate its
centenary; it was re-built (after being burnt down)
in 1812. H. B. Irving, who is playing there, as No-
body in “Everywoman,” is to have an Irving Theatre
of his own. How is this for a strenuous Shaks-
pearian week, by Benson and Co. ? Monday, “The
Merchant” ; Tuesday, “Antony and Cleopatra” ; Wed-
nesday, “As You Like It” and “The Merry Wives” ;
Thursday, “Antony” ; Friday, “The Rivals” ; Satur-
day, “The Merchant” and “Hamlet.” But what was
“The Rivals” doing in that galley?
“Deadhead” : I do not think that Randolph Bedford
can be responsible for all of “The Unseen Eye,” as
produced at the Sydney Palace. The frequent ex-
planatory soliloquies and the impassioned utterances
of most of the characters are so utterly unßedfordian
that I can only assume Randolph wrote a drama
and an unscrupulous management “meloed” it. All
the same, Bedford needs some cross-examination.
Why did he make Lucy a cripple? Her misfortune
does not forward the action of the play one bit.
Why does he let Eliza (the slavey) make Mabel
Haughton (the heroine) look like a back number?
The dictograph is a Stone Age affair, built on the
lines of an ex-Q; een of Spain’s musical-box, without
stops. A man o! John Proctor’s stamp ought to have
kicked the larynx out of it as soon as it started
babbling—but maybe the management wouldn’t let
him. The same management sent a policeman on
night duty in a helmet and a stand-up collar ! Also,
who engaged a “tec.” who, when he arrives at a sus-
pect’s house in the middle of the night and hammers
impatiently on the door, forgets to look through the
clear glass sidelights, although a full view could be
had in a brjghtiy-lit room of incriminating papers
being burned? The fatuously honest lydy, selling
matches at 2 a.m., belongs to “The Lights of Lon-
don” and other imported dramas. I hope Randolph
has written the next piece, “A Woman of Impulse,”
on his pat. If he hasn’t, I’ll forward a few more
brief remarks.
“Genung”: Hal Gye’s “The Stage Miracle” (B.
17/10/’l2) reminds me of my second job. The first
was in a bank. This lasted one day. It was not my
fault it only lasted one day. It
was my ignorance. How was I
to know they counted the money
every afternoon? The second job
was a series of Biblical tableaux
in connection with the Y.M.C.A.
in Bendilarat. My job was pull-
ing up and letting down the cur-
tain. It was the only paid job
in the show. That’s why I took
it. One tableau represented
“David and Absalom.” It was
well got up. The background
and properties were most effect-
ive. A grey-bearded, atmor-cla -1
David was bending mournfully
over the corpse of long-haired
Absalom. Then someone elocuted
“The Death of Absalom.” He
did it so well that when I let
down “the rag” a great sigh of
sympathy went up from the big
crowd of Wowsers—Holy Joes,
we called them in those days. I
gave one look to the stage, and
then silently and swiftly ran the
curtain up again. What the
audience saw now Was good King
David with his white beard twist-
ed round behind his back, having
a scientific spar with an exceed-
ingly healthy Absalom arrayed in
short shirt and cycling knickers.
They sparred on, unconscious of
their audience till the roar of in-
sulted Wowserdom nearly lifted
the roof. Blasphemy, they called
it. I just dropped the ropes and ran. ’ Didn’t wait
tor a week s notice. Didn’t wait for back pay
Didn t wait for nothing.
“One Who Pays”: As a supporter of both Church
and stage, f raise my protest against a crowd of
Sydney parsons being dragged as deadheads to see
Ben Hur, m order that they may discover how it
advances the church and stage entente. Both' Like
the Sign of the Cross,” “Barabbas” and “Joseph in
Canaan Ben Hur” is all right as a spectacle; but
if the clergy want to be of any use to the stage, let
them support good healthy plays, like “Hypocrites ”
‘ Diana of Dobsons” and the “Third Floor Back!”
And above all let them avoid the dead-head abomina-
tion and pay their way.
“Circle”: During Act 3, “Ben Hur” becomes a
forest of ladies’ legs, and the stage is tenanted by
wild females arrayed only in singlets, tights and
leopard skins, who clash cymbals, leap about with
what the daily paper young man calls abandon, and
rush off to shrill music—also abandoned. During
Act 4, there is a tigerish love scene between
Iras, the shameless Egyptian lady, and the fearfully
emotional Ben ; in fact, things are so warm on the
ferry boat that old Simonides shudders and hurries
his young daughter away. Yet, at the invitation of
the .1. C. Williamson management, these terrible
spectacles were witnessed last week by a whole train-
load of parsons; and when one of them came before
the curtain and uttered words of praise, his brother
saints made no protest. Why? Evidently because
there is a miracle in Act 6, and because the wicked
lose every coin through backing the wrong team in
the chariot race of Act 5. The betting, too, is not
done per medium of the unsanctified totalisator, but
by good old Ikey Moses.
The mixture of kinematograph with ordinary
stagework is an idea with great possibilities. At
the Scala (London), a new “Mephisto” drama un-
folds the story of Wine, and Woman, and Gold
There are hardly any players, except Old Nick, a
Spirit of Good, who finally routs him, Old Harry, and
some dancers. The rest is done per film —the Derby,
Monte Carlo, a burning house, and so on. The same
idea has been largely used in Paris to show lurid
situations of farce-comedy which are thought a little
strong even for the French stage. (Seeing them at
second-hand is supposed not to count, somehow.)
And now Beerbohm Tree introduces a few feet of
film into his new Drake play to show the capture of
a Spanish galleon.
The Princess’s Theatre, in London, where Wilson
Barrett made a fortune with the original production
of “Silver King,” is to be pulled down, together with
some adjoining premises. A £400,000 hotel will be
built on the site.
“The Chee-ild” : I note that more than one aspir-
ing playwright testifies that he has been told by
Australian managers that there is no demand for the
one-act 7 lay. This statement seems to call for con-
tradiction with both feet. Observe the sort of run we
get for our money. For six bright shillings we view
a thin comedy lasting a little over two hours, and
then we are turned into the street an hour and three-
quarters before the pubs close. Take “Alias Jimmy
Valentine” for a specimen. Essentially a one-act
play, it is attenuated into three or four acts by the
introduction of the irrelevant gaoler, the German
lock inventor, and the sentimental female prison re-
formers ; yet it is all comfortably over (with the
assistance of three intervals) by 10.15 p.m. Would
that be accepted in Europe or America as a fair
evening’s entertainment for six bob? Why should we
not expect what they get elsewhere —a bright curtain-
raiser? The cold truth is that there is no market
for the one-act play, because the Australian managers
won’t make one. The need for it is there all right,
but until the public yells for the thing the manage-
ments won’t make the change.
As might have been expected, Mrs. Brown-Potter,
having announced her conversion to Theosophy, has
started lecturing a la Annie Besant. She made, her
debut at the fashionable Ritz Hotel in Piccadilly,
London. Dressed in flowing Asiatic garb, standing on
a raised platform surrounded by colored lights, and
with a towering palm tree on each side, the con-
verted Cora prattled for an hour or so about the
teachings of some superhuman beings who minister
in a “temple situated among the topmost snow-capped
peaks of the Himalayas.”
The English Church Congress has been discussing
the “amusements of the people.” Speakers were
practically unanimous that there had been a vast
change for the better in theatres and music-halls dur-
ing recent years, wherefore nowadays “indiscriminate
and wholesale condemnation of theatres and music-
halls by the clergy was as ill-advised as it was
unjust.”

“0.K.,” concerning certain gassy water :
Hepburn (Vic.) has found its famous mineral
water which was lost, and the residents of that pic-
turesque district are making some sort of com-
bined effort to safeguard its valuable possession
from the machinations of Private Enterprise. The
spring, which was discovered by the Daylesford gold-
rushers nearly 50 years ago, fell, in course of time,
into the power of a syndicate, which 15 months
back got permission from the fatuous Daylesford
council to stop the water for two or three days
whilst some mysterious “improvements” were being
effected. When these things had been done, and
the stopper was withdrawn, something had happened.
The water came from the old outlet almost as freely
as before, but the gas had all gone. Hepburn
looked for the bubbles of reputation in its precious
fluid, and found them not. Whilst the anxious resi-
dents were waiting for the gas to return, the water
flowed away, seemingly, in quest of the gas. Then
came the assumption that the bonedryness of the
spring was due to the operations at the North
Frenchman mine, not far away. And, seeing that
the North Frenchman hadn’t found a reef, the syn-
dicate Consented to close the mine in consideration
of a £5OO grant from the Government. But the
closing down of the mine had no effect on the
gone-dry spring; and Hepburn was losing all hope
when somebody made the joyous discovery that a
previously neglected spring, at some distance from
the main outlet, was giving 100 gallons an hour.
So water from the new supply is being led along to
the springhouse and the baths beyond, and if there is
any to spare from those purposes, the syndicate can
come in and get it—which is a good deal less, of
course, than the syndicate, being a shindykit, asked
for.
From the peaceful Loddon
The Loddon River (Vic.) horror is giving the
dairy settlers of the north a few things to think
about. It shows them, for example, that the plaster-
ing of a house with Scripture texts isn’t much of a
protection against the owner's homicidal mania. The
overwrought maniac in this case rose up in the
middle of the night, brained his wife and four
children and then filled himself fatally with lead.
Here was a man who, in spite of a family taint,
could clearly have lived a decent and tolerably happy
life if the prevalent earth-hunger of the Northern dis-
trict had not got hold of him. The fiendish massacre
he committed, almost without parallel among Aus-
tralian domestic tragedies, is directly traceable to.
the fact that he grabbed at 320 acres of agricul-
tural land with barely capital enough to cultivate
40. To meet the eternal demand of the money-
lender, he worked (often with his lantern) from,
three o’clock in the morning to past 11 o’clock
each night; and his drudge of a wife did much the
same. He would work as a navvy on a road con-
tract for eight hours each day, then return to work
nearly all night on his farm —not for his living,
note; but in order that he might own for all time
this slice of the earth. Flesh and blood couldn’t
stand it. Influenza attacked him, and let loose
various devils; and they did the rest.

The enterprising Australian youth! The
day after a cable message was published
stating that letters containing money had
been abstracted from letter-pillars by means
of bird-lime, this ingenuous inquiry written
in a boyish hand was received by an Ade-
laide daily newspaper: “If you plese, how
do you make bird-lime?”
The Vie. Executive seems to have gone
over the legal odds in sentencing a wretched
sexual maniac and a small-sized burglar to
three floggings apiece, in addition to im-
prisonment for life. The second and third
whippings were to have been served out at
six months’ intervals, but the Law seemingly
doesn’t permit of prisoners being kept in
painful expectation for a whole year. So
the culprits will be belted only once.

“10-2-4” discovers an heroic soul: —
Governor Strickland will find at least one warm
admirer when he arrives in N. S. Wales, namely,
myself. My enthusiasm is based on a hint dropped
by Henley, M.L.A., the other night. Said he of the
defective aspirate:—
“I have met and travelled with Sir Gerald Strick-
land, on board the Orontes, four years ago, when I
was returning from England, and he was on his way
to Tasmania. He is of a retiring disposition, and
did not mix much -with the passengers.”
A trifling remark to the uninitiated, possibly ; but to
those who are accustomed to go down to the sea in
mail ships, full of significance. I know those strong,
scark souls who do not mix much with the passen-
gers. I likewise know the frenzy of hate which
burns in the bosoms of the Henleys of this world
in regard to them. Henley, if one can get any sort
of a line by his performances in the Assembly and
elsewhere, is a member of that loathly class —the
Passengers who are Determined to Make Things Go
with a Swing. With fancy’s eye I can see the dread-
ful, well-meaning little man hurrying round tne
Orontes, in his bowler hat, black tail coat, chest-
protector tie and sand-shoes, littering the entire ves-
sel with aspirates in the attempt to get people who
only want to be left alone to play bull board, quoits
(plain and bucket), and other tiresome and asinine
games; join in the sweep on the ship’s run ; sing,
recite, conjure, or perform some similar debasing
act at the forthcoming concert ; participate in a
g'ame of animal grab in the music saloon; choose a
fancy costume at the barber’s shop—preferably one
with a false nose, or kindred comic effect in it —
for the fancy-dress ball; be one of the jury at a
mock trial, and by various other devices cause wide-
spread fury and boredom. One after the other,
passengers turn weakly from those pursuits which
really appeal to them, and consent, with some
muddled idea that it is all for the public good, to
join in revels which they abhor. Suddenly Henley
comes to Strickland. He probably addresses the
potentate ingratiatingly as “Me Lord,” if not “Yer
Grace.” In vain. Strickland will have none of
him. Perhaps he merely goes on reading while
Henley shuffles foolishly before him. A still more
splendid vision is that of Strickland rising menac-
ingly and threatening to kick Henley, in the event
of that legislator persisting in his importunities.
Henley retires in disorder to the smoking-room
where his confederates lurk. The story of Strick-
land’s intolerable hauteur is told. Amidst a cease-
less crash of falling “h’s,” such conduct is con-
demned as unmanly, un-British, and calculated to
sap the foundations of Empire. In a little while
the schemes to render the passengers jolly and soci-
able are embarked on once more. Strickland con-
tinues to read placidly. The passengers who are
being “amused” by Henley and his colleagues watch
the recalcitrant, enviously, admiringly. They would
give anything to follow Strickland’s lead, but lack
the nerve. That has always been my trouble. But
it does not debar me from depositing my meed of
homage at the feet of a braver and more resolute
spirit—if indeed a spirit has feet.
“Squills” : The action brought
against the British Medical
Association by a Consumption
Curemonger is an interesting
exposure of the fallibility of
the Faculty. Apart from a
question re the correctness of
the analysis published in the
Association’s book of revela-
tions, there was a conflict of
medical opinion as to the
effects of the “quack” cure.
Several doctors gave it credit
for being beneficial, or even ab-
solutely curative; others said
it was injurious to the patients
they had known of. Possibly
both sides were right. Seeing
that oualified men often admit
the failure of their experi-
mental physic, by changing
the prescription when the
patient gets worse, an unquali-
fied man can’t be expected to
get always the same results
with his one prescription. A
consumptive may be benefited
by a mixture that serves his
lungs, provided that his kidneys
or liver are not diseased. An-
other consumptive dies from
the ill effects of the same stuff
on his Bright’s disease, or
what not. There are risks in
all desperate treatments. What
is a cure under certain condi-
tions is a kill when the condi-
tions are quite otherwise. And
the most learned practitioners
are sometimes blind to the
otherwiseness of the condi-
tions. In which onse they invent a
new medical name for the fatal blunder, and
certify that the patient died of it in a proper
manner.

Melbourne Argus touched the limit of
daily Jeames-journalism last week in pub-
lishing a photo (12 x 7) of the vice-regals at
a State Government House garden party. It
was a lovely picture of the Fullers and their
guests seated in the foreground, with seven
aides and abettors standing behind. Snob-
ocracy gloats over photos of this sort. They
serve as object-lessons in class distinction in
the upperest circles. Also they draw public
attention to the importance of spats. And
every Tory newspaper ought to insist upon
the importance of spats.

Prom the Oldest Inhabitant’s grandpa:—
The first census taken in Melbourne was on May
30, 1836, by Captain Stewart, P.M. The tally was
177 ; only 35 were females.
The city was first known by the native name of
Narr’n. Altered afterwards to Bearbran. This name
didn’t last long. AVhen Hoddle came in ’37 he
wanted the town to be christened Glenelg. Batman,
the first settler, wanted it called Batmania. Gov-
ernor Bourke arrived in March, ’37 ; he objected, to
the first name because he hated Glenelg, then Secre-
tary of State for the Colonies, and to the second be-
cause of the last two syllables. He went for safety,
and fixed it at Melbourne. Melbourne was Prime
Minister of England.
Hoddle then planned the city. Between the big
streets he placed narrow lanes to serve the out-
houses. Hence Little Collins, Little Flinders, Lil
Buk, etc.
The first pub was just behind where the Custom
House stands—Johnny Fawkner’s. Johnny’s father had
been sent out in 1802 for buying stolen jewellery,
and Johnny himself was, in 1814, publicly flogged
before his father’s house for helping some convicts
to escape. His pub was the hub for many yearsi He
also ran a circulating library and a newspaper. Be-
sides, he was sawyer, farmer, hotelkeeper, bush
lawyer, land jobber, member of Parliament and corpse.
He kept a good pub. Rum was rum in those days.
The Westralian Government a while ago
bought a steamer to bring out population
for its empty wastes; and arrangements
were made whereby it was claimed that a
first saloon passage would be provided for
£3O. The ship has now arrived; and ac-
cording to the yarns of passengers, what
was provided was a blend of Tophet and
Chaos. Fourteen letters have dropped in
up to the time of writing alleging that the
lavatory arrangements were bad, or that the
water was rusty, or if it wasn’t rusty that
it was brackish ; that hot baths were unob-
tainable in cold weather, and cold baths
unobtainable in hot; that stewards were so
grimy that they couldn’t be distinguished
from firemen; that the cabins were dirty,
the bread bad, the dishes often unwiped,
and ice, cake, fruit, tea and coffee always
scarce. In addition, the electric light seems
to have had a playful habit of going out,
and the wireless plant frequently refused
duty. Also, the fish was decayed, the meat
tainted, and the butter rancid, while the
cheese was adorned with thumbprints like
the library of a criminal department. It is
a stirring record of what General Muddle
can do when the old gentleman really tries :
and even if half the 14 correspondents’
statements are blotted out as the work of
fevered heads, hungry stomachs and en-
raged imaginations, there is enough left over
to make a pretty substantial indictment of
the people who had charge of the under-
taking.
From a man with
a memory:—
Apropos “Old St.
James’s” (B. 24/10/’l2) :
Fifty years ago the
only church in Mel-
bourne with a peal of
bells was old St.
James’s, and it also
had an organised com-
pany of bell-ringers.
William Barton, an
old London showman,
kept an hotel on the
opposite corner (Ohan-
cerv-lane and William-
street), and on prac-
tice nights he used to
send the ringers a buc-
ket of beer. His son
mostly carried the
beer, and I carried the
pint-pots. One night,
on our way to the bel-
fry, we encountered
the verger. He was
horrified at the idea of
“bringing beer into the
sacred edifice.” What
would .Mr. Beecher (the
rector) say? We had
to take that beer back,
but the ringers got
the ; >’ pint all the
sain e. Just before
Barry Sullivan, then
lessee of the Theatre
Royal, produced
“Faust,” he strode in-
to Barton’s one day,
and, in the usual de-
liberate tones, asked
where he could find the
captain of the bell-
ringers. Barton sent
a messenger off for the
captain, who was 'em-
ployed in the Loco. De-
partment of the Rail-
ways. Bill Chrisfield
—he was the captain
—tpld the actor-mana-
ger that to get a set
of bell-ringers for the
show was as easy as
falling off a log—if
Mr. Sullivan would
provide the bells.
Within five minutes
Sullivan had stalked
down Little Collins-
street to James
McEwan’s, where the
captain selected the ironmongery, and rehearsals were
commenced. “Faust” was produced and proved a
big success, Sullivan being Mephistopheles. It ran
for 24 weeks, a remarkable thing for those times.
A Newtown (Sydney) female has origi-
nated a new and terrifying method of dis-
posing of the unwanted infant. Walking up
to a house where a window ticket an-
nounced that there were rooms to let, she
took an apartment and deposited the child
on the bed; then she went out and never
came back any more. Certainly it is a much
more humane method than that by which the
Wowser and the Liberal Union female en-
courage unfortunate girls to dispose of their
progeny. But it has the disadvantage of ex-
posing the “dropper” to the risk of early
identification. Besides, the rooms-to-let
duchess mostly wants money in advance.
So, one way and another, the new method
isn’t likely to be very popular.

The Presbyterian Messenger (Vic.)
spoke of those people who cried out against
the abominable idea of inflicting three
floggings on Moore, the man sen-
tenced to life-long imprisonment without
a hope, as “drivelling moral degenerates,”
talkers of “sentimental rubbish.” “God Al-
mighty gives the magistrate a sword, not
a painted lath,” continued the Christian
brother. “There are people so hardened to all
ethical considerations that society can only
impress them through their skins.” Just so. It
reminds us that the Presbyterian Messen-
ger recently printed a picture of Christ with
a palm leaf in His hand. Now, flogging being
a godly thing (“God Almighty puts a sword
into the magistrate’s hand”) , The Bulletin
cordially invites its saintly contemporary to
print a companion picture showing Christ
with a cat-o’-nine-tails in His hand.

ON THE DISGUSTING PEACE AT PARKES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Parkes, Thursday.—A public meeting, which was
called with tire object of protesting against the evic-
tion of the Governor-General, was held last night, and
was attended by about 20 persons. It was decided
to postpone the gathering until November 18, and
a committee was appointed to draft motions and
arrange speeches. — Sydney Sun. 7/11/’l2.
There’s a fearful lack of uproar in the neighborhood
of Parkes;
There’s a dearth of indignation and a scarcity of
barks ;
There’s no groaning,
And no moaning ;
There’s no discontent at all ;
But a rotten,
God-forgotten
Peace envelopes like a pall.
All the happy country township ambles gaily, on its
way,
And no deaf’ning howls of anguish mar the long and
happy diy.
But that soon will be adjusted, and the streets with
rebels thronged,
If they’ll only come and listen when we tell them
we’ve been wronged.
'Tis a weakness most depressing of the countryman
who stares—-
This indifference to the city and its glorious affairs.
When we’re slighted,
Unexcited
Ho still goes about his jobs;
While we’re railing
And bewailing,
He will not assist with sobs —
Or at least he won’t assist us till we show him why
he should,
For he’s dreadfully self-centred and his heart is made
of wood.
Still we’re hoping to inflame him ’gainst the boorish
Labor narks.
And our hearts will thrill with gladness when there's
grief and rage in Parkes.

“Melb”: The Grange, the historic man-
sion of boomster Fink, on the corner of St.
Ivilda-road and Domain-road, lias just been
knocked down. Last year the place was
half burned, and the property was after-
wards bought by the Victorian Govern-
ment to square off the Domain. A splendid
view over park lands to Hobson's Bay is
thus opened up. Of course, the chunk now
restored to the public should never have been
alienated. Fitzgibbon, who fought hard against
every proposed theft from the people’s
lands, used to tell how he rode with Governor
Latrobe round the Melbourne reserves, and
how proud Latrobe was of having provided
such lungs for the future city. Pretty well
all of them have since been truncated, and
some must yet be repurchased at great cost.
A very gradual lady is Mrs. A. M. Walsh,
who for 12 years did the washing for the
Supreme and County Courts at Ballarat.
During that time the lady omitted to send
in a bill; and wh'fen the Crown Law De-
partment got one for £5B 2s. 5d.. it kicked
like the ass the Law is supposed to be.
Mrs. Walsh’s position in matrimony was
that of the wife of the court crier—a gentle-
man who some time ago gave up his job.
and, like royalty, was succeeded by his son.
Mrs. Walsh, following suit, gave up the
washing of the court towels, and proceeded
to square things up in a business-like way
by forwarding a bill. Then started a tre-
mendous row. The Crown Law Depart-
ment thought Mrs. Walsh ought to apply
to her husband for payment, holding that it
was part of his duties to supply clean towels
and other official linen. Mrs. Walsh entered
a demurrer; after which the secretary made
a number of futile trips to Ballarat to effect
a settlement. How much these trips added
to the bill it is hard to say; but, anyhow,
the Department eventually parted up
£lB 6s. by instalments, and the very gradual
Mrs. Walsh, who is still stonily obdurate, is
considering further action, while the whole
force of the Department is mobilising to
meet madame’s onslaught.
“Karee”: “Buttonstiek,” “Machete,” “Pot-
ash Bromide,” et tutti quanti (in a Dago

the uses and useless-

against cavalry. They
all seem to fora t that
the war-cycle used by
the Germans, French,
Dago, Austrian and
Belgian armies, and
used with success, is
the Martineau tyne,
a collapsible, alumin-
ium affair of the light-
est weight. With such
a. machine there is no
need, as suggested by
“Buttonstiek,” for the
cyclist to drop “his
mount under cover”
before crawling to a
position. He merely
folds the bike, wheel
touching wheel, hoists
it flat on his knap-

bis post. When serv-

time had to charge a

corps with their

ders, and I witnessed
many spirited bayonet
charges carried
through in spite of
the added weight of
the wheel; for the
French infantryman
lias to charge, fully
equipped, with haver-
sack, knapsack, am-
munition. etc., on his
]) ati e n t shoulders,
being taught to de-
pend not on the army waggons for supplies,
but on liis own carrying capacity.
One day last month a motor-car dashed
into a squad of cadets who were being
drilled at Clifton Hill (Melb.) and injured
one of them. The driver did not stop to see
what damage he had done, or find out if it
was necessary to rush the injured boy to a
hospital or a doctor. He simply went like
Jehu, furiously, and for the time being got
right away. But the police, in a spasm of
wakefulness, succeeded in tracking him
down. Haled before the Bench on three
charges, he was hit with fines amounting in
all to £l2, with 15s. costs. Part of his
offence was that he had no license —which
was probably why he did not stop. But the
practice of making a bolt for safety, under
any circumstances whatever, requires to be
severely discouraged. The average man
who runs a car can easily find £5, the sum
which this young ruffian was called upon to
pay for. bolting. Three months without the
option is the deterrent required-—partly in
the interests of motorists themselves, who
otherwise may find an enraged public taking
drastic reprisals.

Whereat there was applause, though if the
same sort of thing had been said in the
case of plasterers, carpenters or hod-car-
riers, there would have been merely a howl
of disapproval, coupled with a demand for
the immediate arrest of somebody.
BASE INGRATITODE.
OWNER (whose horse has just run second):
“What kind of a blanky horseman do you.
call yourself?”
JOCKEY (in disgust): “Well, strike me fat.
some of you chaps is never satisfied.
Didn't I beat seven of 'em !”
A malicious Fate has been working over-
time in Adelaide. (1) An old well, sunk
half a century back, was boarded over and
forgotten. In time an hotel was built over
it. In the course of years the boards rotted.
A cook arrived, who was a heavyweight; she
happened to step fair in the middle of the
old well, and went down to her death. (2)
A long plank, being planed, bent and broke,
and the splinters flew. One of them sought
out the foreman of the shed, over 20ft.
away. It happened (a) to strike him on
the head, (6) to come end-on, (c) to
pierce his brain. (3) An hotel servant
(Grand Central) rang for the lift —auto-
matic. There happened to be a hole in the
glass of the doorway. He put his head
through to watch it come up; it happened
to be coming down, instead. He made the
third corpse.
Last week Sydney specialised in fire
thrills. An 80-foot ladder from the Circu-
lar Quay station was reared like a wine-
soaked mantis in front of a tall fire, when
there was a sound like a shot, and the
crowd drew deep breaths for the two hel-
meted figures clinging high up on the frac-
tured thing. But, instead of collapsing
with the men, the great ladder swayed over
and rested wearily against the burning
building, and so the pair crept back to
ea-rth. Next night, a policeman had come
to the door of the_ Clarence-street station to
get a breath of air, or arrest attention, or
something, when a high, far cry of “Fire!”
was wafted to him. The hail came from a
man clinging to the dark ledge of a three-
storey window four doors away. The con-
stable yelled to him to hang on while he
rang for the brigade, which promptly trundled
along with its big ladder, and thus brought
the dazed and choking refugee down to the
street. While on night work he had been
trapped by a fire, which started in the base-
ment and blocked both the goods-life shaft and
stairway. A rope ladder (kept, presum-
ably, for such emergencies) was found- by
the desperate man to be stuck in its box.
So he tried the window ledge, and won by a
brief minute or so. If lie had been on the
thirteenth floor of a skyscraper he couldn’t
have been picked like a wingless fly off the
outside wall. Skyscraper enthusiasts, of
course, argue that all decent ’scrapers are
fireproof, like the Devil and other great in-
stitutions. But even the ’scraper seems to
burn well when it gets a start, and there is
no proof that the Devil wasn’t consumed
hundreds of years ago.

A terrible thing happened at a meeting-
of the Northcote (Melb.) Council, the other-
evening. The council has a “Socialistic’”
member who was advocating day labor
when the Mayor ruled him out of order.
The disrespectful Socialist moved that the
Mayor’s ruling be disagreed with, and made
remarks derogatory to the chair, where-
upon the mayor desired him to sit down.
He refused to sit down, so the Mayor said:
“There is only one course left me to adopt.
(Pause.) I name councillor McDonell.”
The council was naturally too broken-up to
proceed any further. Nobody, however,
seems to know what happened, to the un-
happy victim. What does happen to a
town councillor when he is named by the
Mayor ?

A SCORCHER.
[FOR THE BULLETIB.]
90.4—Record in the City.—Hottest Day since
Last Summer.—Welcome Southerly Change.
—Newspaper Headlines.
No breezes stirred ; the polished sea was grey ;
But in the morning silences I heard
A bird —
Some tiny feathered thing which seemed to say
In nervous chirps, “There will be hell to-day!”
The sky was slightly hazed. (It always is
When something diabolical is due).
It flew
That tiny bird away in one wild whiz.
(The tiny prophet knew his humble biz.)
The hot North-easter, like a furnace blast,
Rushed from the region Arthur, M.L.A.,
(Hooray !)
Insists on representing, first and last,
As Liberal. From there it travelled fast.
It leapt the Harbor, and, in robes of dust,
Arrayed the fainting city to the south.
Each mouth
Was dry as any oven, until thrust
With fervor through the long beer’s snowy crust.
The wind flew by at forty knots or so;
The hours stood still (or so at least it seemed),
Men dreamed,
And tried to work, o’er hot desks crouching low,
For Takerndown, O’Swindle, Bust and Co.
“At 3.ls”—when all were nicely done—
“ The wind veered round.” How sweet and cool
it sounds!
Like hounds,
Mad hounds unleashed, that fiercely spring and run,
The Pole-struck winds came back chock full of fufi.
The temperature fell like some mean cur
Kicked from a stairhead by a scornful god
Steel-shod.
The cool breaths passing made the forests stir
And cooled hot whiskers, feathers, grass and fur.
As tired housefathers dimmed the white-hot gas,
And on cane lounges sprawled in garments white
Bediglit,
They gazed aloft and saw a berg-lilce mass—
The white moon floating in Night's brimming glass.
N.S.W.
E. O’F.

“Warrumbueca” : The Adelaide horsehair
who advocated co-operation amongst farmers, in the
ownership of agricultural implements (B. 10/10/’l2)
has evidently about as much knowledge of coi kying
, , ~ , «...,, , .. ,
as that contributor “Agncola,” who alleged that
farmers burned wheat stubble for the fun of seeing
a blaze. It is true that a farmer who knows his
games uses, say, his plough “only about once a year,”
but the “once” is all the year round. His cultivator
.... ... . ~ . . , ,
likewise. All crops m one district are ready for the
reaper and binder on practically the one day, and cocky
rushes the machine into the paddock, so as to get as
much cut as possible while the crop is just in that
condition to make the best hay. Every day that it
stands after that condition has been reached is
detrimental, so far as hay-making is concerned In
the same way, if. the crops are allowed to stand for
gram, all in a given district ripen at the same time
LfTT Wl , gr expedition, as thun-
2™? Vf T dB n T „ V llke *
S“ e . t 0 all ™ the I thoUght , s t 0 dwell °n the
efforts of four or five cockles who are joint owners
in a reaper-and-binder, struggling in an atmosphere
of whiskers and profanity, for the right to secure
a little hay before the sun ripens the growing crop
into hard straw, or for the right to garner in some
wheat before the seed is entirely thrashed out by the
sportive winds. It is because he is unable to pur-
chase the necessary implements of his craft that the
mortgage-burdened cocky so often goes under. The
modern farmer wants his own implements, and lots
of them, and the off-chance of using a jointly-owned
piece of harvesting machinery is no better to him
than a ticket in Tatt.’s, or an interest in the Resur-
rection.
Australia is always discovering some new
and wonderful creature, or traces of him. The 10ft.
hairy man has left his awful footprints on the Ma
State’s mud, and the kangaroo-tiger or paddy-
melon-leopard has given glimpses of himself v, hile
skimming lightly over six-wire fences, with a bullock
or a jackeroo or something tucked safely under his
arm. Now arrives the fire-moth, which, if it multi-
plies, will drive capital out of the country worse
than Six Hatters. One blew along to a N.S.W. town-
ship last week, and when a lady lit a candle in her
bedroom, the creature flew into the flame, caught
fire, whirled himself into the bed-curtains, set fire
to them, then dropped on to the quilt and com-
mitted arson there as well. After that he whirled
round generally, and had a first-class conflagration
going before anybody could ring up for the police
or some other sort of trap. Two little children sleep-
ing in the room were rescued with difficulty by the
scared and horrified mother. If this 'type of bug
becomes numerous, curtains and quilts will require
to be of asbestos. All moths dash into a light; but
this particular one seems, to be unusually strong and
large, and to have the power of bouncing round
while he is still alight. The insurance companies
may presently have to combine in self-defence, and
offer valuable rewards for scalps.
“Yarrum”: These Norwegian whalers are
playing havoc among the whales between Botany
and Twofold Bays. Recently, on a Sunday morning,
I saw one shot off Pt. Bass; and the critter was
secured and lashed fast to the side of the steamer
within the hour. The boat then sped southward, and,
two miles further on, right off the Kiama Blowhole,
another aquatic mammal was shot and lashed along
the port side of the vessel. The two monsters, one
a Rorqual or black whale, 94ft. long, were taken on
to Jervis Bay, where, at time of writing, there are
no less than 10 ashore, to be tried out. Three
large whales, fastened together, have been lying
upon the beach at Shellharbor for a fortnight, with-
out being called for, and, as they are getting a bit
high—and a high whale is a fearsome thing—the
townspeople have thought of calling a thanksgiving
service, or something. Many of the townsmen are of
opinion that it would be cheaper, and not half so
nasty, to shift the town. The skipper of the Camp-
bell says each insect is worth “all out £600.” The
Norwegians use every part of the whale except the
spout.
“Pick”: The knock-out to “Bromide.”
“Jimmie Pannikin” and other upholders of the bike-
in-war idea, comes from the cycle trade itself. Any
dealer in jiggers will explain that, notwithstanding
the great decrease in prices, their output has slumped
horribly, and that it is almost impossible to place a
bike except in country parts where the roads are
smooth and level. The push-and-burst-yourself ma-
chine is now a back number, and every unfortunate
who has pedalled far, either for business or pleasure,
can understand why. It is murderous toil even on
good roads, and utter madness when attempted across
country. The shearers referred to by “Pannikin”
adopted the jigger because on many stations they
were charged a heavy agistment fee for their horses ;
but they always travelled the roads—-and the closing
of roads is a painfully frequent occurrence in war
time. Even there the sheep barbers did more leading
and cursing than riding. Generally after a season’s
trial of plugging they returned thankfully to horse-
flesh.

“Tiaki” : The remarks of “Tumumu” (•]'».
10/10/’l2) re the sick-soil belt, revive some vege-
table memories. In many parts of the Rickettv Isles,
and at certain seasons, cattle and sheep get on the
jag from a too free indulgence in either rangiora
(Brachyglottis rangiora ) or tutu (Coriaria rusci-
folia). The effects are similar to those felt by a man
who has swallowed a gallon of beer after eating a
pound of baking-powder, only more so. The animals
(especially horses) become as corpulent as a repre-
sentative Mr. Fat, assume a very knowing look, and
neigh out all sorts of roysterous challenges before
proceeding to do steeplechases and hurdle-jumps that
a champion would tremble at. If discovered in time
the drunken patient is (1) put through his paces until
he sweats off the effects, or (2) his head is well
smoked until he “coughs it up,” or (3) incisions are
made for blood-letting, which, it is claimed, is a
sure cure. I have heard some bushmen assert that
a last resource is to bore holes in the animal’s hide
and let the wind out. Henare makes a physic out of
a flax root and salt, which he administers to his
steed when “tutued.” Curiously enough tutu leaves
are among the cures used by the Maori for dysen-
tery. The berries (which are small and black) may
be sucked, and a delicious juice extracted. But he
who swallows the small stones attends his own
funeral. The same unprofitable experience falls to
the person who eats honey poisoned by the fool-bee’s
industry amongst the rangiora.
* * * »
“Te Babarantiko,” press agent for the
octopus: Much has been written about the pursuit
of human beings by the octopus, but, as a matter of
fact, the poor brute is more often the hunted than
the hunter. When the young ’pus is not long out of
the egg, and is from half a dozen to a dozen inches
long, it is no bad experience to hunt him by torch-
light, in the shallow water on the outer edge of the
coral reef, when he comes out for food and recreation.
All you see in the blaze of the palm-leaf torch
waved by the native above your head, is the lit-up
circle of water. As you peer into the liquid you
see a small, shapeless lump of jelly. The nigger
makes a grab at it, tranquilly bites it to death,
and slides it into the basket. In the season thousands
of the young innocents are spread out on mats in
the native villages to be sun-cured. When the ’pus
is a size larger, the old men catch him swimming
along the edge of the reef. Some training is needed
to tell his abode among the innumerable holes and
cracks. Nai Akaba taught me that if I saw a few
pebbles and broken bits of coral in a hole, there
surely was a devil-fish in it. We set to work to dis-
lodge one with an iron bar, and after I got a good
hold of the fiend, or whatever it is, he came with
long arms twisting and writhing, and, with a curious
sucking sensation fastened on my arms and hands.
Nai Akaba laid hold of him and bit him just below the
bulb-shaped head. The dark-brown sepia gushed out
over his face, arms and hands, streaming down his
chest, coloring him a dull, dark brown. The twisting
tentacles relaxed; the octopus was dead. One great
’pus was so securely housed we failed to dislodge him.
We could touch him with our fingers, but could get
no. grip. Stepping back, we waited and kept quiet,
Nai Akaba with iron bar poised. Slowly a shadowy
form arose. Then Nai Akaba hurled the bar. The
’pus dropped back, but a moment too late: the iron
had pierced the head, nailing him to the coral
rock, and he was without difficulty hauled out, bitten
to death, and turned inside out.
“Reka” : Last week a half-caste postman
came down our street, and before a large house with
an imposing Maori name he smote his thigh and
chortled loud and long. I inquired the reason of
his mirth. He pointed to the name on the gate.
“Pain in the stomach,” he said, briefly. Recovering
a little he became communicative. “There’s a house
in the next road called Boiled Eel, and one up in
Ponsonbv named Old Woman.” Heard a complaint
myself recently from a suburbanite, who called his
place a Maori name which meant “Rest awhile,” or
“Come-and-have-a-drink,” or something equally cheer-
ful. He thought it pleasant to live in a place with
a good homely title, until wandering Maoris read
the sign and began to take the place at its word. As
he couldn’t very well support the whole population,
he painted out the name, and is now looking for
something that means “Go to Tophet,” or “Beware
of several devils.”

The local cab proprietor’s idea of justice not

“Stingaree”: The Queensland ’gator or
croc, has only one friend—the white crane. Have
often crept towards a sleeping saurian, with murder
in my hand, but just at the moment when I was
within range a crane has raised himself into the
air with a shriek, and the croc., thus notified of
danger, has swerved and plunged into the water.
Wherever there is a ’gator haunt there will also
he found white cranes. Another species of the
bird, I’m told, does sentry duty for the Egyptian
croc., in a similar manner.
“WOT’S UP ’ERE?”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We had dinner at about 12 o’clock, and felt all
right after it, and played several games. About 1.30
p.m. one of the ladies complained of feeling sick, but
we didn’t think it was anything serious. Then sud-
denly four of them collapsed. That was about 3.30.
Soon there were 14 of them bad. I administered an
emetic of salt water. It was not long before we had
a big crowd round us, and it took two constables all
their time to keep the people back. —Sydney ptomaine
picnicker’s account of the usual summer trayedy.
“Stan’ back there! Mind yer toes!!”
“Orright! Don’t lose yer ’ead !
Wot’s up in ’ere? ’Oo knows?”
“ ’Oo sez there was two dead?”
Upon the velvet grass,
Beside the scornful sea,
They shoved and tried to pass
To view the tragedy.
No more with woodland love
They helped old Time to fly,
Giving for slaps a shove,
And now and then a sigh.
But arms round waistlines wound,
And full of calm delight,
Surveyed with joy profound
The unexpected sight.
They caught the wild “Wot’s up?”
Of those that pushed and shoved.
(On tragedy to sup
Much more than love they loved.)
To very few Pate gives
Joy’s overflowing cup;
The plain bloke while he lives
Must shove and cry, “Wot’s up?”
N.S.W.

SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bright, bright the sun burns up the road. How faint
the wild hops smell!
The. brown cups of boronia blooms with sickly spices
swell.
Spring’s bosom, beating up the wind, has swept the
morning sweet;
Last night between the ringbarked trees I heard
your horse’s feet;
I did not know those hoofs would strike across my
heart to-day,
Now guess how little time it takes to kiss and to
betray.

IN TOPHET. THE BIRD-NESTER.
“Barcoo Billy”: Noticed recently an ar-
ticle in a magazine, referring to the absolute disap-
pearance of Leichhardt’s expedition, and re-
marking that it is surprising that some
trace was not found of the live stock (horses,
cattle and goats) which accompanied it into
Central Queensland. Evidently the writer of that
article has never been on the Upper Barcoo, or he
would not complain of the scarcity of goats. The
early pioneers found them in large numbers, and
now they infest the landscape. Likewise, in the
ranges on Enniskillen, mobs of clhanskin qattle and
horses, have been running for years past, and for
these even the earliest settlers cannot accotint. Owing
to the nature of the country they are generally un-
molested, and continue to roam at their own free
will. But goats! In spite of periodical killing-off,
many of the Central towns reek of "oat. ProbaMv
.Tericho, on the Jordan (C.Q.), holds the record.
Like the original Jericho, it is a fragrant place.
“J.W.C.” : This is the plain, unvarnished
lie about Danny, the rousey at The Fair Cow, Woolan-
grabbit. In a fit of good feeling towards humanity
he hurled himself into the adjacent river, but was
saved from a timely death by the local John Hop.
Danny was locked up for the night, and next morning
was discharged by the Beak, with a warning not
to do it again. An hour later, Danny was mopping
up our sympathy in the bar. “Gorstruth,” he com-
plained, “them police blokes are the (unspecified)

me. After they locked me up last night they ' it
me in me wet duds fer four hours. Gorstruth (and
various other things), a bloke might have caught his
death of cold.”

“Reka” : According to Dr. Roinare, M.L.
Minister for Native Affairs, the pure-bred Maori bids
fair to soon be as. extinct as the moa. So rare is
he . that the Doctor asserts he can count the few re-
maining on the fingers of one
hand. The distinction of being a
Maori is. now much coveted, and
when entering the children at the
, Government colleges the parents
scorn the “Quarter,” “Half” and
“Three-quarter” columns, and
make every effort to get the
children classed as “Maori.” The
teachers are not over strict, and
classify by color rather than by
ancestry. Eketere, very black-
haired and . brown-eyed, chanced
along ,the other day, and was, on
her own statement, entered as
“Maori.” Later came Riria,
equally dark, and by choice
“Maori” also. The third arrival
from the same household was
Anihi, fair-skinned, blue-eyed,
with a cloud of fluffy yellow hair,
who wept bitterly when an as-
tounded teacher declined to mark
her as a Maori, and hastened to
adjust the rest of the family.
Golden-hair’s mother called to
protest, but admitted that the
children were quarter-castes. This
mix-up is not infrequent in such
cases, where the brown strain is
running out, and blue or grey eyes
are as common as brown. Yellow
hair is not uncommon even among
the older Maoris, who attribute it
to inter-marriage with the red-haired race they claim
to have wiped out on their arrival here.

“Culex” : “E.S.S.” displays much ignor-
ance about ducks (mountainous and perfumed) when
he affirms (B. 10/10/’l2) that they are purely orna-
mental, and no better to eat than soap. The moun-
tain duck of my acquaintance on the salt lakes round
Esperanee (W.A.), when handled by a judicious chef,
needs no sauce to make him go. Have, also tried the
musk or beralla, and admit that he is somewhat
tough; but when he has been deprived of his as-
bestos hide, and treated firmly, he is as tender as a
spring flapper. Moreover, the musk doesn’t fight over
his fancy feeding ground. I have seen him associate
almost to the verge of familiarity with members of
the teal and other tribes. I allow that “E.S.S.”
knows a lot about ducks; so do I. But mine wear
their own feathers.
“Eucalyptus Ernie”: Consider the quan-
dong. This fruit is of the gayest red color imagin-
able, and its beauty must be designed to tempt
some bird or beast to eat it. The trees never seem
to grow in clumps, but are found scattered through
the bush. Nor are young trees found sprouting
where the fruit falls from the old ones. All this
points to the idea that the seed —a good, solid one
it is, too—needs to pass through the alimentary
canal of some animal before it can germinate. Now,
the birds of the bush are too small; brother kangaroo
surely must be acquitted; and we are left face to
face with the question: Do gohannas swallow quan-
dongs ?
"Pick”: Re the 7ft. high monstrosity, with
grey hair and a face like a baboon, and feet which
resemble those of Jack Johnson, as they would be if
the latter had a couple of immense hands superim-
posed in the section where the toes are—the night-
mare vision which- several prominent citizens of
Monaro have seen, and the hoof-marlcs whereof have
been submitted, in the form of plaster casts, to Pro-
fessor Dav’d. Far be it from me to cast aspersions
on a promising marrow-freeze, but I recall that, in
the Gundary Hills, between Goulburn and Marulan,
some years ago,' a . vast hairy figure, with a bellow
like a fog-horn, frequently put to headlong flight
’possum shooters and other nightly wayfarers. It
has not appeared, curiously enough, since an illicit
distillery was closed down in those parts. Also, in
the Jingera country, the breaking up of a cattle-
duffing syndicate was coincident with the disappear-
ance of a gigantic man-beast, with fur and a deafen-
ing howl and other grisly' appurtenances. I under-
stand that there is an industry near Thoko that would
be all the better, finane'ally and otherwise, for a
wild man or two —the sort of horror that keeps
prying law-abiders at home o’ nights. But, as afore-
said, the last thing I would care to do would be to
shed cold water on any little excitement that is cal-
culated to cheer the dull round of the baclcblocker.

“Sr’s” blood-curdling yarn :
. Old Kelly, next door, goes in for pigeon homing,
The practice, as far as I can make out, is for all
those having pigeons to pay some bloke’s fare into
the country, where he lets the birds adrift. Then,
as each bird gets home to the family loft, the pro-
prietor seizes his fowl and bolts to the winning-post,
being allowed 14sec. for each 100 yards he lives from
the judge’s box,. Last Saturday afternoon Kelly and
his missus had some dispute about the absence of
Kelly’s week’s screw. Mrs. Kelly called Kelly to
order by throwing half the kitchen crockery at him,
and Kelly locked her in the bedroom and poured
scalding language through the keyhole. To the ac-
companiment of Mrs. K.’s hysterical yells, our street
gathered round, and the interfering neighbor
street has an interfering neighbor) sent for the
police. The John arrived when all the trouble was
over and peace had obtained once more in Kelly’s
house. The interfering neighbor, however, inferred
from the silence that Kelly had murdered his. wife.
Just then out dashed Kelly with a woman’s head or
something under his coat, and we all fell over one
another to get out of the murderer’s way. Off sailed
Kelly with the John and a bodyguard after him. At
the corner the interfering neighbor, the John and 14
heavy men fell on Kelly and sat on his chest. I
came along just ip time to hear Kelly finish his
speech: “An’ now, .you blanky blanks, see what you’ve
done! I’ve lost two blanky quid.” His mad dash
was for the judge’s box with his homing pigeon.

“McLevy”: I demand some information.
Up in Queensland shearers invoke Providence for
rain, when it is a fair thing for a holiday, thus:
“Send it down, Hughie.” Thought it was purely
local until I happened on Manly Beach the other
day, when a storm-cloud banked up and a sun-
browning nude arose and remarked to the sky,
“What’s the game, Hughie?” Does any Abo-liar
know the origin of the term ? I believe the Queens-
land black’s word for cloud is something like
“ugon,” and the shearers and surf-bathers may have
misapplied it to the man behind the gun. If that
is not the explanation, what in fury is?”
“A. Ivito”: I have discovered a substi-
tute for the juice of the cow, and at one fell swish
propose to remove the curse of dairying from Aus-
tralia’s children. The cow cocky and his slaving
family will be a thing of the past when I get going,
and the animal that is emptied into a bucket twice
a day will become a mere provider of meat and
leather. It was a sailor at Port Melbourne who told
me about whale’s milk. He had been a whaler, and
knew what he was talking about. The fish juice,
he said, was exceedingly rich —no better butter or
cheese could be desired. Asked how these Straw-
berries and Polevs of the sea were to be leg-roped
and bailed, he said that they were fonder of music
than were snakes, and if a boy played a mouth organ
they would simply roll over and expose their flowing
udders. The rest was easy.
