“Gouger”: Am prepared to back the centi-
pedes of Queensland to outpoint the centipedes of
any other Australian State. There are three species ;
at least, there are three I wot of. All Outback
residents know the Sin. yellow crawler whose bite
induces the same frame of mind as you instantly
fall into when you tread barefoot on the camp-fire.
Nc. 2 fiend is 4in. or sin. long. He has a caress that
is a cross between boiling neuralgia and a vision of
the Spanisli Inquisition. The third specimen is 2in. long,
and wears a black and red jersey. His contribution to a
Pleasant Sunday Afternoon feels something like
swallowing a pint of molten grindstone. The pain
of the bite may last for weeks, and the only cure I
know is not to let the animal, or whatever it is,
bite you.
* * * *
“Campflow” shakes up his weight of
years to remember infantile pranks in the Sydney of
long ago: “I left Sydney as a toddler in the ’so’s,
and returned to it as a raw bush youngster in ’63,
taking up my abode with a McKenzie family in
Kent-street South. Went out with Tom McK., aged
14, the first evening of my stay. He told me I must
be ’nishiated before I would be allowed to play
with the push, but, on further consideration, added
that as I was only a little chap, he might get them
to let me off. ‘I can punch the captain, anyway,’ he
said, and that made me feel good in his company.
It happened that there was another candidate for
admission to the craft that evening—a big, soft
youth from Windsor. It was decided on the vote of
the push that he was large enough to stand a double
share of ’nishiation, and so admit us both. He was
soon introduced to the smellful mystery of King of
the Golden Sword, nearty drowned as King of the
Honey Bees, and chased home amid shrieks of mirth
and derision. After that we played ‘Tallyho’ over
and around the graves of the burial-ground, which
occupied the site of the present Town Hall. A low
wall, surmounted by iron railings, enclosed the place
of tombs, and there was a regular boys’ track
through it from Bathurst-street to Druitt-street. I
remember my final run home that night along
Gieorge-street and down Hell Fire-lane. We banged
at all the doors as we passed, and as I brought up
the rear of the hunt, it was my fortune to get the
tail-end of a string of purple language, accompanied
by something much harder. Said something sent me
several yards without using my feet, and rubbed off
some skin and buttons from my front aspect. I
didn’t stay to gather up the lost articles, but col-
lected what was left intact of me, and sprinted my
best for safety. I wonder where that lane was. It
must have been somewhere near Bathurst-street, and
there should be older Sydney boys than I who can
still remember it.”

“Wandi” : The other day a Pummy Grant
(assisted immigrant) was handed a bridle and told
to catch a horse that was feeding in a small square.
Pummy sneaked up behind the quadruped and clutched
him by the tail to prevent him running away.
Luckily he was a children’s pet, and therefore used
to all manner of indignities. However, Pummy re-
turned in a few minutes without him, whistling un-
concernedly. “Where’s the horse?” his employer
demanded. “Oh, I want another bridle,” said
Pummy. “What’s the matter with that one?” “It
won’t fit by quite two inches. The horse’s head’s
too long.” After a brief but rousing interval, Pummy
was enlightened as to what the buckles on the cheek
straps were for. The mxt day he was told to grease
the buggy. As he was a long while over this job
the boss went to see what he was doing. What he
saw struck him speechless for a moment or two. Then
he spoke to Pummy in a manner that astonished him.
He had greased the whole of the vehicle, including
the seat and cushions.
JIMMYJAMBEROO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
During the past few weeks, the Government Botani-
cal Collector, Mr. J. L. Boorman, has been engaged
collecting specimens of botanical interest in the
Paroo and adjacent districts. He travelled 400 miles,
and made a very comprehensive collection of the
various grasses, fruit and timbers of these parts.
—.4 Bourlce (iV.S.IF.) newspaper item.
And what about the fantods by the Darling’s outer
hanks ?
And the fishes" that are fished for by the hatters and
the cranks?
The jim-jams and deliriums that mercilessly gloat
Before the stony cheque-man’s eyes, and frizzle in his
throat?
What have our friends the horrors done that they
should be ignored
By those scientific searchers, with their coils of
crimson cord ?
From Bourke to Ilungerford, and on to Barringun
and hack,
Are animals that never lived on any other track.
And, bundled all together, what a mixture they
would make! —
The red, and blue, and emerald, and ev’ry kind of
snake;
The centipedes that stand upon their rearmost legs
and talk ;
And those unprecedented worms that fly instead of
walk.
The tall tarantulas that take a fearful furlong stride ;
The lizards that the spiders ev’ry midnight mount
and ride ;
The frogs that stand upon their heads and hideously
croak
A sadder song than any sung by Lawson or by Boake.
Mosquitoes, too, that change to flames as soon as it
is dark ;
The crows that eat the swollen tongue—and quark,
and quark, and quark;
And that queer, gibb’ring ghost that comes, when
night begins to fall,
With icy feet and burning breath —the grimmest ghost
of all!
Who cares for fruit, and grass, and wood?—-ive see
them every day !
The things we really want to see are, those that
went astray
What time the Ark of long ago was resting in the
mud,
And Noah’s eyes were blinded by the scudding of a
scud.
And out beyond the Back o’ Bourke those curious
creatures be—
A weird and unexampled sort of mad menagerie !
They twist and turn ; they freeze and burn ; they roar
as lions do.
In ev’ry shape and size and tint—at Jimmyja'mberoo.

A trade journal, Fairplay, publishes a yarn that
Mr. Rowland Ferrars Pickering, “for many years
‘Nemo’ of Sydney Mail, and whose father founded
Bell s Life in Sydney', has by the death of an inter-
mediate, become next in line for the title of Earl
Ferrars and the £62,000 per annum that the estates
yield. The present earl is 65 years of age, and his
heir, according to my “Burke,” is an architect,
Walter Knight Shirley (family name of Ferrers), of
Victoria-road, Kensington. One Earl Ferrers was
hanged at Tyburn for the murder of his steward in
1760, a rope of silk being used on the occasion. So
much for the earl. Rowley Pickering’s father did not
found Bell's Life in Sydney. It was founded in
1845 by one Joseph Roberts, of Burwood, whose
widow sold to Charles Hamilton Nichols in 1849.
Nichols took George Ferrers Pickering into partner-
ship- , The widow Roberts afterwards became the
vvife of Sir John Lackey, M.L.C. Pickering was mar-
ried to a sister of Eyre Goulburn Ellis, the solicitor
and partner in the old-time firm of Ellis and Makin-
son. He sat in the fifth N.S.W. Parliament, re-
presenting the northern goldfields. He was a good
sport, a _ thorough aristocrat—which was probably
why in his old age he looked upon the wine He is
understood to have died in Fiji. Rowley Pick the
son, was left a fortune by lawyer Ellis, who never
married.
A seismograph placed in the neighborhood
of the late King Edward’s grave would pro-
bably record movements in the coffin. The
corpse must be quite dizzy from turning in
its grave. The memoir by Sydney Lee in
the supplement of “The Dictionary of Nat-
ional Biography” attacked the elaborate pre-
sence that Edward was a “peace-maker.”
Now, in the Fortnightly, Edward Legge
retorts. When the butcher of Servia was
trying to get the Powers to recognise his
murder of Alexander aud Draga as an act
of mere absent-mindedness, Edward refused
on behalf of England. “My job,” he practi-
cally told the ambassadors, “is to be King.
Alexander’s job was also to be King. As
you see, we belonged to the same guild. I
cannot be indifferent to the assassination of
a member of my profession, or. if you like,
a member of my guild. We should be
obliged to shut up our businesses if we, the
kings, considered the assassination of kings
as of no consequence at all.” Edward was a
good trade-unionist. Legge states that
Edward was primarily a business man, a
royal merchant, acting as his own commer-
cial traveller, and “booking orders” right
and left, on his journeys. He disliked the
Kaiser. ; “The personal relations of King
and Kaiser lapsed into comparative calm
only when they were apart from each other.
....The restlessness, the eccentricitv and
the unaccountability of the nephew were a
constant source of irritation to the long-
suffering uncle.” The squabbles are interest-
ing reading.
The Royds, whose homestead is down
Braidwood way (N.S.W.), have a unique
collection of turf trophies won by horses
bred by themselves. Amongst them is a
cup known as the First Australian Prize,
won by Rob Roy, on Hyde Park (Sydney)
racecourse in 1819. The winner was
owned by the grandfather of the late E. M.
Royds, of Balladabra, near Braidwood. The
cup is of silver, and bears this inscription:
“Fortitudine et celeritate,
Pledge from this Cup, the First Australian Prize;
May each revolving year the races bring,
That training horses from these sports may rise.
Health to the patron. Long live the King!
Sydney, May 31, 1819.
Given by the inhabitants to the proprietors
of the winning horse.”
The “poetry” is supposed to be from the
pen of an accomplished rogue, Michael
Massey Robinson. This Robinson, who may
be considered Australia’s pioneer poet, was
an attorney in good practice, transported in
1798 for “an attack on Mr. Oldham.” the
“attack” being merely a threatening letter.
Robinson came out in the ship Barwell in
a special cabin. The same vessel brought
Judge-Advocate Dore, and the pair chummed
on _ the voyage. Dore, glad to have the
assistance of such a lawyer, spoke well of
him to Governor Hunter, who immediately
granted Robinson local emancipation, and
appointed him clerk-associate to the Judge-
Advocate. When Macquarie assumed office
in 1810 he gave him another lift by making
him chief clerk in the Secretary’s office, a
post of some importance.

As soon as a Decent Guy gets on top in
the United States, the press, or his section
of it, sets to work deliberately to convert
him into a Great Man. Woodrow Wilson,
President-elect of the Republic, is now un-
dergoing the process. Already he is a
gigantic figure ; but he is going to be more
magnificent later on, when the painters and
decorators have got their hands in. Some
extracts from a recent article, by C. R.
Macaulay, indicate how the job is being
done: — •
The slight stoop into which he invariablv falls,
when standing or sitting, is not that which comes
from the weight of years. It is a scholastic stoop
—the stoop of the scholar and the thinker. .. .The
posture was well exemplified in the immortal Abra-
ham Lmeoln. .Napoleon had it, as did Frederick
the Great ' and Caesar—three indomitable warriors.
After, that burst, one hardly notices that
Voltaire and Thomas Carlyle are also
rushed into Wilson’s company. Again:—
His eyes are worthy of careful study. They are
fathoms deep, keen and penetrating, but withal
kindly to a degree. They utterly controvert the
popular impression that their owner is cold, un-
sympathetic, and not easily approachable.
Here we witness the installation of the
Great Man’s 40-h.p. heart. The Yankee writer
wandered reverefttly round the immortal.
When I was a boy,” he said, “I had a pas-
sionate fondness for the navy. My great
desire was to enlist in the service and become
a sailor By way of contrast (and this is
probably the one true thing in the whole
boiling), it is mentioned that he loves non-
use rhymes like the “Purple Cow” and
the Walrus and the Carpenter.” When
it became plain that he was the coming
figurehead, 200 or 2000 reporters came and
camped on his lawn to study him closely and
make him Great. Who couldn’t be great
with the help of 20,000 reporters?

“Chasuble”: A thing or two more about
the late eminent Rev. Treacy, founder and
head of the Christian Brothers order in
Australia (B. 7/11/’l2). Born in Tip-
perary, he became a pupil of the Christian
Brothers there. The school was under Bro-
ther Larkin, who had unsuccessfully tried
to found the brotherhood in Sydney. In due
time Treacy entered the order. About 1867,
Archbishop Goold, of Melbourne, sent to the
Superior-General for brothers. The N.S.W.
failure blocked this, until Goold appealed to
the Pope, whose influence caused Bros.
Treacy, Bodkin, Lynch and Nolan to be sent
to Melbourne. Treacy was in charge. They
began at St. Francis’s in 1869 with 200
boys, O’Shanassy was enthusiastic for
them; also Plunkett, of N.S.W., then in
Melbourne. He was determined on extension
to Sydney. A Melbourne meeting put £llOO
in Treacy’s hands. He diligently canvassed
for more, and soon bought from Benjamin
Benjamin, for £3OOO, the splendid little half-
acre in Victoria Parade, on which the Chris-
tian Brothers’ School was built —largely by
indefatigable travel and art union. Treacy’s
other monuments comprise colleges at
Nudgee (Q.), Adelaide and Perth, and the
Novitiate at Strathfield (N.S.W.).
B. K. WISE , K.C.

THE LIBERAL HANDSHAKE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When we shake hands with a Laborit'e, he (’ops
not know that we are feel ins’ his hand to ascertain
whether he is telling the truth or not about his
past life. —Bruce Smith in the Reps.
We clasp their base-born hands with zest;
Their vulgar breasts expand with joy ;
Consuming pride is manifest
Upon their faces. ’Twoukl annoy
The Socialistic dogs to know
The reason why We treat them so.
They think We take their grimy paws
Within Our manicured hands,
And oscillate the same because
We like the owners. How it brands
These Labor upstarts, near and far,
For the conceited hounds they are!
Do Royal Dukes demean themselves
So low as to consort with hinds ?
Does he who loafs with him who delves
Associate? Do master-minds
Collogue with fools for preference?
No! No! The thing's not common-fcense.
And so with Us. Although We seem
Consumed with warmth and love, no less;
Although Our hand-grips fairly teem
With what might pass for friendliness,
’Tis but a fraudulent pretence—-
Each “shake’s” a hunt for Evidence.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

Six weeks to Christmas, six weeks to go,
To the happy holidays, men and maidens know.
Think about your present, think about your dress,
Think about your Christmas fete, and its happiness.
Six weeks to Christmas, only six I vow,
Six weeks will quickly pass, give your orders now.

How much more than £229,629 was made in the
half-year this paper doesn’t know. Anything more
that was made was put into plant or other pro-
perty, and the cost was written off, ostensibly against
the depreciation of other assets. Also, in connection
with the Sugar Commission, “our expenses, which
were considerable, have been written off.” Happy
co. would only pay the whole cost of most expensive
litigation and still show the biggest profit on re-
cord —a profit in excess of 15 p.c. upon the profusely-
watered capital! So far the co. has scored ; in fact,
it has got so into the habit of scoring that, when
there is any public outcry against it and its ways, it
usually says in effect —“Bah ! What do we care for
public outcries ! We make and distribute too much
profit, you think? Well, just to show how much we
care what you think, we’ll divide something extra
this time.” The management has repeatedly pursued
that policy of exasperation; and if it gets a hard
hit one of these days it will have only itself to
thank. They who live by the sword shall die by
the sword. And the investor who doesn’t remember
it is liable to be sorry.
Making the usual few moans at the half-yearly
meeting, chairman lvater, of the Sugar Co., said:
“The only other matter to which I need now allude
is the recent attack on the Gas Light Co. by the
Ministry of this State. The proposal to confiscate
a large part of the shareholders’ assets is one which
all interested in the business life of Australia should
bear in mind, especially when the vote for the
Referendum is to be taken next year.” Quite right.
The Referendum next year is to decide, among other
things, whether certain powers shall be taken from
the State and given to the Commonwealth. Evi-
dently Mr. Kater is now of the very proper opinion
that the Commonwealth should have these powers,
the State Government having proved itself not, in
his opinion, fit to be trusted. It is support for the
Referendum proposals from a quite unexpected quar-
ter. Still, The Bulletin can see that the State
Government has driven Mr. Kater to it.

The ’Asia has mana —which, so far as a bank is con-
cerned, means that when an Australian has no special
or personal reason for taking his business to any
bank in particular, the chances are he will turn into
one of three or four because he has a vague idea
that in some indefinite way they stand at the top
of the tree. Possibly a good deal of this feeling was
born at the time of the great burst, when the public
took note of the fact that the ’Asia, the Union and
the Wales pulled through. Certainly one or two
others didn’t keep anybody waiting on the door-mat
either; but, as subsequent events proved, those two
or three would have been better off if they had hung
things up for a while. Anyway, there is the fact:
the ’Asia has a fine connection, and apparently
doesn’t have to chase good business with a lasso.
It will be noticed that large sums are shown as
written off. These have been mainly in reduction
of the premises account, which stands now at an
utterly absurd £61,006. It is evidently intended to
write this asset down to nothing. Of course it is
really worth a great deal; for there are not only the
banking premises—there are large numbers of hired-
out offices as well. The difference between the book
value and the market value forms an internal re-
serve. In April, 1894, the premises were alleged to
be worth £444,102. Probably half a million or
thereabouts has been put into buildings, etc., since
then, and the market value of the whole lot may be
£750,000. If it is, the ’Asia is plainly £688,994
better off than it claims to be—that is to say, it is
better off by the difference between the true value
and the book value. When a bank adopts a policy
of hard writing down, and at one point where the
matter can be probed is stronger even than it ap-
pears to be on the surface, the public naturallv
takes most of the rest for granted. Hence, in part,
the mana before mentioned.
As the bank has large assets which it does not
show, its earnings in proportion to capital and visible
reserves combined are naturally very high:—
Profits in Proportion to Shareholders’ Funds.
Oct., 1907 £l2 9 sp.c. I Apl., 1910 £ll 12 lOp.c.

Oct., 1908 11 16 9 „ I Apl., 1911 11 11 B’’
Apl., 1909 10 15 10 ~ I Oct., 1911 11 16 11 „
Oct., 1909 10 16 9 „ I Apl., 1912 11 16 2 „
And this high rate of ean. is not accompanied
by excessive risks, the result of excessive liabilities:
it has a shade less than £6 of deposits, etc., for
every £1 of its own funds (capital and visible re-
serves). The principal movements in the past 12
months are set out next: —
April, ’ll. April, ’l2.
Notes .. £515,846 £210,441 Dec. £305,405
Bills, etc. . . 2,836,033 2,887,447 Inc. 51,414

The bank has had heavy calls made upon it during
the year, and, in the absence of new deposit money,
its liquid assets have been cut into pretty deeply.
Nevertheless, at the end of April, 37 p.c. of the
liabilities were still represented by liquid assets,
which is a trifle better than the average holding,
though a good deal less than this bank has been sit-
ting behind lately—another proof of the fact that
even this exceptionally strong institution isn’t in a
position to make any large amount of advances at
present, and that the yarn about the banks hanging
on to vast sums of idle money is all rubbish.

Vic. State Coal Mine (Wonthaggi)
This enterprise borrowed £150,000 from the
Treasury for a start, and by June 30 last owed the
State another £11,605; but meantime £7050 had
been paid back, leaving a net liability of £154,555.
Practically all the money was put into plant and
development. The land belonged to the State: it is
not valued at anything, and apparently is not ex-
pected to give any return. The profit shown for
the two years the mine has been worked is as fol-
lows :—-
Written
Z off for To Sink- Total
Depreciation, ing Fund. Profit.
1911 .. .. £17,052 £7,050 £24,102
1912 . . . . 1,892 7,942 9,834
Total .. £18,944 £14,992 £33,936
The actual depreciation during the past year was
estimated at £10,709 ; so, reckoning that as a fair
charge, there was an actual loss of £875 on the last
year’s operations. The poor result is due to labor
troubles —which, of course, should never have occurred
in State employ—insufficient capital expenditure,
etc.
An entirely wrong principle is adopted in connec-
tion with the sinking fund. The accumulation of
money, with the help of compound interest, is one
of the essential features of such a fund. In this
case the idea apparently is to use the money to pay
off debt. That sounds all right; but it isn’t. An
enterprise borrows say £150,000 to buy mine or
other machinery. A depreciation fund is established
to replace machinery as it is worn out. A sinking
fund is also established to pay off the debt. Now, it
is a fair thing, the machinery having cost £150,000,
to ask it to go on paying interest on £150,000.
There is no reason why in the second vear it should
pay interest on only £140,000. Yet that is all it is
asked to do if the sinking fund contribution is
used year by year to reduce the debt. If £7500 is
used to pay off debt year by year, obviously it will
take 20 years to wipe the thing out; whereas if the
sinking fund is accumulated at 4 p.c. compound in-
terest, it will take only 14 years and some months.
The plan adopted is the lazy way—the way of
shuffling out of a little bit of interest every year,
though the machinery (a renewal fund being pro-
vided) ought to be earning quite as much interes 4 '.
etc., in the 19th year as it was in the first year. Instead
of certifying such accounts as correct, it ; s time some
Auditor-General told the truth about them, and re-
norted that the alleged sinking fund was a sinking
fraud.

There is a trifle to be shorn off the profits for the
past year and paid away in bonuses to employees.
The Bulletin won’t swear to the figures for previous
years, though any adjustments that might have to
be made would be immaterial. The trouble has been
that hitherto no printed balance-sheet had been
issued, and the information otherwise available was
fragmentary. The lack of information was due to
the peculiar circumstances under which the co. was
formed. There were three partners in the old firm
of Finney, Isles and Co. in its latter days—Tom
Finney and two juniors. The present co. was the out-
come of the struggle of the juniors to retain a hold on
a concern in which they had 5 tons llcwt. of faith.
To make matters as easy as possible the Queensland
Trustees, Finney’s executor, left close-on £50,000 in
the business, on certain conditions. They weren’t
bad conditions; still, the directors thought it well
to get their head out of this bail while the State was
affluent: hence the new canital issue (at 2s. pre-
mium) last year. The premium, less exes., is added
to the reserves.
Nearly £70,000 was thus got in, and less than
£25,000 was needed to remove the last of the Finney
plaster. The balance has gone partly into stock and
partly into still further additions to premises. This
shows the sprightly movement of this co. in detail :• —
1905. 1909. 1910. 1911. 1912.
Capital .. .. £29,462 £66,392 £67,293 £85,193 £151,993
Debt toFinnev’s 42,704 30,507 27,458 24,407
Other liabilities 60,962 111,674 133,795 126,898 128,798
Stock .. .. 60,878 82,106 89,119 106,601 136,940
Real property.. 7,665 58,266 84,332 87,671 109,078
Rook debts .. 25,550 43,876 45,122 51,631 54,874
Other assets .. 25,648 43,588 34,649 31,535 29,129
That is pretty swift going ; and if there are any old
stagers among the shareholders, they have probably
lost their breath long ago. The co. can’t, however,
be justly accused of over-trading. Seven years ago
its capital was less than one-half of its liabilities;
now the capital is nearly 20 p.c. greater than the
liabilities. And whereas there were less than no re-
serves seven years ago, there are very substantial
ones to-day. Of course, this all means that the
shareholders have put up a lot of money. But it also
means that the shareholder’s £1 hasn’t got to take
anything like the risk. it had to seven years ago. For
another thing, £97,572, or two-thirds of the capital,
is in freeholds, apart from fixtures, etc. No doubt
freehold values slump ; still, a block in the heart of
a city is more of a gilt-edged security than a con-
signment of chiffons. For which reason shareholders
have no right to expect that the present capital will
make profits at anything like the same rate as the
capital of a few years back. As to profit, by the
way, last year's figures were shrunk by the Badger
trouble. Queensland is a place very liable to trouble,
and trouble means erratic profits." Prior to the past
year things had been going with a quite unusual
swing: wherefore this paper isn’t at all convinced
that the profits of Queensland enterprises for those
years were normal. The energetic Finney-Isles man-
agement has, however, so many new agencies for
whinpiqg up profit that a receding tide probably
wouldn’t carry the figures very far from their recent
average.

Harts, Ltd., caterers, etc., of Sydney :■ —-
Written To Re- De-
Profit. Dividends. Off. serves, ficit.
Dec., 1909.. £11,700
Dec., 1910.. £5296 7p.c. = £2BOl £2OOO £495 11,205
Further goodwill items, £2s ! ' .. .. 11,455
June, 1911.. 4169 8 p.c. = 2000 1800 369 11,086
Dec., 1911.. 2625 8,, ~ 2000 200 425 10,661
June, 1912.. 4568 8 ~ ~ 2000 2000 56S 10,093
The public got its first small look into this business
in 1910, when printed accounts were issued for the
first time, the object being, admittedly, to encourage
the subscription of some outside money. At that
time the capital was £40,007 in £1 shares. The
other 9993 shares were then issued at par, the man-
aging director, Philip Hart, guaranteeing an aver-
age 7 p.c. div. up to the end of 1913, and backing
his guarantee by a deposit of 20,000 shares in the
co. The £11,700 deficit with which the table starts
was the amount paid (in shares, apparently) for
the goodwill of the business. That, of course, is a
paper asset pure and simple. There is another of
very much the same character —“Leases, £7000.”
There is, of course, nothing whatever to show for
that amount. The vendors, it seems, made contracts
with certain landlords, or certain other lessees who
had the right to sub-let; and those vendors think (or
thought in 1910) that they had got £7OOO the best of
the bargains—that is to say, that somebody else
would pay them £7OOO more than they paid them-
selves. Possibly. The Bulletin doesn’t know.
But it is a very doubtful asset, anyhow. Another
paper asset dropped into the accounts after the new
issue of shares, “Flotation exes., less £125 written
off, £125.” Subsequently this other £125 disap-
peared, and this paper has had to surmise that it
was written off out of profits. The large amounts
shown in the table as written off are to cover de-
preciation—a pretty large matter where cups and
saucers are concerned. This was the position as at
June 30:—
Stock, cash and book debts, less liabilities.. £4,573
Plant, furniture and machinery (less depre-
ciation reserves) . . . . . 21,019
Paper assets (goodwill and leases) . . . . 19,245
Leasehold improvements . . . . .. 8,769
Total assets .. .. .. ..£53,606
The assets in the first line are good, in the second
line fair; the others are doubtful. The position,
therefore, is not brilliant. Profits, certainly, have been
satisfactory enough, though a very great deal de-
pends upon whether the right amount has been writ-
ten off to cover smashes, chips and other deprecia-
tion.
* * * *
The Co-operative Assurance Co., Ltd.
(Sydney).—Registered in July, 1911, this co. began
business in October. The first year’s figures of an
insurance concern are generally pretty shuddersome,
and these don’t come to blows with the rule. Things
ran out like this :—-
Preliminary and extension exes. . . £4,654
Other expenses . . . . . . 11,971
Death claims . . . . . . 104
Total outgoings .. .. ..£16,729
Premiums . . . . . . . . £3SSO
Interest. . . . . . . . . . 861
Total income . . . . . . . . 4,441
Deficiency for the year . . . . £12,288
Add £3OOO, the book value of 6000 shares issued to
the promoters, and you have a present deficit of
£15,288. That is to say, of the capital of £38,894
the foregoing amount is not represented by assets
that are visible to anything except the eye of faith.
More than that, it has to be noted that the share-
holders’ capital has been earning interest; and
under ordinary circumstances the shareholders
would therefore want a dividend—that in-
terest, in short, with a little bit added for
luck. Also, claims are exceedingly small during
the first year or so of a co.’s operations, because all
policies are new, and a medical examination, while
it won’t protect a man against tuberculosis four or
five years afterwards, should at least save a society
from admitting a man who has the marks of early
death on him. So, one way and another, while it is
inevitable that an insurance office’s expenses during
the first year or two must be abnormally large, it
is equally clear that the outgoings in claims should
be abnormally miall. Things are not so altogether
one-sided as the conductors of young offices generally
try to make out.
“Preliminary and establishment exes.” are lumped
into one heap. This is objectionable. Prelim, exes.,
including the promoters’ £3OOO, ought to be one
item ; establishment exes., if included as an asset at
all (and it is a very poor asset), ought to be separ-
ated. Also, it is most undesirable that all general
exes. —directors’ fees, salaries, commissions, etc.—
should be bundled together. Not only shareholders,
but policy-holders as well, are entitled to know what
directors’ fees and medical-referee fees, for example,
run to. If this information isn’t given, intending
policy-holders can only assume that it isn’t satisfac-
tory enough to give ; and where there is even a doubt,
the intending policy-holder will unquestionably go
to one of the older and proved offices —provided, of
course, he regards insurance as an ordinary business
matter. Admittedly, there are thousands of people
who don’t.
The co. is in 500,000 shares, 128,240 of which have
been issued ; 16,035 of them are fully paid and the
balance are paid to 6s. At least, calls have been
made up to 6s. Policy-holders are entitled to regard
this money as a guarantee (as far as it will go) of
the satisfaction of all claims —that is to say, that
the widow will get paid when the policy-holder dies.
A purely mutual office, without share capital, cannot,
of course, offer any such guarantees. On the other
hand, the public has to remember that the investor
doesn’t risk his money for nothing, and is going to
presently share in the profits, if there are any. That
doesn’t necessarily mean, however, that policy-holders
will get smaller bonuses. The theory of the thing is
that, having a direct interest in making profits, the
shareholders will run the business on stricter prin-
ciples, and that the policy-holders will thereby gain
more than they lose through having to share profits.
The new office is pushing the idea of house-purchase
in conjunction with life assurance. It is new to
Australia, though it has been worked satisfactorily
elsewhere; and, given economical management, it
deserves encouragement here.

This co. owns the East Greta freehold and Stanford
Merthyr leasehold coal mines, and the fairly exten-
sive East Greta railway; and one way and another
it is on about as good a wicket as any mining con-
cern on the Newcastle and Maitland field. Of late
years it has wrapped up its figures in such a way
that very little good can be got out of them. What,
however, is known is that it was at one time (and
probablv still is) so conservatively managed that the
book value of the mining properties was written down
to almost nothing. Five years ago, before the smoth-
ering policy was introduced, the East Greta mine
stood in the accounts at only £10,499, and the Stan-

This is another of the long-distance undertaking
establishments of Melbourne. The funeral of the
old boom has lasted for nearly 20 years, and the
turning of the last sod seems a long way off yet. It
was the old.trouble —not only was the shareholders’
money advanced on securities at ridiculously inflated
value's, but a big sum of deposit and other public
money went the same way. The values were so
inflated, in fact, that the time soon came when, in
order to make ends meet, the creditors had to forego
most of their interest. In 1894 they consented (it
was practically liooson’s choice) to take 24 n. 0.,
and, so far as this paper knows, that is the thin
skillv they have been feeding on ever since. The
Profit and Loss account doesn’t show the outgoings
in interest; the net interest earnings are shown —
presumably after deduction of interest paid. Last
year these earnings amounted to £6401, and after
paying for management and upkeep of property, etc.,
the society was £604 to the bad. In addition to
that there was the usual loss on properties sold;
that is to say, thev didn’t fetch what they stand at
in the books. This shows the progress of the
funeral: —•
1898. 1910. 1911. 1912.

Thus between 1898 and j•) 12—14 years —the liabili-
ties have been reduced from £339,205 to £150,203,
or by £189,002. Meantime the assets have been
reduced by £237,912. So it has taken £237,912
of assets (book value) to pay off £189,002 of debt;
and there still remains £150,203 of debt to pay off.
At that rate it will take approximately £190,000 of
assets (book value) to clear the remaining creditors
off the premises. The assets now stand at £341,724,
so on the foregoing reckoning there would be
£151,724 left for the shareholders; and on the values
realised during the past 14 years that lot should be
worth a trifle over £120,000. But this paper won’t
swear that the assets now in hand are worth as much
in the £ of their book values as the assets that have
gone. Recent realisations have certainly not been
up to the average, in spite of the booinsome condi-
tions of the past few years. Meantime, of course,
the shareholders are getting no interest; and the
£120,000, if it ever materialises, isn’t worth
£120,000 if it is only payable an indefinite number
of years hence. Already most of the weary share-
holders are 70 or thereabouts, and the prospect of
waiting an indefinite number of years doesn’t entice
them. Various hints have been given from time to
time that the management would be a bit more
popular if it let some daylight in, especially with
regard to the nature and real value of the remaining
assets; and there is some hone that, during the
coming year, a step, or two will be taken that wav.
There are 46,298 shares (paid to £5) in the con-
cern, and probably some of the people who own them
are still able to remember when the trifles were worth
£8 apiece.
A. H. Chambers has been moved up to
the job of chief inspector of the Union Bank, with
headquarters in Melbourne. The Union is, in some
ways, the most English bank in Australia ; it doesn’t
keep even a local director on the Commonwealth
premises. This means large responsibilities for the
inspectors and other senior officers; and the results
are the best proof that they have done their work
astonishingly well. Mr. Chambers’s beat has lately
covered N. S. Wales and Queensland, with head-
quarters in Sydney.
A heartening circular has been issued by
Australian Marine Fibres, Ltd. —the concern which
is dredging a partially-decayed seaweed from the
bed of Spencer’s Gulf (S.A.), and hopes to sell it
at great profit to foreign spinners. The plant, it is
said, is now in operation, sample bales of fibre have
been distributed, and a first consignment of 20
bales has been sent to London for auction. Opti-
mistic letters are published—the shareholders have
had nothing else to live on so far. Now comes the
test —what will the market really pay for the stuff?
The official talk of offers hitherto made appears to
have been all moonshine.
* * * *
Tingha Consolidated consists of a num-
ber of Stannifer and Copeton (N.S.W.) claims that
were tied up into a bundle and put under Mel-
bourne management six years ago. It has never
done much good for itself or anybody else; in fact,
the claims would probably have got along better as
they were. Now, however, the co. has got hold of
a new broom, and this general manager, Jas. Symes,
promises to atone for the lean years by a series of
fat ones. The directors bow down humbly and
worship the new god, and incidentally inform the
shareholders that they are not fit to hold their job a
day longer. For instance: “Another very important
matter initiated by the general manager is that of
providing adequate water for the plants. For the
first time in our history this problem has been fairly
faced, and is being solved.” Directors who, after
six years, admit that dreadful omission ought to be
too much ashamed of themselves to face the share-
holders without offering their resignations. Under
the new control they don’t think it too much to
expect a shilling div. on the 200,000 shares this
current year. Neither does The Bulletin ; only,
beforp making any prophecies, it would prefer to
see whether the new broom can sweep as well as talk.

Prosuects in the Eleanora mine. Hillgrove
(N.S.W.), are on the improve, and if the N.E. end
of No. 8 level continues to show stone assaying over
loz. per ton for a width of 34in., the mine will be
anything but the rank duffer it was thought to be
when the New Hillgrove 00. gave it best. Still, the
Eleanora has been capricious in the past, and the
recovery of the gold has been difficult because of the
large quantity of base mineral in the stone. The
management, by the way, appears to have blind faith
in assays, for no trial crushings are reported from
development work.

* * * *
The mines at Bodangora (N.S.W.) are
wearing a careworn expression. The battery is run-
ning two shifts, with 15 head dropping. The Nos.
3 4 and 5 levels are apparently worked out, and
the ore in sight at the No. 6 (lowest) level will
last only a few weeks. So wild is the effort to keep
the mill running that the truckers are developing a
Donaldson stride. Simon Jenkin, who has put up
a big fight against overwhelming odds, begged to
be supplied with compressors and drills to get down
and open up levels, and keep development ahead.
The directors blew cold and supplied a boiler and a
splendid cement foundation, but nary a drill. In
disgust Jenkin threw in the sponge, and leaves this
month. Macintyre (erstwhile commander of the
same show when Peter Mannell owned it) picks up
the reins again. # *
Vegetable Creek, Emmaville (N.S.W.),
seems unable to bear the burden of the 25 p.c. royalty
which it carries. Although now and again affairs
look hopeful, a policy of vigorous development is
impossible, mainly because of the drain of this 2 o
p.c. of the proceeds. Chandler’s shaft has proved a
big disappointment, but the look of things at the
Rock shaft has improved somewhat. The lead should
be intersected at Allen’s shaft shortly after Christ-
mas, at a point where good tin was located by pre-
liminary prospecting. Altogether, the length of lead
opened "has been comparatively very small, though the
average results over the whole are fair; but until
more working faces are opened, so that the effect of
the blank areas can be counteracted, it seems that
the co. will have merely a hand-to-mouth existence,
with possibly an occasional threepenny call thrown
in to keep the pot boiling.
A small co., the Y Water Extended, has
been formed to prospect the deeper portion of the
Graveyard Deep Lead, Emmaville (N.S.W.). The
only cash payment is £lOO to the vendors. From
its situation at the lower end of the lead on which
the Y Water Tin Co. is working so successfully, and
from local geological indications, this property should
justify the good opinion held of it by those inter-
ested. Some work was done on the area (320 acres)
in the early da.ys of the field ; but although results
were encouraging, local lack of capital . prevented
further development. The present co. intends to
thoroughly prospect the area, and then, if results
warrant it, float into a larger co.
Pulganbar Quicksilver Co. (N.S.W.) re-
ports that it is making fair progress with the erec-
tion of a reducing plant, of which, by the way, no
description has yet been given, in California the
metallurgy of quicksilver has been greatly improved
in recent years, and the use of shaft furnaces
equipped with condensing chambers is now pretty
well universal, the old type of distilling plant being
used only for experimental work and for special
kinds of'ore. At the Pulganbar two levels at 70ft.
and 140 ft. have proved cinnabar, copper sulphides
and fahl ore; but the reports are destitute of
assays. The one thing the manager is quite confi-
dent about is the supply of ore, though what really
matters is the supply of metal in the ore.

* * * *
With its usual assurance, Murchison
Associated reports for the 13 months ended June that
the rich ore discovered on the surface of its hitherto
unprofitable Westralian property did not hold down.
As a result of recent work in other parts of the
show, a “considerable quantity” of payable stuff has
been exposed, and instructions have been given to
treat it and some old tailings on hand without dela'c
The heavy loss sustained at Bullfinch is deplored,
but the directors blame the other fellow. In this
case much of the blame is fastened on to the ex-
Premier of Westralia, Frank Wilson, because in a
burst of optimism at the opening of Parliament, he
said that the field bade fair to eclipse anything dis-
covered in the State. Surely, however, the Board, of
the Murchison Ass. did not part up before sending
an expert along to look into things. The co. now
depends mostly on rubber plantations in Ceylon.
These assets are declared to be sound, and available
funds are said to be sufficient to take the co. to the
dividend stage without-falling by-the way. *
Field-geologist Blatchford, who is cred-
ited in the West with knowing what he is talking
about, expresses his oninion of the Wodgina tin field
to this effect: (1) Numerous tin-bearing lodes of
commercial value exist in and about the field; (2)
with one exception, none of these lodes has been
proved deeper than 25ft. ; (3) the field has every
appearance of permanency ; (4) the one mine which
has been worked on a large scale has produced
£26,000 worth of tin; (5) as there is no encourage-
ment offered to prospect for low-grade ore, only
pockets of stone rich enough to treat by hand on the
spot are being sought after; (6) high transport
charges, absence of crushing facilities and scarcity
of water are hindering the development of the field.
He recommends the establishment of State-owned
and State-aided concentrating and crushing plants.
Filtration troubles and a temporary
shortage of water have for the time being upset
Associated Northern at Ora Banda (W.A.). No diffi-
culty is anticipated in righting things, though for
the present the tonnage will be a bit less than the
estimated capacity of the plant—which, by the way,
is 9000, and not 12,000 tons, as previously stated.
* * * *
Greenfinch (W.A.) struggles on. As a
result of the last half-year’s work the profit and loss
account was reduced by £1146. Still, all that
the co. owed on September 30 was £538,
against which there were cash assets totalling
£1251, machinery and stores £3155 (which must
be taken for what it is worth), and trifles (lease
option, etc.) £BOO. The co.’s purchase of the
optioned lease has been hung up owing to disputed
ownership, which the Westralian Courts are now about
to decide. Among other belongings which the co.
claims are 2300 tons of sands, of an estimated re-
coverable value of £l6OO, and 400 tons of sta
sulphide ore, worth about £3 per ton. Of oxidised
ore, about 400 tons are estimated to remain above
the 100 ft. level, and 1500 tons are expected to be
won above the No. 2 level. The reef is small and
the ground hard in the bottom, but so far values
have proved payable. The outlook, however, is not
yet such as to make the shares worth much, it worth
buying at all.
Bullrush (W.A.) has paid over the pur-
chase money for the Royal Standard at Yum, and
the task of proving the show to be as good as repre-
sented to shareholders has begun. In July last the
management “assumed” the monthly profit when
treatment was in full swing at £2200 per month,
or £26,400 per year—a profit which if capitalised
over a period of five years at 4 per cent, would
make a value equal to £105,600, or Is. 6d. per
share. Returns, however, haven’t so far come
within cooee of this. As regards the Kyarra, which
at the same time was estimated to add another 4s.
on to the potential value of Bullrushes, a report
has just come to hand that the mill is nearing
completion, and that crushing will start next month.
* * * *
During October, Mount Morgan secured
an average furnace extraction of 2.9 p.c. copper and
7.7Bdwt. gold from 29,316 tons ore. The 853 tons
of copper recovered at £6O is worth £51,180, and
the 11,4220 z. of gold, at 84s. per oz., is worth
£47,972; total, £99,152, or £3 7s. 7d. per ton of
ore. It is the best extraction of gold this year, and
suggests that the management is using more barren
ironstone, brought from near Kabra, in order to smelt
more silicious ore. Another move has been made to
obtain a supply of anthracite coal (80 p.c. carbon)
by taking an option over the Mammoth colliery. Pro-
duction at the big mine has been steadily improving
under the new conditions, and the output for five
months of the current half-year totals 4248 tons of
copper, containing 48,6650 z. gold. The recent average
return has been 2.9 p.c. copper, and if that average
is maintained it will require as much as 343,000 tons
of ore to produce 10,000 tons of copper. Luckily,
according to the latest estimate, there are 3,526,000
tons in reserve ; but the value of such a bulky propo-
sition is largely a guess. Also, with satisfactory
smelting practice, reserves are increased from 20
p.c. to 30 p.c. by the use of pvritic fluxes; and
counting these, the total is 4,231,200 tons.
Brilliant Extended, Charters Towers, was
£1728 to' the good for October. Four months have
given a profit of £4323 on working account. Un-
fortunately the co. was making losses before July,
so some time must elapse before the cumbersome
quarter-million concern recovers its leeway. For nine
months it crushed 26,200 tons for £48,008, which
is the largest quantity of stone from one mine on the
field. The worst feature is that the deepest, levels
have become lightly mineralised, and the sinking of
the shaft below No. 20 doesn’t hold out much pro-
mise, for the stone is white and poor.
* * * *
Brilliant Deeps, Charters Towers, has
crushed 2618 tons of stone this year for £17,341, an
average of £6 12s. sd. per ton. Practically all the
profit, together with the proceeds of 25,000 new
shares at 75., has gone to purchase and erect the
new haulage plant and develop the mine. The half-
year closed with a small overdraft, and this, added
to the decline in the grade of stone, caused the
slump in shares. The last crushing of 558 tons re-
turned £6 os. 2d. per ton.
* * * *
Charters Towers has reason to remember
the Black Jack boom in the ’Bo’s. The records
show that 10,951 tons of stone were crushed for
19,2260 z. But the pay shoot was lost, and the
Peter and Jack has been trying for three years to
pick it up. The co. has been following a leader
branching off into the hanging wall, and latterly
has got from £4 to £5 per ton from it, though it
is still small. So far operations are in the develop-
ment stage, but there is quite enough exposed to
build a hope upon. The claim is six miles from the
Towers, and the optimists are already regarding it
as almost another Stockholm.
Mount Elliott (Q.) produced 607 tons
copper in October, following 630 tons in September,
680 tons in August and 718 tons in July. The
total for a run of four months has been 2640 tons
copper, 46700 z. gold, 49220 z. silver, from 22,511
tons of ore. The average extraction works out at
11.71 p.c. copper, 4.l4dwt. gold and 4.7dwt. silver.
Having rather a large number of prospecting ven-
tures in hand, it is not surprising that shares con-
tinue high. The market is always inclined to be
in a good humor with a concern paying big divi-
dends. The gamble is periodically refreshed by the
announcement that a new mine has been acquired
or that a strike of rich ore has been made. The
latest is a bore-hole development at the Native Com-
panion. Boring at an angle of 80deg., the drill
penetrated 16ft. of ore from 43ft. to 59ft. —and some
rich assays have been obtained. The value of this
lens of ore will be determined by a crosscut, for
the find isn’t far from the shaft. Previous bores
disclosed nothing of value. The locality is noted
for rich patches.
•** * *
According to official figures the Great
Fitzroy (Q.) is just about paying its way, if
copper realises the estimated value put upon it.
Last month revenue was estimated at £16,853, and
costs amounted to £15,376 —-to which must be
added management exes. During four months the
surplus on working account was £7060, from 650
tons of copper, 36330 z. gold and 11,0200 z. silver.
With copper selling at £BO, and with £2O per ton of
gold and silver in the red metal, conditions are
favorable; but costs swallow all the copper value
and half the precious metals. No wonder the Fitz-
roy is looking for something new. P. Osborne _is
superintending operations at the Laloki mine
(Papua). Assays run over 2 p.c. copper and 2dwt.
gold for a width of 40ft. The stuff is highly
pvritic.
The rich little St. Mungo (Q.) had an
October output of 156 tons, worth 23.2 p.c. of cop-
per. The Charters Towers shareholders are rejoicing
in a new make at the North No. 3 level; it has come
in at right angles to the contact lode. They are
only into it about 4ft., but at that point there is a
face showing 3-Jft. of clean ore. This is north of
any of the co.’s other workings.
* * * *
At Rossville (N.Q.) the tin-scratchers
are installing hydraulic plants, and between times
praying that the approaching rainy season will
hurry. Many of them will do well with plenty
of water, up-to-date appliances, and high prices.
Horton, from down South, has lately been boring
claims on Mount Hartley and Mount Romeo, with a
view to purchase; but so far the plant has not
proved any big-depth proposition, though it has
prodded some splendid surface shows. The best
show so far proved in the district is the Annan
River Co.’s, where one face runs to a depth of over
70ft. and carries good tin all through. The Home
Rule is working three shifts, but the last few
clean-ups have not been brilliant. This claim has
also been feeling the want of moisture, though its
water right is one of the best in the district. How-
ever, the annual thunderstorms have started, so it
should be wet enough presently.
Mine-engineer Horton has been looking out for a
decent tin proposition in the North; but he ap-
pears to have given the job best, believing that
very rarely does the North Queensland tin live
down. It was the Annam River Co. that took his
eye and led him to that district. During its life-
time (about seven years) this eo. has gradually
bought up claims and taken up leases until now it
is looking a pretty big thing. The highest price it
has yet given for a single claim was A6OOO for one
held by four men; and it looks like a good bar-
gain. Since the start, the co. has spent over
£16,000 cutting water races, etc. One race alone
cost over £lO,OOO, and covers a distance of seven
miles, part of it over fearfully rough mountain
gorges. Over 80,000 feet of cedar and kauri were
used up in this little job alone.
The once-famous Lode Hill has fallen on evil
days, and there is a struggle to maintain even the
present reduced output.
Prince Alfred tin mine (N.Q.) has lately
crushed at Gurrumbah 200 tons of ore for 26 tons
of concentrates worth £3OOO. This means a glit-
tering £l5 per ton of ore. But a lot of the gilt
is oft the gingerbread before the owners get it. lor
one thing, the ore has to be packed to the battery,
and the concentrates then have to be packed six
miles to rail. And, unless this shoot_ is different
from every other in North Queensland, it will prove
to be short. As to depth, it goes down 90ft. for
certain. The present co. gave Seaman, the former
owner, £4OOO in cash and £3OOO in shares for the
show. #
Blair Atliol (Q.) turned out 37,095 tons
of coal last half-year. After writing off deprecia-
tion and making what looks like a very fair expendi-
ture on renewals, a profit of £3937 was shown. As
a good deal of development work is still in progress,
it was, however, possible to pay only one 6d. div.
(£1875). By thus limiting divs., the financial posi-
tion has been kept quite satisfactory. There certainly
was an overdraft of £2444 on Sept. 30, and the co.
owed £2OO besides; but stores and book debts are
put down as worth £4423.
* * * *
The Pompeii (N.Q.), owned by the Irv-
inebank Co., was discovered in 1911, and a glance at
the monthly tin returns of this year show it to be
a consistent milker, often eclipsing the staid old
Vulcan. During September it gave 12 1 tons, worth
£1125 The Vulcan. during the same time treated
518 tons for £969. But what Pomp, will do when
it has been going as long as the Vulcan is quite
another matter.
It has cost £17,000 and 25 weary years
to drive a tunnel into the hills at the back of Bright
(Vic.), to pick up the old Oriental shoot of ore
that put many a bright sovereign into old J. A.
Wallace’s wide pockets. Most of the contributors
were a poor crowd that found halfpenny calls big
enough to wrestle with. Some stuck on and others
came in as the tired dropped out or as children were
born At last, when they had got half-way through
the mountain, the Mines Department had to deter-
mine whether the tunnel had dodged the shoot. A
fresh scheme of work was suggested, and driving has
gone on of late on a body of buck quartz picked up
in a crosscut to the south. Now a hymn of praise
is being sung because an enrichment is being proved
which dollies up to 12cwt. Bright patters out
that the 1500 ft. overhead is all virgin country, but
what is wanted is more driving and more capital to
bump up rises to see if the virgin ground carries the
precious metal. # #
Garden Gully United is the chap with the
big record at Bendigo. Like Johnson’s Reef, it struck
at last a bad attack of chills, and so it has been
standing neglected in the corner. During the latter
period it has been poking about m the hope of get-
ting better things at 1500 ft. in its main shaft, and
it has also found time to explore a formation,
which for some reason had dodged previous atten-
tion. Perhaps the richness of the big saddle over-
head was responsible for that neglect. Still, it is
rather choice to hear that a mere 2 ft. make of
stone, representing the new find, has impressed the
investors.” A good bit of prospecting will have to
be done between the mam and the south shafts to
see if this little stranger lives at that point. The
mine staff is, however, so certain of its presence that
thev count on a nice little run of divs. to swell the
mine’s big total. To leaven things up still more,
connection is being made with the Pandora workings
the idea being to chase after some allegedly good stuff
left by a tribute party. It isn t often that the Ben-
digonian, when on tribute, lets profits remain in the
face so it would be unwise to count on settling bets
from anything won from this part of the mine until
a new block has been opened up.
* * *
A piece of paper flutters out from the
Mount Jasper office. The co. holds a block of copper-
bearing country near the Hazelwood River (Tas.), as
well as the old Mount Stewart mine. Frank Powell
visited the claim and cold-watered the shows, especi-
ally turning the tap on to the new Jasper find.
S nee Powell’s inspection, the work done on the new
Jasper outcrop is said by the directors to indicate
that the best of mining experts can t see any further
into rock than any other man. Shafts, they say, have
given good copper prospects. Hence the circular and
hence the news that the co. is, in the near future
to despatch regular parcels of copper ore from the
lode. Unfortunately, the Board s diplomatic note
doesn’t mention the quantities to be shipped monthly.
With North Lyell shut up and Mount Balfour as
misty as the 'morning sun, something is certainly
needed to fill up the gaps in the copper record of
the State.
*** . .
Mount Lveil’s record is a brilliant one, for
shareholders have been paid £2,766,000 from prac-
tically two mines, whose total production has be®
120,000 tons of copper. The old co produced 48,000
tons copper, 4,120,0000 z. silver and 155,0000 z gold.
Taking gold at £4 and silver at 2s , the value m
these metals is £1,032,000. No wonder the old con-
cern gave big dividends, for its copper contained over
£2l worth of gold and silver per ton. In recent
years the copper grade has fallen, and to make mat-
ters worse the copper only contains about £l3 wortn
of precious metals. Still, for a period costs were
very low—almost the lowest in the world. And, in
spite of the cloud, the end isn’t here yet, though the
glory has departed. Notwithstanding the North Lyell
fire ’ which it is pretty certain now can only be con-
quered by flooding, two furnaces are smelting.
* * * *
During October the Australian-owned
Tongkah Harbor (Siam) produced 871 tons tin oxide
from 276,800 cubic yards of washdirt. The average
was 0 7061 b. per cubic yard. Tin oxide has been
realising high prices, but the stuff in Siam is sub-
ject to royalty. The washdirt is fully 25 p.c. less
in metal value than the previous half-year’s In that
term 1,778,5601 b. of oxide were got from 1,647,634
cubic yards. There may be other areas that will
average lib. per yard, but the rapid decline m the
value” of the dirt isn’t very comforting. When the
No 6 dredge is in commission, the total capacity
will be 4,000,000 cubic yards a year. The harbor
bottom may be able to supply that output and main-
tain a fib. grade; but even to this day there is no
certainty about the extent and value of the wash.
The success of the Tongkah has been re-
sponsible for many things, including the Hindu
Choung Co. This is a Burmese tin-dredging con-
cern which Melbourne people were induced to buy
early last year. The price was £65,000 in shares
and £2OOO in cash; and another £lOOO is to come
out of profits. The co. is in 108,090 shares, the
vendors’ lot fully paid, the others paid to 13s. As
usual there was much delay in getting a move on,
and the co. still has a swag of forfeited shares on
hand, the owners having got tired even before the
dredge made a start. The area is un-get-at-able; it is
30-odd miles from port, over a dreadful track, and
much of the plant had to be dragged over the
mountains by elephants. The co. has an area of
5 sq. miles under prospecting license; but s .° fal j
the ground has been worth only about one-third of
lib. per cubic yard, and that hasn’t paid working
exes, plus incidentals. The latter may ease off, and
the manager says he thinks the value will improve;
so there may be something to the good presently.
On present appearances, however, an absurd price
was paid for the property.
* * * *
Sanpliiretown (Q.) still occasionally does
something. The other day, James Peterson, a sap-
phire miner at the New Hush, got 210 oz. of sap-
phires and corundums from five loads of wash-dirt.
Nowadays the low-grade stone is also finding a
market, and is allegedly used for mechanical pur-
poses. Previously, this kind of gem was mainly un-
employed, and rarely found a new owner except
when the raw tourist poked into the field.

LETTER
Sydney, November 11, 1912.
My Dear Arini.—
A Sydney friend who pranced through
the recent frenzy of Horse week in Mel-
bourne, falls over all the best-natured ad-
jectives in her efforts to describe the Den-
mans’ Red Ball. About -uiOO souls and
bodies spent an hour or more creeping at
a snail’s pace through the gates of Govern-
ment House in car or humble hack-chariot.
Sydney women held their own in the fierce
competition of the frocks, ivrrs. Tony Hor-
dern, Mrs. Alf Turnbull, Mrs. Leonard
Dangar and Mrs. Harry Levy were in their
best. Mrs. Donald Macintyre’s diamond
crown and necklet were only second to the
blaze of diamonds worn by Lady Denman.
The Grey-Egerton lady was a good third
with her brow encased in a 10,000 candle-
power diamond illumination.
Sydney’s Gaud Mayor Clarke shook a
blithesome foot at the Red Ball, having
ventured forth with no municipal uphol-
stery to wear him down, as he did at the
Denmans’ last Rose Ball in this town.
Concerning the froth of rumor that bub-
bled round Lady Denman’s alleged edict
against the osprey, one trembling guest
cowered in the cloak room, afraid
to show her head because it had
an egret for its main theme. She
was severely warned by the usual know-
all acquaintance that ospreys . were for-
bidden fowl in the presence of her Ex.
A Sydney friend who observed the tragedy
that followed says that Samson losing his
locks to the shears of Delilah was a mere
circumstance to the execution of the osprey
tail with a borrowed pair of scissors. After
the sacrifice had left the lady with about a
foot less coiffure and a half-mourning expres-
sion, an attendant dashed forward to ex-
plain that the whole story of the osprey
edict was a plain, unvarnished lie, though
the G.-Generaless doesn’t wear the fluff
herself.
The sodden weather that chased the Cup
gown to a close hiding place under water-
proof armor, drove one dressy Sydney dame
to a state of mild dress-tremens before the
Denmans’ garden party. She registered a
solemn oath that come rain, hail or mud, she
would garden-party in fine-weather drapery.
When the early-mormng tea-cup jingled at
the lady’s door, she bounded to the window
and discovered that a Reckitt’s blue sky had
arrived overhead. In the sudden outpour-
ing of a grateful heart, she presented the
handmaiden with a tip so large that even
that experienced damsel is suspected of
having bitten it in private to test its worth.
Invitations have been posted for a farewell
hop in the vice-regal nest at Rose Bay. The
date is early next week—“or thereabouts,”
as Jessie Lonnen says. As the Chelmsford
luggage is not ready for the hold of an out-
going steamer by a long way, the State
Governor and his household are presumably
on the wing for their summer shelter at
Sutton Forest. When they make a per-
manent descent, the illuminated address and
the local cleric with a sob in his throat,
will be waiting on the wharf, or in some
adjacent barn.
How translucent the past often seems by
the light of the present! AVasn’t it a year
or 15 months ago that the Stricklands were
packed up for a determined departure to
London. The good-bye bun had been eaten
to its last crumb in W.A.—and then the bag-
gage was unstrapped again. It looks as if
the key lies in the surmise that Governor
Lhelmsford may have given notice as far
back as the middle of 1911, and that the
Stricklands got enough of a hint to take
their household gods out of brown paper and
remain on the premises.
Governors are moving round Australasia
like chess pawns. The knight Strickland
soon moves east, and when the time comes,
feir Harry Barron will sit in the vice-
regal chair at Perth. Barron seems to be
only just working into the charmed circle
ot professional Governors. He hadn’t even
rUled ,.i? ve ™ a tropic monsoon till he took
over the Tasmanian billet'. He is a gunner
by early training, and, oddly enough, he
mows Haifa (where he commanded the
R.A. from 1900 to 1909) as well as does
his predecessor at Perth. Since 1909 he
has been followed by the National Anthem
in the Apple Island.
