The white-cotton gloves of the Law, in all
its deep-blue majesty, are likely to clutch
the runaway husband with a firmer grip, if
the inter-State Maintenance Orders Bill be-
comes legal machinery in N.S.W. Attorney-
General Holman has planked this measure
down in the electric atmosphere of the As-
sembly. If a husband throws domestic
bricks at his wife, or gives her a black eye
in haste, he will have to pay for it with
steady maintenance, if the damages are laid
before a sympathetic court. With a reason-
able cause of complaint, any woman who
finds matrimony full of lurid language and
male fist, can apply for the custody of her
youngsters (under the age of 11) and get
a judicial separation with a satisfactory
crust.
Her Majesty’s Theatre has had its face
washed and garnished with fresh paint and
extras. If it is white—as in the dress-
ing-room, where you put the last dab of
powder on your nose —it is merely paint,
and smells as fresh as Tom Sawyer’s fence.
But where it is gold, it is gold leaf, and looks
like escaped bullion. Buff, brown, ivory and
gold, with white as a relief, give the theatre
a new and dashing appearance, and make it
look a satisfactory home for grand opera.
The seats are peacock-blue, and soft as the
nest of that showy fowl. The curtain,
which ought to have repeated the cheerful
blue of the seats, has digressed into pale-
brown plush, a color that never seems to go
with plush. But. in spite of that, the whole
place is so rejuvenated that it looks exactly
a fortnight old.
William Mcßae, who comes from North
Queensland to step into the shoes recently
worn by Denison Miller in the Bank of N. S.
Wales, is said to be a Personality of versa-
tile attainments. He’s a bachelor on the
sunny side of 50 —a good judge of a horse,
who owns a persuasive tongue, independent
means, a. profound knowledge of Shakspeare,
and a Bismarck chin. For four years past
he has been the big bank’s inspector in the
sun-kissed North; before that he was in
charge of the Fiji end of the same bank’s
affairs, and sat in the Legislative Council
of the tropic isle in his off time. I haven’t
heard whether he plays cricket better than
Trumper, or wishes to challenge Hop to a
tournament of bowls. These details may
drift in later on.
Talking of bank changes, Melbourne is to
get our Union Bank Chambers—not the big,
bright, freestone premises that overlook the
ancient and dingy S. M. Herald office, but
the head of the business in N. S. Wales, and
his wife. Mr. Chambers becomes the bank’s
chief inspector. He, too, gained much of his
bank experience in Queensland. His wife
was a Miss Ferguson, a daughter of John,
who was a Senator in Australia’s first Par-
liament. Also, more to the point, perhaps, so
far as his family’s comfort goes, the Hon.
John was in the original Mount Morgan syn-
dicate. I don’t think Mr. Chambers is
guilty of anything much except hard work
and a sunny disposition.
Squatter George Yivers, of Kings Plains
station (N.S.W.), hurried back from Europe
the other day. His business only holds him
here a few weeks, and includes a look at his
1!)12 wool clip. Later, he rushes off to Cairo,
where the pretty Mrs. George lingers by the
ancient Nile, in new Paquin frocks.
Miss Maud Watson, of this town, is stay-
ing with the Vivers lady till her tall wool-
pack steams back to her side.
Alfred Marks, a son of the late John
Marks, M.L.C., recently arrived here, after
an absence of many years from his native
State. His permanent roof is in England.
An only chick, a tall, slim girl of 16, is fin-
ishing her flapper days in a Swiss academy.
She is another Australian-born damsel who
threatens to be a beauty, and she is likely to
have her first grown-up waltz in Sydney.
Mrs. Hugh Ward, recently back from a
visit to U.S., hurried off to her native town,
Pittsburg, to be present at her father’s
birthday party. The blithe veteran cheer-
fully acknowledged 80 summers, but looked
up his birth certificate to make sure. He
found he was four years older than he meant
to be, but remained as young as he felt,
which was somewhere in the mellow sixties.
Mrs. Ward had a look-in at the leading
musical centres in U.S., and reports their
steady climb to a higher ledge. In the
teaching hives the best masters are snatched
from Europe’s chief fountains of harmony,
to rap the knuckles of Miss ’Murka if she
doesn’t smite the right notes first time of
guessing.
Prof. Macmillan Brown, of Maoriland,
recently sprang ashore here, after a
trip through the East —-which is north. He is
suave and dapper, and there is no trace of
the schoolmaster about his holiday remarks.
On the voyage from Java the learned
Brown occasionally dipped into the origin
of “classy” names that were getting their
sea-legs on board, and he generally effer-
vesced with joyous anecdotes. His hobby
is Polynesia and its coffee-colored races.
Fred Leist, Tom Roberts, Fullwood and
Arthur Streeton are included in the list of
oversea Australians who exhibit at the So-
ciety of Artists’ Show this year. Nov. 20
is the opening day.
Dorothea Baird, having wisely decided to
give up the stage, recently spoke about the
profession to the National Union of Women
Workers at Oxford. She hit one rather
elderly nail on the head by referring to the
stage as the only occupation that gives
women an equal opportunity with men. A
novel sells for the stuff that is in it; and the
fact that some of the financial plums of
recent literary competitions in London fell
into the feminine lap has missed the eye of
the H. B. Irving lady. Also, the woman
doctor is no longer penalised bv sex. At
least, half a dozen other occupations, once
regarded as a male monopoly, now lift up
the latch to the woman who. knocks long
and loud enough. But, after pecking thusfar
at Dorothea’s plumage, one gladly hands
her a posy for a sensible postscript about
Australia. “There is plenty of occupation
there; but teachers, typists and clerks are
not wanted so much as women who are well-
trained in domestic science.”-
Mrs. Claud McKay, who used to be Doro-
thy Sidney on the stage, is bubbling over
with impressions of life behind the scenes in
London. She recently returned to this town
after an IS months’ trip abroad. Her obser-
vations are worth noting, as the Sidney
family have been mumming since the frivo-
lous days of Charles 11. and his curly-
wigged Court. Mrs. McKay saw a large
gulf between the work of the actors of live
years ago and the affectations they cultivate
to-day. Mostly she blames the influence of
the dramatic schools and the “society”
actor or actress, an elegant clothes-peg
much seen in West End theatres.
“Australia is too young and fresh to
stand the style of players who now mono-
polise the cast of J. M. Barrie’s plays, for
instance. But the jaded West Ender sees some-
thin2', almost new in this pose of artificial
simplicity in its elderly stage favorites. A
rehearsal,” says Mrs. McKay, “of a Barrie
play is as full of this odd pose, which pre-
tends to be very young, as the public per-
formances. Between their cues the stars
lie about, or sit on the ground, as young-
sters might. The talk is all of ‘feeling’ and
‘atmosphere,’ and no one is surprised to see
an old-young actor strike his forehead with
one hand, and rush to the other side of the
stage to finish his lines. ‘I couldn’t feel it
over there! I must get into the atmosphere
further down the stage,’ is the usual ex-
planation.” The desire to feel a handy
weapon attacks the actor who is not of the
society-pet order so fiercely that he has to
bind himself over to keep the peace.
Mrs. Pitt, a_ Victorian writer of verse, is
paying a hurried visit to this town. Her
metre is full of sympathy for the man with
the hoe, and the woman who controls the
weekly wash, minds the baby, cooks the
food and darns the family socks at, or about,
the same time.
Nella Webb, now appearing with loud
applause at the Tivoli, will have given a
tea-party at the Australia before this reaches
you. The hostess requested “the pleasure
of your presence” on notepaper of unusual
decorative glare with a gold beast clawing
the air on a field of red sealing-wax.
Frances Hodgkins, a Maoriland girl who
has made her name easily recognisable in art
circles of France and Britain, dropped into
Australia from London a few days ago with
some samples. Miss Hodgkins gets the ar-
tistic sense from her father, who has left
proofs of his ability on the walls of both
Melbourne and Sydney Galleries. For the
past seven years she has been painting in
Paris, and has exhibited regularly at the
Salon and Royal Academy. About the end
of this month or the beginning of December
Melbourne folk will be given an opportunity
to judge the lady’s work for themselves.
In “The Quaker Girl” Grace Palotta
(Madame Blum) experiments with the
panier skirt in a silk garment shot with the
bloom of a purple plum. It is as near per-
fection as this baggy drape can get. Palotta
wears her clothes with an air (or a some-
thing) that the average woman can’t grow
in her wardrobe. Still. I noticeu that even
the Viennese actress came nearer to a
waddle in this gown than one believed pos-
sible to her. A slit of inserted lace at one
side of the skirt gave the shot
garment a tendency to sag in the front
breadth. This crime is common to the
hurried skirt of the mere toiler, but is
mostly foreign to the Palotta drapery. The
lady came into her own in the ball scene,
with a foot or so of black and white osprey
on her head, and a fairy film of white and
black gown into which, I presume, she had
been carefully poured by her dresser.
The Women’s Pioneer Club in Sydney was
founded by Miss Christine Spark with much
hard labor and a generous output of per-
sonal cash. So the club, which is nominally
founded on ancestral bones and other his-
toric dust, rose up last week to mark its
sense of hon. sec. Christine’s breathless
efforts to serve up a sound financial state-
ment. A bag with a snug lining of sove-
reigns, the whole surrounded by a glorified
tea-fight, was dedicated to the lady’s resolve
to hand over the hon. sec.’s quill. The Hope
Johnston Pioneer Club, which slams the
door on anyone who can’t swear to at least
one authentic early-Australian skeleton in
his family locker, was born after the lady
Pioneers were in full bloom. Hence the
female descendants of successful politicians,
squatters and other early birds who caught
the primeval worm in this State vrit their
teeth when they tack the prefix “women”
before their club-name.
The Overseas Club, of which Dr. Arthur
is president, met on Saturday evening to
discuss the question of finding a permanent
roof. Also, it looked into the inside of its
cashbox. This club consists of about 1800
members, so that it moves with more legs
than any centipede mentioned this side of the
Flood. Some of its members haven’t moved
so rapidly in the matter of paying up their
subscriptions, however. One daring spirit
suggested that Speaker Willis should be
resorted to as a possible president, if finances
and other nuisances didn’t improve. Amidst
a whirl of dissent, a still, calm voice (like
the voice of Conscience before it whoops
over the new hat you couldn’t afford)
pierced the argument with. a persistent:
“But you don’t know the kind of man he
really is! Willis is all right!’'
The recent scoop of the Sydney railway-
station thief, who got away with a Mosman
dame’s Cup luggage, ought to straighten up
a few loose methods in our cloak-room man-
agement. This baggage was sent in to al-
leged safe keeping in the morning. At three
o’clock in the afternoon it was signed for
by an audacious gent., who affixed his
signature to the necessary scroll as Dr.
Blank—the lady’s husband. Exit one of
the most extensive Cup wardrobes of the
week, with valuable peltry,_ for Melbourne’s
frequent November chill included in the
cargo. To atone for the heart-ach'" he
caused, the thief, if ever he should be caught,
ought to be condemned to wear four dif-
ferent race frocks every day for a week, each
frock to be hooked (by himself) up the back
with 90 hooks and several dozen dress clips.
A postcard of Ella Caspers, the Goul-
burn contralto, came to hand the other day.
It shows a plump profile, and is a cheery
revised edition of the delicate-looking Ella
who left these parts.
Manly is plotting the details of its Janu-
ary water-carnival, which is to spread over
a week from the 11th of that summery
month. Alderman R. B. Paterson is already
in the throes of profound thought over the
neck-to-knee surf events, the Venetian de-
corations, and the hire of every Manlv chair
that leans back on firm legs. Last year’s
splash only occupied most of one day, and
a slice of the subsequent starlight. Among
other attractions this year, the pride of the
marine village, the champion amateur band,
which knocked the wind out of all competi-
tors in a recent contest, will chase the fly-
ing hours with its brass instruments.
“Milestones,” the new play by Knoblanch
and Arnold Bennett, is going to give our
girls a treat in December, by bringing back
to Sydney the manly form and unrivalled
calves of Julius Knight. Its clothes will
drift from the flounces of the ’6o’s to the
present skimpy swathe of ninon. The play
is ingenious, yet easy to handle, from the
stage-carpenter’s point of view. Everything
occurs in one room, in three acts. As the
human Milestones pass through the adven-
tures that career round three generations of
flesh, they modernise in clothes and man-
ners. The room, also, works its way
from the heavy legs of mid-Victorian furni-
ture to the frivolous lounge that supports
the 20th century spine.
Avoca, once the Darling Point home-nest
of the Bowker family, is said to have
changed hands for about £4OOO. In view
of the drought in houses, the new owner
means to build a few in the Avoca grounds.
On Friday next, one end of the new
duplicate cable to Maoriland will be
officially dragged through a Bondi breaker,
in the presence of a large assembly of poli-
ticians and Important persons. The Sil-
vertown, a veteran cable craft, ought to
strike this coast to-day (Monday), after a
good-weather voyage from London via the
Cape. The new cable end is likely to be
met by a full-grown band, for the test
house at Bondi was built on land set apart
for a park, and it grew a bandstand before
a test house was raised there. When the
wire has been well and truly dragged ashore,
the Silvertown will head for Auckland. The
event at Bondi will be christened with
champagne at an official lunch.
The Women’s Handicrafts Show is torn
open this week by the Matthew Henry Ste-
phen lady. Mrs. Murray Prior and Miss
Bundock will hand the welcorfie bun to all
comers on November 14. The Cullen lady
now presides over the cogitations of this
society.
May Allah keep your Cup full of happi-
ness.—Yours,
VANDORIAN.
“G. 8.”: Permit me to arise and smite
Governor-General Denman on his bump of
vandalism. New South Wales waratahs
were used by the ton to deck his dancing
saloon on Cup night. The ordinary destruc-
tion of the bush glory is quite sufficient to
promise its early extinction, and the effects
of a senseless and wide-spread ravage will
be disastrous to next year’s blossoming. In-
deed, flower-trade experts fear that there
will be a paucity of waratah blossoms in
New South Wales for years to come. Action
had to be taken to check the danger to the
indigenous flora brought about by suburban
and country wild-flower shows, and when
vice-regality roots the waratah up in tons
to pleasure snobocracy for an hour or two I
desire to say that the time is ripe to express
disapproval to vice-regality with the most
effective, not to say violent, instrument on
the premises.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, November 11, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The cat that has been half drowned is
chary of the water. Derby day having
proved a shower-bath, the social cats and
kittens looked at the watery Cup day sky
and decided, with bleak mee-yows, to cut
out the day’s strutting. The real sporting
woman who declines to be the slave of her
frocks went out early in last year’s coat
and skirt, and defied the heavens to do their
worst; but the mere clothes-carrier waited
slyly and hopefully until the time drew
near for the great gallop. The hoped-
for sunburst miraculously arrived, and the
clothes brigade rapidly made ready for the
gorgeous parade which was to enrage the
plainly-garbed females, and drive them into
the darkest corners of the grandstand. Just
after the Hurdle Race, motor after motor
rushed up to the terrace steps and deposited
birds of paradise—and of the other place.
Every butterfly vision was smiling mali-
ciously at her cleverness. But, as soon as
they had preened themselves a little, down
came the rain again, and the enraged strate-
gists had to charge desperately at the
already-packed stands and claw piteously
for shelter. It was a fearful trick for the
weather to play.
State Governor Fuller and his navy-blue
lady arrived early on the scene. Auntie
I’hipps, clad in black and with a saucy
feather in her hat, had all the sporting
Helps around her. At noon, the Denmans
trundled along in state, assisted by bewigged
servitors. Her Ex., wrapped in a wonder-
ful ermine coat, defied the weather with the
unconcern of a Persian kitten safe on a
drawing-room cushion. His Ex. was buried
in an overcoat with a huge astrachan collar.
The Bosanquet twins were in thick creamy
clothing. Miss Ogilvie looked over their
heads with her mischief-making grey eyes,
and showed a hat set at a cute angle. The
Chelmsford lady wore peacock-green velvet
patterns on her crepe-de-chine gown. Mrs.
Godley carried black raiment in a distin-
guished way. The various Helps were all
top-hatted, and shone like city pavements
on a wet evening.
Mayor Clarke, of Sydney, and Deputy
Town Clerk Layton joined the top-hat brigade
at the vice-regal box. Melbourne’s new Mayor
Hennessy was another who was glossy as
regards the hat; but ex-Gaud Davey re-
lapsed into soft headaear. Mrs. Davey, with
blue feathers rampant, gave a lunch party
under the vines. Mrs. Richard Casey, in
blue serge, talked cheerfully of her coming
trip to India. She is to stay with the Car-
michaels. Mrs. Pat Osborne, buried in a
fur coat, met most of her friends between
showers. She is shortly going to England
to visit sister Mrs. Vallange. Mrs. Tony
Hordern veiled a bird of paradise in her
hat, and wore green silk suitings. Mrs.
Peter Mitchell hid a satin suit under the
newest thing in fur capes, but showed a few
inches of black silk ankle. Her Cup frocks
are unwrapped dreams waiting for a suit-
able function and bright weather.
.Mrs. Willie Kelly looked like a neat mag-
pie with broad white marks on her sable
suit. Edwin Geach, of Sydney, enjoyed the
sport in a huge raincoat, then bolted to catch
the Adelaide express. The English tennis
champions paraded the latest cut in London
clothes. J. D. Williams bemoaned the
weather as loudly as any woman; it
prevented him wearing his Cup suit
made in America. I understand it is
a beautiful thing of checks, with swollen
or inflamed shoulders. Miss Williams and
Miss Bell left their race frocks unpacked,
and made two of the wise coat-and-skirt
army. Mrs. Bowes Kelly, with her own
hands, trimmed a wet-day hat in 10 minutes,
and left her elaborate feathered headgear in
its box. She was dressed nattily in blue
from top to toe.
The radiant weather of Steeplechase Day
hatched out joyous myriads of Flemington
butterflies. Every scrap of color that had
been packed away earlier in the week was
taken out and given a last glorious airing.
The rain had fled for good, leaving the
lawns and roses fresh and lovely beneath a
blue sky. Lunch parties were jolly affairs.
The Agar Wynnes, who had a Cider win
later in the day, included Milnes, Hills and
other Sydney people who had been good 1,0
them during the Randwick season. Mrs.
Geoff Syme, in an adorable rose-trimmed
hat, hostessed another gathering. The Grey-
Smiths and the Maddens chattered away over
a game pie; and “Dewar” Theil and his
brother, with their respective wives, urged
Melbourne friends to eat and drink. The
vice-regal box was packed with a choice
assortment of governing folk. (I am drag-
ging them in at the tail-end by way of
change.) Pretty girls hid themselves under
the new Quaker bonnets, which only give
a tantalising glimpse of the nape of the
collarless neck.
The Red Ball, with which the Denmans
celebrated the Cup, was attended by an
astonishingly big crowd. Either there are
more of the Best People in Melbourne this
year, or else the visitors’ list put on a
special train for the occasion. The ballroom
was packed. A quarter of the dancing space
was roped off for the Two-Steppers. It
irresistibly recalled the horse ring at an
agricultural show.
The slender Denman lady was gowned
angelically in clinging white satin. A lace
scarf, golden with age and fragile as a cob-
web, hung loosely round her shoulders, and
the diamond stars in her hair were about
777. The Fuller lady wore the blue of an
Australian summer sky. It was a flimsy
transparency that showed golden iris glit-
tering on the brocade. Miss Ogilvie, the
Sydney beauty-girl, was kept busy refusing
dances and sighing prettily over a filled card.
Bigjohn Forrest managed to crowd himself
into his Court uniform. The struggle is
obviously becoming desperate. Mrs. George
Chirnside looked like a grim fashion-plate
in pinkish purple. A trimly respectful
osprey stood at attention on the mathemati-
cal centre of her head. Daughter Audrey
whirled decorously under mamma’s eye.
Mrs. Stewart McArthur did her leopard
turn again in the lovely skin dress. Mrs.
Geoffrey Syme pitted her brilliant com-
plexion against leaf-green chiffon brocade
and wore beautiful lace. Jewels are not
mentioned above a whisper in the G. Syme
household since the burglary, and the lady
wore only a quart of so of diamonds. One
woman looked as if she would be strangled
by her diamond collar before the evening’s
exertion was over. I don’t know if she
weathered the last dance, but she was bear-
ing up when I last saw her before supper.
Admiral Creswell wore an assortment of
decorations on his broad chest, and danced
all the evening. Would that there were
more sailors! Margaret Cooper, with hus-
band Humble-Crofts shadowing her devotedly,
blazed in a dress of many-colored tulles.
Purple and blue and red films hung over
gold satin lent the star a suitable mixed-
limelight effect. Mrs. Captain Waters, in
a zebra-striped dress, bemoaned the absence
of sister Mrs. Waugh, who was seriously
ill in Sydney.
The interior of Farlei.gh House was more
or less hidden behind cartloads of fruit and
flowers for Mrs. Charlie Fairbairn’s dance.
In the handy little corners there were ripe
cherries hanging overhead, and whilst the
man talked sentiment, the girl, as a rule,
munched the pretty means to indigestion.
The black-garbed hostess wore a satisfied
smile, whilst the two buds, Katherine Simp-
son and Hope Osborne, clung close to her,
and formed a white and lemon-colored re-
lief. Esther, the tawny-haired daughter of
the house, was encased in an Oriental dress,
in which green predominated. The Barttelot
dame brought Alicia and Beatrice Bosan-
quet. Mrs. Chauvel, in blue, was under the
care of her gorgeous colonel. Others who
gyrated, or sat and gossiped, were Mrs. Pat
Osborne,, the Hunter Landales, the Ronald
Murrays, the numerous Clarke clan, and a
trainload or so of the best Sydney people.
Thoughtful Mrs. Charles anticipated the
usual breakfast hour and fed the jaded
guests on bacon and eggs before day-dawn.
Polo made a tempestuous start on Wed-
nesday of last week. The Denman lady in-
vited motorists to make a party for the run-
out to Kooyong, and the Government House
drive was lined with a double row of cars
when she glided down to the gate in an open
machine. Trams and other contemptible
traffic stood still while an unbroken mile of
whirring cars sped across the tracks. Cham-
berlain Nevill balanced precariously on the
back of the vice-regal chariot, which also
contained the Barttelot dame. The G.-G.
himself had gone out earlier in the day, ac-
companied by Help Nutting. Quilter and
Barttelot were also on the scene early, and
struggled into weatherbeaten polo duds. The
rain was in close attendance; so frocky
spectators kept to their travelling shelters,
which formed a great circle round the play-
ing area, and looked like a row of opera
boxes. The mud flew merrily from the
ponies’ hoofs, and a camera girl snapped the
G.-G. as he strode to cover spattered from
white cap to boot-tip.
The Denman lady, in a brown rain-coat
and snug white hat, got away early to pre-
pare for the hostessing of a dinner and
dance. She backed her car out of the line,
and tried the nerves of several shovers by
just grazing their timbers. At last, after
ennobling a young oak tree by bumping it
with the back wheels, she whirred off home.
Two vice-regal garden parties rounded off
the lively week. The Fullers, getting in
early with their strawberry spree, drew Dust
and Sudden Rain in the great weather lot-
tery. Women and men rushed pell-mell for
shelter like disturbed rabbits and stampeded
poultry. But, huddle as they might, the
doors were opened only to a favored few.
Now the furious outsiders want to know
why they weren’t sheltered in the scullerv. or
somewhere. A number would have been
quite content to share the kennel with the
vice-regal dog until the storm was over.
The Denmans’ party was blessed bv a re-
spectful Providence with the first dry day of
the season, and the female guests put on
their very best garments and strutted de-
lightedly.
The Sydneysiders and Maorilanders chat-
tered about the. engagement of Miss Vida
Mills and Mr. Jim Burns. It is a maritime
contract, in a manner of speaking. She is
the. daughter of Mr. David Mills, of the
Union Steamship Co., and his father is
Colonel Burns, of Burns, Philp and Co.
He is in the ancestral business, and with
diligence, punctuality, despatch, the flight
of years and a few other things, should
succeed to the position now held by his
male parent.
The Australian Club Ball, on Friday
night, helped to bring the bewildering week
to a brilliant finish. The big marble stair-
case was canopied with red roses and shaded
with rose-pink lights. The wrap-muffled
women were drafted up strange back stairs,
and directed to their cloak-rooms ; then, hav-
ing powdered their noses, they were care-
fully marshalled and told to rejoin their
escorts by way of the centre staircase. It
was excellent stage management. The ball-
room was so crowded that the early dances
were just good-natured jostling. “At any
rate, no one can fall in this crush!” re-
marked one member philosophically. Presi-
dent “Jimmy” McLaughlin received the Gov-
ernment House people, and helped them take
supper. The Denman lady wore a startling
pearl and diamond tiara. Lady Fuller was
a dream of a fair woman in blue. Miss
Phipps' looked smart and sportful in black.
Aimee Clarke, wearing baby side-curls, was
quaintly pretty. Mamma Grey-Egerton was
nearly strangled in diamonds. Strangula-
tion by diamonds is one of the privileges of
the very rich. Miss Ogilvie was a vision in
lace. Little Miss Inglis-Hudson and Miss
Fuller, both of Sydney, were conspicuously
pretty in a roomful of beauty. Kathleen
McPherson-Knox was one of Melbourne’s
best representatives, and young Mrs. Geoff
Syme another.
Councillor D. Y. Hennessy was elevated
to the Mayoral rabbit-skin, and invested
with the city’s hereditary dust problem, last
Saturday. The reign commenced, as usual,
with a great deal of food and drink, and
about four columns of loose eloquence. Mrs.
Hennessy was permitted to invite lady
friends to gaze on the ruins of the banquet,
listen to the talk, and absorb music and
coffee. It sounds thoroughly depressing, but
some, at least, of the guests enjoyed it.
Our own Victor Hood is hurrying back to
the occasional sunshine of this land. He has
had secretarial jobs in most of the Australian
States, and is now to stand at the elbow of
Strickland, the new Ma State Excellency.
Private Secretary Vernon, the black-
haired, black-eyed prop of the Denman
household, is receiving congratulations on
his engagement to an English girl. He goes
off early in the coming year to claim her
and step into a comfortable official post
provided by the good British Government.
Coal baron William Brown, owner of
Piastre, was not at the club to receive the
Cup and congratulations, some important
lawsuit having drawn him away to Eng-
land. Friend Inglis spoke a few words on
behalf of the absent conqueror, who is a very
smart and well-liked man about town, a
great pal of Rupert Clarke’s, and a fine
matrimonial catch who gracefully avoids
being caught.
It is alleged that the coal-owning Browns
patched up their differences just before Mel-
bourne Cup. Both of them tipped Piastre
to win in the event of Duke Foote being
beaten, the only difference in the opinions
of the brethren being that one of them didn’t
think the Duke could lose.
Mrs. Consul Bouvet, a lady with a creamy
magnolia complexion and a fascinating
figure, managed to drift languidly back into
the social whirlpool half-way through Cup
week. She has been in a private hospital.
The Bullfinch Doolettes added to their
little pile by a £2OOO win with Maltravers,
on Oaks Day. Mrs. Doolette, garbed in
white, hurried round and arranged a small
impromptu dinner at the Savoy, where the
win was quietly champagned.
Mrs. Collins, of Auckland, one-time a
dashing McCarron maiden, of Melbourne,
has been carrying some of the smartest
dresses to social victory during the Cup-
ping. She has just returned from foreign
parts, and is well supplied with “dreams.”
Miss Brierley, who came to this country
under the wing of Mrs. Fink, joins in the
fun.
“Rosna” writes from London:—
Editha Le Hunte, for a long time S. Australia’s
only vice-regal chick, was married here the other
day to Godfrey Pease, lately private sec. to his
Excellency of the West Indies. Her father left
Trinidad to govern itself for a month, and came
over to give her away; and two of the, four brides-
maidens were imported from Adelaide for the occa-
sion. They were a Milne damsel and the Bakewells’
iolly daughter Valerie. It was an autumn-tinted
knotting. The bridesmaids were visions of maize-
color and roses, with mob cans and veils of golden
net. The pages were likewise golden clad; so was
the reception-room; so was the bride in her going-
away chiffons. She’s a fair-haired, pretty slin of a
girl, and well-adorned the white charmeuse wedding
robe, with its wealth of ancient lace. South Austra-
lian names were spread largely over the wedding
gifts, for the Le Iluntes have kept in touch with
the Slowly City to a degree precious rare among
ex-Exes.
Local dignitaries, political and otherwise,
looked as rigid as the waratahs upon the
walls when Chamberlain Nevill beckoned
the visiting Grey-Egerton dame into the
official lancers and left them out in the
cold. _ The Grey-Egerton baronet was not
on view; but his lady sparkled enough for
two, being like Bret Harte’s famous heroine,
“bediamonded out of all reason.” By the
way, tiaras were so frequent and free that
night as to be hardly impressive at all.
Writes my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Otranto brought us yet another cleric—the
Rev. H. R. Cavalier. He is under contract to per-
form—-I mean discourse —at the Anglican Cathedral.
The holy place has neither endowments nor pew-
rents, and so it has to rely on what the ungodly
vaudevillain terms, I believe, “the strength of its
Bill” to attract a congregation. Of course there
was no local talent available. There never is in
clerical affairs.
Dr. Holtze, of the Botanic Gardens, has been
in Melbourne, Sydney and Brisbane, searching for
tips and things. As a critic he is wary. “All the
gardens of the other States,” says he, “are good—
in some way.” But on reading between the lines
it seems that the sloping lawns and the cool shadows
of the Melbourne Gardens have pleased him most.
Mrs. Prosser, who for five years has been Mayoress
of Norwood, was entertained at tea on Wednesday
by the feminine ratepayers (and non-ratepayers) of
Norwood. They also gave her a diamond ring. E. T.
Smith, who has himself been Mayor of Norwood more
than once, handed over the glittering goods, together
with the usual glittering remarks.
The Children’s Hospital is to be enlarged. Like
the rest of our private enterprise healing-establish-
ments, its financial position is none too good. The
£40,000 which the public has given to it in the
last 12 years has been mopped up like water.
At the beginning of the year Mr. John Warren
went out of politics for good. On Monday morning
he was presented with a portrait of himself, by
some of his constituents and his former colleagues.
He is 82, yet proposes shortly to undergo an opera-
tion for cataract. Such gameness merits not a single
portrait merely, but an entire picture gallery. The
Duncan knight made the presentation, and announced
that the portrait would exhibited in the Art
Gallery before being officially taken over to its
subject.
Mrs. Fearon gave a dance to about 70 young people
at Netherby on Wednesday evening.
The picture shows have removed themselves from
the Town Hall for the summer, and concert cos. and
choral societies are tumbling over one another to
secure dates. The Adelaide Choral Society led the
way with a good performance of “The Creation,” at
which Mr. Eugene Alderman’s orchestra scored a
win.
Scots gathered in force (and kilts) for the Hallow-
e’en celebrations. Mr. and Mrs. Wemyss and the
Peter Woods were the joint entertainers, but illness
kept Mr. Wemyss away. Bagpipes, supper, spor-
rans, Highland flings, brose, haggis, siller and other
Scottish luxuries were rife.
The South Australian Yacht Club struck bad
weather for its opening jollifications on Saturday.
The morning was a flood, and the afternoon a ty-
phoon. The seafarers were as cheery as was possible
in the disastrous circumstances.
The Glenelg Bowling Club declared itself open
for the season on the same awful day. The bowlers
were even merrier than the mariners. The bowler
has always to his hand a priceless prophylactic (in
divers brands) against the fury of the elements.
Mrs. Bob Rvmill has gone to Penola for the sum-
mer, and the Teesdale Smiths are installed at Mount
Lofty.
The first polo match of the season occurred at
Plvmpton on Saturday.
Guy Fawkes Dav bad one small resurrection, which
did the Children’s Hospital a bit of good. Small
convalescents stationed themselves at the dif-
ferent gates with “guys,” and begged for pennies—-
their cash value at the end of the morning was
£8 10s.

NEURALGIC PAINS CURED.
“For seven years I suffered with neuralgia and
shooting pains in the head,” says Mrs. M. A. Crad-
ock, John Street, Salisbury (S.A.). “I tried many
things, but found nothing equal to Chamberlain’s
Pain Balm. 1 have recommended it to many, and
they have been relieved. It has also relieved my
son of these neuralgic pains.”*
The Australian Mutual Provident Society an-
nounces in our special columns that the next distri-
bution of the profits will be made as at 31st
December, 1912, when all participating policies issued
in the Ordinary Department previous to the close of
the current year, as well as those already on the
books, will participate in the Cash Bonus to be al-
lotted for this year. Since its inception the Society has
distributed among the members no less a sum than
£16,853,102 by of Cash Bonuses, while the
amount divided for the single year 1911 was
£888,911, the latter representing reversionary addi-
tions to policies of £1,550,000.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Victoria at present holds (on paper) the
world’s record for prevalence of lunacy.
“But,” says John Weary, in excusing him-
self for his somnolent disregard of the
matter, “there is nothing alarming when we
come to look into it. The number of insane
could be very materially reduced if we dealt
with the old people who are worn out in
mind, and often in body, as they would be
dealt with in any other country. In other
communities they would be placed in bene-
volent institutions, not in lunatic asylums.”
Exactly; and it was to carry out this dis-
cerning policy that an expert was imported
to Victoria some eight or 10 years ago. Also,
it is as head of the Lunacy Department that
Weary is paid a salary a good deal larger
than he earns. But, apnarently, there is
“nothing alarming” in the fact that the
things aren’t done which these gentlemen
are paid to do here as they are done else-
where.
The Commonwealth Government is guilty
of reckless finance, all right; but the reck-
lessness is rather in earning than in spend-
ing. The Customs and Excise revenue for
October was £1,402,241), or £327,297 more
than for October, 1911. For the four months
of this financial year the receipts have been
£5,422,372, or £740,202 more than for the
corresponding section of last year. Yet Fisher
estimated a decrease of £200,000 for the
year! Naturally a Treasurer likes to have a
buoyant revenue, but seeing that, as Cus-
toms revenue increases, employment de-
creases, that huge tariff revenue is pretty
bad business for the Commonwealth.
Fat hits his own friends sometimes. Those
energetic individuals who leased land at
Wonthaggi (Vic.) when the State coal mine
was opened, and built shops, offices, etc.,
thereon, in the expectation that the mine
would be worked to supply the State with
its own coal to the mine’s full capacity, are
now complaining of general deadness. In-
stead of being a thriving, increasing city,
the place is moribund—mainly because the
Fat Landlords last year refused to vote the
£lOO,OOO for developmental work that the
case called for. Still there are many degrees
of Fat, and it is less painful to squeeze the
comparatively little Fat of Wonthaggi than
the very bloated Fat who bosses the inter-
state coal and shipping business.
The Rev. M. J. O’Reilly, president of St.
Stanislaus College, Bathurst, complains that
the uniforms for which the cadets were
measured 12 and 18 months ago have not
yet come to hand, and meantime the boys
have increased so in stature that the old
measurements are useless. This seems to
be in line with a lot of things. The rail-
way to the N.T., for which Australia’s
measure has long been taken, will probably
be out of date before the first sod is turned.
The Australian citizen army will probably
be several sizes too small by the time it
arrives from the makers. Even Australia
itself, which Providence, or something,
measured to fit the aspirations of a great
white race, may be too cramped to afford a
hiding place for the remnant of that race
when the trouble arrives.
AN INDIGNATION MEETING.
The appointment of Labor adherents to various
offices by the Federal Ministry is highly discredit-
able.-“Liberal” comment.
FIRST LIBERAL : “Most unjust and reprehen-
sible, in my opinion .”
SECOND DITTO : “Most discreditable, sir. If
we hadn’t pushed your lad in when we
were in power , we wouldn’t have a ghost
of a chance now!”
“There is no measure of the value of work
except the old one ‘Get what you can !’ ” says
Judge Ileydon, of the N.S.W. Arbitration
Court. The Bulletin hears this with some
surprise. If Mr. Heydon planted a turnip
seed in his garden, and watered it, and other-
wise nurtured it, this paper had an idea that
Mr. Heydon would be entitled to the turnip.
It also has an idea that if some Ned Kelly
bushranged Hey don’s garden and got as
much of the turnip as he could, the Judge
would' at once jump to the conclusion that it
was a case for gaol.
Melbourne Age has come to the conclu-
sion that Victorian’s Legislative Council is
merely political cat’s-meat. Observe :
It is the self-appointed mission of the Council to
arrest progress in the interests of property. And
that it has done.... The national interest matters
nothing. The Leg. Council never troubles about the
national interest. We do not accuse the Council of
conscious wrong-doing. That would be unkind. It
acts wrongly, of course, but merely because it is in
a condition of intellectual and political decomposi-
tion.
Now, when does the Age propose to do any-
thing? When, for instance, does it intend to
give a hand to a party that will pledge itself
to shift this piece of intellectual and political
decomposition?
ON THE GOOD SHIP SCRAPS-AN.
PATCHES.
Renewed efforts are being made to consolidate
the Federal Liberal party inside and outside the
Federal Parliament. This afternoon Mr. Deakin was
approached by the Parliamentary party and re-
quested to adjust the situation, etc.— News item.
The Argits loathes and despises the
Labor party’s programme because “it glori-
fies sponging into a policy,” and “strikes at
the root of character” by wet-nursing and
coddling the people. Fat’s motives are
always excellent. What he
says is: “Leave me free to
sweat and rob the people
as the only reliable means
of improving their charac-
ters.” By the way, Victoria
pays about half a million
annually in pensions, prac-
tically all of it to ex-civil
servants of the “superior”
class. The Argus never
once denounces this as a
policy that glorifies spong-
ing, and never seems to have
suspected the smallest dan-
ger to the strength of char-
acter of the recipients.
The Rev. A. Isaacs sup-
porting a Melbourne Congre-
gational Union motion
against the Australian de-
fence laws, said: “Trust in
the Lord is all-sufficient.”
What evidence can the rev.
gent, produce? Has any
known nation since the days
of King Khammurabi, 2300
B.c. found trust in the Lord
sufficient? Meantime, while
Mr. Isaacs is producing the
proofs, he might tell us
whether he thinks trust in
the Lord would be sufficient
to protect his hen-roost
in a district where Chinese chicken-lifters
were particularly active.
“W.” : I propose that we call the capital
“King-dom Come” out of compliment
to the O’Malley and in reference to the
period when it is likely to be occupied.
UNDER A CLOUD.
The feature of the formal proceedings of the
N.S.W. Legislative Assembly yesterday was the
shadow of the financial cloud.... The shadow deep-
ened during questions without notice, when the recent
dismissals of laborers were referred tor— Daily paper.
“Melb ” : Ex-President
Wrixon’s portrait has
been hung beside those
of Palmer, Mitchell,
Mcßain and Zeal in the
Vic. Legislative Council
corridor. Wrixon started
out well by marrying
Money Miller’s daughter,
though the estate did not
pan out so well as the
prattlers expected. There
are lots of people who
don’t aspire to be top-
notchers, and Wrixon was
one of them. There was
a time when he was the
only man in Vic. who
looked like being a Chief
Justice or a Premier, but
he didn’t reach out after
either job. There have
been others like him. Not
infrequently a Willie
Watt prefers to be
vizier to a Jack Murray,
and there is a character-
istic refusal of Deakin’s
on record. Once Victoria
looked round in despair
for anything that seemed
to have the marks of a
Premier a b.o u t it.
Deakin was in full view,
and the financial crisis
howled to him with all
the tongues of the lost in Cohenna.
Deakin side-stepped a tough proposition, and
George Turner, an almost unknown man,
bogged into the quagmire and won through—
a genuine man with just a little more stiffen-
ing of the spine than either Deakin or
Wrixon.
A vociferous section of his own party is
making discomfort for Beeby, Minister for
Labor (and a few other things) in N.S.W.
Beeby passed a pretty stringent anti-strike
law, which, of course, meant penalties for
breaches; and there _ are alleged Laborites
who reckon that it isn’t a Labor Govern-
ment’s job to penalise men for exercising
“the sacred right to strike.” If striking is
to be made illegal, penalties are inevitable;
so it simply amounts to this: Is the Labor
party going to rely on Arbitration and
reason, or on strikes and physical endurance
without regard to rights and wrongs? The
sooner the party quite makes up its official
mind about that, the sooner it will put either
Beeby or the sacred-right-to-strike people in
their proper place. Incidentally, it is just
as well to remember that the sacred right
to strike involves the sacred right to lock-
out ; and that may easily be a serious thing
for the workers when the slump gets a little
worse, and there are a few thousand unem-
ployed ready to do a turn for the Fat
employer.
The Massey Government (M.L.) is losing
no time in carrying out the “honorable
understanding” with its stout friends and
well-wishers. One of the most unpleasant
eyesores to those friends and well-wishers
has been the State Fire Insurance Dept.,
and how to wipe it out, in whole or
part, has been the cause of many a sleepless
night. The most profitable part of the
department’s business has been in conjunc-
tion with the Advances to Settlers’ Dept.;
each borrower was compelled to insure his
property in the State Fire office. That has
been altered now. Under Massey’s rule, the
regulation, which read: “You will require to
furnish State Fire insurance cover,” has
recently been replaced by this: “You will re-
quire to furnish a quarter-day insurance
policy, preferably effected with the State Fire
Office.” Now good old Pirate Enterprise is
preparing to show the Backbone the advan-
tages of his “solid” office as compared with
the State office which the Government itself
has weakened on.
W. Angliss, of the Melbourne
butchering trade, is snorting so
violently at the idea of such a
thing as a local Beef Trust
that people are wondering what
perturbs him. If the alleged
meat ring exists only in the
imagination of the Minister of
Agriculture and other people
who naturally know nothing of
what they talk about, why does
Angliss write half-column let-
ters to the Argus, protesting
against their ignorance? Why
worry? Surely a myth could
be left to explode of its own
gas.
“Mr. Ocky”: One of Vic-
toria’s crying needs is for a
Labor party leader who will
hustle things along a bit. Prin-
ter-politician Prendergast is be-
coming every day more impos-
sible, for he has developed an
inertia that would do credit to
a whole-hog Tory. That is to
say, he is inert in most of the
things that really matter, whilst
in the tiddly-winking concerns
he makes a most disproportion-
ate bid for cheap advertise-
ment. He helps to block some
of the good proposals made by
the Cabinet, mainly for the
reason that an absurd party
system encourages him to do
so, but lacks the energy to forcibly pro-
nounce his own best ideas at every oppor-
tunity. Watt may lose his office, but Mac-
kinnon or McLeod is much more likely to
acquire it than G. M. Prendergast.
THE STRENGTH TO BE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 heard the march of a Nation’s feet as Australia’s
pride went by,
And niv heart was thrilled with a stronger beat, and
a glad tear dimmed each eye;
For this was the dream of my younger days, my
vision of strength to be,
And my soul-chords sang like a harp of praise, with
an anthem brave and free !
As the Young Guard passed with a martial tread,
that challenged the base and mean,
I cried “All hail!” and I bared my head to Aus-
tralia’s war-machine.
And it seemed to me that the Nation’s soul rang
forth in a major key—
“We have turned our face to a nobler goal ; we are
marching, God, with Thee!”
Then planet and star and the farthest sun blazed out
in an echoing hymn—
“Ye have turned your backs on the creeds foredone,
and the erutch-faithed gospels dim!
Ye have raised the flag of a nation just—ye have
spurned with a strong man’s heel
This niddering crew with its feckless trust! Ye have
girded tempered steel!
Now this is the law of the ages all, that life for a
land begins
When it grasps the steel at the danger-call and turns
from its faithless sins!
And this is the law of our Captain, God, that only
the strong shall thrive —-
In the days when the bolts are lightning-shod, ye
shall save your land alive!”
Then the Nation’s voice, in a stronger key, pealed
forth to the farthest star —
“We have turned our face to our destiny from the
racecourse revel far!
We have gripped our Steel with a Strong Man’s trust
in the work that is ours to do—
We have faced our Task as a nation must, for the
girding-years be few.
We have wasted years of our grasping-time; with
fingers slackened and slow
We have toyed with life and its tasks sublime—hark
now how the bugles blow !
For this is the proof of our stronger zeal, and
trebled the proof shall be
When our ship-yards clamor with turret and keel, and
the rail joins with the sea!”
From planet and star and the farthest sun came
echoing forth “All hail!
And ye are the kith of the breed once spun from
each English shire and dale !
Yea, ye are the kin of the pauper breed that whines
at the name of steel.
Whilst your kinsmen cringe in the hour of need, ye
are arming the Commonweal!
Lo, this is the law of the ages all, that the seed
such Empires sow
Is reaped in tears when the bugles call and the
brazen trumpets blow!
Yea, this is the law of our Captain, God —that only
the Strong Lands thrive,
And only the weak shall kiss the rod and bow
’neath fetter and gyve! ”
I heard the drums of Australia beat. As they echo
. around this sphere; v
I would weave a psalm of my faith complete that
our crutch-faithed kin may hear!
I would hail my dream of the Younger Days, ere the
Prodigal’s course was run,
Ere this land had turned from its husk-strewn ways—
(l, too, was a Prodigal Son!)
Shall the Young Guard pass with its martial tread,
that challenges all things mean ?—-
Shall England hearken ?—her drums are dead, and
vain is her war-machine !
Has she turned her back on the noblest goal ? —is she
marching, God, with Thee?—
I only know that my Nation’s Soul is ‘endowed with
Thy strength to be!
VICTORIA.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The unaccountably bad performance of
hot-pot Duke Foote in this year's Cup, which was
on all-fours with the unaccountably bad perform-
ance of hot-pot Trafalgar in last year’s Cup, may
prove the best thing that could happen for the
public. In backing the old-established favorite that
"must get there or thereabouts,” a lot of confiding
punters reckon that thev are at least assured of a
pleasant thrill for their money. They are not going
for a big win, but they are paying an extravagant
price, as it were, for the expectation of seeing their
gee in the front rank coming down the straight.
Last year it seemed impossible, bar accidents, that
Trafalgar could fail to make a bold show in the
Cup. He had won the Melbourne Stakes brilliantly,
and his staying power was proved ; nothing but his
heavy weight could stop him from winning, and the
weight wouldn’t tell until his backers had experi-
enced their thrill. This year, nothing could stop
Duke Foote, bar accidents, save a gradual failing
of his energy after he had gone more than 12 fur-
longs. Up to that distance he had been tested and
proved. Yet he ran exactly as Trafalgar ran last
year. He was never nearer the front than 7th, and
he finished 15th. The people who took 7 to 2 about
Trafalgar a year ago, and 6 to 4 about Duke Foote
last week, were not given a ghost of a winning
show for their gamble. The completeness of the
failure was equally unaccountable in both cases.
Moral: Don’t take a very short price about any-
thing for a Melbourne Cup.
Mr. “Baron” Brown made no pretence of re-
ceiving his defeat philosophically. To a reporter
who asked him what he thought of Duke Foote’s
backwardness, the owner would merely say: “I
wasn’t on lop of him. Ask the bov who was on
him.” From which it appears that “Baron” Brown
doesn’t admit the possibility of Duke Foote having
been beaten for want of stamina, or because he
wasn’t feeling well. Yet the fact remains that the
brilliant animal ran badly in last year’s Cup, but
was equal to winning the Williamstown Cup (11
furlongs) a few days later.
The notion that English-bred horses in Aus-
tralia are sprinters rather than stayers is _ a
popular delusion, considering that none of the im-
ported ones have been first-class or even second-
class performers in their own country, or have been
tested for stamina before being put to it on this
side. Their average form in two-mile races is good
enough. Yoyou, who ran second to M°rriwee, was
the first imported horse to go for a Melbourne Cup,
and if the race had been a week later, Yoyou
would certainly have won it. Comedy King was
imported as a youngster with his dam. Five English
horses ran in this year’s Cup, and three of them—
Hallowmas, Almissa and Eudorus —finished in the
first six. What the gees from the Cold Country
lack is sturdiness and amiability, as a rule. Two
notable specimens—Son of the Marsh and Sand-
bath —have been raced in winkers. The latter is,
apparently, a most sulky beast, though, like Hal-
lowmas, he managed to win the Waverley Handicap
(14 furlongs) at Rand wick. They ran Sandbath
without winkers for the Melbourne Cup this time,
and an hour before the race one of his caretakers
made frantic efforts to pour some Dutch courage
into him from a lemonade bottle. But it was a
waste of effort. And a lot of good brandy and
water, or whatever was in the bottle, got spilt
everywhere but into Sandbath’s mouth.
Leaving the favorite’s collapse out of the ques-
tion, there was satisfactory confirmation of “form
in the Melbourne Cup running. Piastre and Ladies
Man, the first and fourth, had run second and third
in the Metropolitan a month earlier, and the Met. of
late years has been a good race to draw inferences
from. In 1906, Poseidon ran second in the Met.
before winning Caulfield and Melbourne Cups. In
1907, Mooltan and Apologue ran first and second
for the Met. Apologue ran second in the Caulfield
Cup to stable companion Poseidon (winner for the
second time), and turned the tables on Mooltan in
the Melbourne Cup. Next year, Mooltan won the
Met. again, and Maranui was third. Maranui then
won the Caulfield Cup, and Mooltan finished fourth
(broken down) for the Melbourne Cup. In 1910,
Flavinius ran third for the Met. before winning
the Caulfield Cup, and last year Lady Medallist was
second in the Met. before winning the Caulfield
Cup. So in six years out of the last seven, the
second or third horse in the Met. has won one or
other of the subsequent Cups, and on one occasion
two of the placed horses in the Met. were placed in
the Melbourne Cup. This year the Met. form was
not represented in the Caulfield event.
Melbourne Cup race this year was some-
thing of a triumph for the touts, as it happened.
Pia- tre was credited with a “phenomenal” track gallop
of if miles, which was the more talked about because
fe' ' or none, of the other candidates had done any
\< :y severe work. Also, Matouree surprised the touts
P r his improved form at exercise, and for 12 furlongs
or thereabouts Matouree was one of the leading lot.
On the- other hand, the horse-watchers reported that
Duke Foote was not shaping like a Cup horse, and
that Uncle Sam showed signs of going off. To the
present writer’s way of thinking there was a distinct
disimprovement in the nervous chestnut. The Uncle
Sam who got badly jostled and somewhat cut about
in the Cup race was not the same lusty animal that
won at Caulfield. He showed the same pluck in his
finishing effort, but he had been slow in getting near
the fore, and there was a want of vim in the run that
placed him third, only a neck or so in front of
Ladies’ Man. A great battler on his day is Uncle
Sam, and not likely to be troubled by a bit of weight
in any company; but his very best days are not
often The chestnuts, as a rule, are not as tough
as the bays and browns. Look at the performances of
Malt King, the handsomest chestnut of them all ! A
champion at a mile and 10 furlongs, his periods of
mightiness have been fitful. And even Trafalgar was
inconsistent (though rarely out of the hunt) as com-
pared with Carbine and Poseidon.
An early fancy for tbe Melbourne Cup is
a vain thing. Trafalgar, the ill-fated favorite in
three previous years, and-a popular selection this time,
broke down three weeks before the race. The Paris-
ian also went amiss. Wilari broke a blood-vessel,
and dropped out of notice. Karnattan, who had been
saved for the Cup, broke his leg and was shot a week
before the race. And a lot of other early fancies
withered on the stem of hope. Then there was
Flavian, the second in both Cups last year. He had
grown into a noble-looking animal, and was said to
be “coming on nicely.” He continued to come on,
though not so nicely as was expected, until Derby
Day. Then he trod on a stone when returning to his
stable from the course, and hurt his heel. Scratched
the day before the Cup. And Lady Medallist. She
was said to be “in great buckle” up to the time of
starting for the Melbourne Stakes. After which it
was discovered that her awful failure was due to a
strained muscle or something. It might as easily
have been the lot of Piastre to kick a stone or strain
a muscle. Bookmakers look for these things to hap-
pen. Early punters forget that they have ever
occurred. ,
Melbourne Cup betting on the Hill at
Flemington was absolute robbery of that large sec-
tion of the puntocracy which goes to the races in a
picnickian spirit, and is most in need of the protec-
tion of the Tote, lor a long time before the start
for the Cup the books were filling their bags with
quids, ’arf-quids and dollars at the all-round market
price of six to four Duke Eoote, three to one Piastre,
three to one Uncle Sam, “10 to one bar three” and
“outsiders,” on a proportionate scale of wickedness.
Remarkable fact that only two of the
jockeys who weighed out for this year’s Cup had
previously ridden the winner of the big event.
“Bobby” Lewis was atop of The Victory in 1902;
McLachlan steered Prince Foote in 1909, and Comedy
King in 1910.
The last two days of the V.R.C. meeting
were largely devoted to reversals of form. For in-
stance, tne furious Sandbath, who had been always
at the heels of slower animals in the llotham Handi-
cap on Derby Day, sprinted away with the Veteran
Slakes on Oaks Day, and did the 12 furlongs in
2min. 34sec., winch is nearly a record for the 1 lem-
ington oval. Mountain Princess scored an actual
record for a mile al flemington, by capturing the
Linlithgow Stakes, in linin. 39sec., the ground being
sufficiently springy for the honest little mare. She and
Sylvander claimed big allowances, whereas Wolawa
a.id Burrawang were penalised 101 b., and the advan-
tage in the weights served the two handicap fliers
at the finish. * ' * *
In the Oaks race the improving Moe
fairly outstayed Perdita, and beat the Derby time
by 3sec., which suggests that the daughter of Wal-
lace and Moama is likely to be the best three-year-
old of the season when the autumn comes.
Saturday was Cider’s day for getting his
revenge on Wolawa, in a muddling contest for the
C. B. Fisher Plate. The betting on this w.f.a. event
was as unsatisfactory as the running of it. For a
while it seemed that Uncle Sam would start as
good a favorite as Eudorus, and a better favorite
than Piastre, on the reasonable assumption that
the big horse would carry 9st. more easily than anv
of them. Finally, the odds against Uncle lengthened
to fives, whilst Cider shortened to fours (two points
less than Wolawa’s price), Piastre and Eudorus being
the favorites. A field that included the winners of
Melbourne and Caulfield Cups, and the winner and
runner-up in the Derby, might have been expected to
put up a lively fight; but the pace from the start
was so slow that Uncle Sam had the lead forced upon
him, as it were. In the straight the field bunched
and the big horse never got a chance to make his
famous effort at the finish —if he was capable of it.
Cider got the run and pipped Eudorus on the post.
After the race, which was 16see. slower than the
Oaks, the appearance of Uncle Sam indicated that
he had been considerably buffeted, or was otherwise
upset.
* * * * ,
Duke Foote’s scratching for the C. B.
Fisher Plate, wherein it had been intended by his
owner that he should reassert himself, was practically
a confession by “Baron” Brown that his champion
was stale, and must have been going stale on the
previous Saturday, when he won the Melbourne
Stakes in the dirt, and trainer Barden reckoned it
bad policy to give the waning Duke a gallop on
Derby Day. But the owner would have his way,
and 'the Melbourne Cup fiasco didn’t immediately
convince him that his way had been wrong. How-
ever, between the Tuesday and the following Satur-
day the Duke went right off, and now he is to be
given a spell. It. doesn’t follow that the favorite
could have won Melbourne Cup had he been allowed
to miss his trying engagement on the wet Derby
Day. All the same, it seems certain that he would
have retained until the Tuesday some of the old
form which he finally exhausted on the Saturday in
the Melbourne Stakes. If his less remain true to
him, Duke Foote will bloom again anon. He is a
much hardier-looking customer than half the local
horses, or any of the imported stuff. #
“H.”: The one month of dismalification
imposed on jockey Shanahan, the rid ' of Piastre,
for interfering with Uncle Sam in the C. B. Fisher
Plate, was one of the displays which bring the
theoretics lly-good stipendiary steward system to
scorn. The five runners were well together inside
the distance, Uncle Sam on the rails, when Piastre
swung in. knocked the Caulfield Cup winner on to
his haunches, and injured one of his hind legs.
Cider and Eudorus rushed past, Cider winning by a
head. Wolawa was fourth. So Uncle Sam was
bumped from a winning position to last. And the
V.R.C. stewards, having found Shanahan guilty, hit
him with a penalty which will enable him to be in
the saddle at the very next meetings of any conse-
quence, viz., the A.J.C. and Tatt.’s summer fixture in
six weeks’ time. It was a splendid opportunity _ to
drive home a lesson that badly needs hammering in;
and it was thrown away.
* * * *
For the first time in the memory of the
oldest member, the Vic. Assembly met on Cup day.
True, the afternoon session was cut so. that members
might see the winning of the utensil; and for a
time there were doubts whether a quorum would be
got in the evening. But Labor reinforcements
dribbled in, and the situation was saved.

The police report of Melbourne Cup day
makes pathetic reading: “Two hundred police pre-
sent—only three pockets picked.” Apparently Pat-
rick Iscariot had cleaned out the crowd.
* * * *
Adelaide Racing Club is generous enough
to congratulate Victoria on robbing it of E. W.
Ellis as stipendiary steward. The job is worth £750
per annum. # #
The Tote at Johannesburg, S’Africa, had
one of its frequent bursts of generosity the other day.
In the Galloway Plate a much-disliked outsider
romped home easily, and its six supporters on the
Tote were rewarded with £389 Bs. for a win and
£5B 16s. for a place. The trio who had backed the
quadruped for a straight-out victory included two
of Britain’s colored population. Even niggers enjoy
the blessing of the Tote, for which the Ikey-bossed
punters of N.S.W. and Vic. are sighing in vain.
As the N.S.W. Government has arrived at
that stage of the spendthrift’s progress where any
stone that may conceal a sovereign is worth turning
over, why doesn’t the McGowlman Ministry make
Levien’s Totalisator Bill a Government measure,
force it through, and get it going in time to scoop
in a few thousand pounds of revenue during the
Christmas horse carnival ? It is hard to say how much
money is spent in gambling during an A.J.C. week
at Rand wick; but as six days’ racing in Maori land
saw £243,417 go through the machine, it is fairly
safe to say that the punters of Randwick invest at
least £lOO,OOO on the usual four days’ meeting.
And £lOO,OOO put through the Tote, yielding 2$
per cent., would give the worried Treasurer Cann
£2500 for a Christmas box. #
The chief item of interest in the boxing
circle of Melbourne last week was the biffing of
Turner. Turner (13st. 41b.) was scrapping with
Walsh (12st. 31b.) at the Athletic Pavilion with
Fu 11a love, amateur middleweight and heavyweight
premier of for referee. Turner showed a
disposition to make it partly a wrestling bout and
partly a boxing contest, and the referee spoke to
him severely. In the fifth round he . was pulling
the men apart, when Turner “gave him wan” on
the neck. The referee immediately changed his name
to Fullafury, swung round and drove a straight
right on to Turner’s cheekbone. The fearsome
smash landed right and square, and sent Turner
spinning against the ropes with an opened cheek.
The referee, having settled that little matter,
pointed to Walsh as winner, and then left the ring
without as much as a look at the man he had prac-
tically slugged out. It was a beautiful wallop.
At the inquest on boxer McCarthy, who
died after his battle with feather-weight George
Taylor in Melbourne, it transpired that the pro-
moter did not have the boxers medically examined
before letting them enter the ring. Taylor was
arrested on a charge of feloniously slaying McCarthy,
but medical evidence proved that he had nothing to
do with the death of his late opponent, who
really came to a sudden end through high blood
pressure. Any violent exercise, such as chopping
wood for the missus, would have brought about the
climax, immediately the exertion passed a certain
point; and any medical examination worthy the
name would have disclosed the fact. The promoter
said he was under the impression it was optional
whether he had the boxers medically examined or
not: If it is optional, it ought not to be, not only
as regards boxing, but as to some other violent
sports as well. In bike racing compulsory examina-
tion is especially necessary, as one man coming down
on a crowded track through heart-failure may cause
death or injury to every man in the heat, while
in motor-bike contests the possibilities are very
gruesome. * * *
Some weeks ago, when bail was first
granted him, some of Johnson’s familiars advised
him to evanish, as the ’Murkans had determined to
get him out of the country or into the cells; but
Johnson decided he would stand his ground, as
leaving would entail losing his Caf6 de Champion, and
all the rest of it. Then when the police dropped on to
the trail of a secret harem, and scented some transac-
tions relating to the bringing of Belle Schreiber from
Pittsburg to Chicago, in 1910, to be a member of
that harem, Johnson opined that the time had come
to get out. But he had left it too late. Still, he
made a sort of effort, and when the police called he
was missing; but they ran him to earth in an ob-
scure part of the town,” hiding in a negro hotel,
and promptly arrested and handcuffed him. ■John-
son showed signs of weeping; and as it is saul the
Law proposed to charge him with eight offences in
connection with white women, he has something to.
weep about. Boiled down, the United States Federal
Law Department charges Johnson with being a
“wb-'te slaver” of a large and aggravated type. And
that’s just about the ugliest charge a nigger can
have brought against him in the U.S.A. It is tor-
tunate for Johnson he’s in Chicago— way down m
Tennessee he’d probably have gone up in smoke ere
this.

Annarpntly we are in for a new way of
deciding fights in Sydney. The referee m the ring
controlled the fight on Saturday night, but when it
was over a Stadium officer walked to the judges and
obtained their written verdicts, and as they . were
apparently unanimous Hill was declared the winner.
Apparently the referee will only five his decision
now when the judges disagree. What a wily old
diplomat the Mclntosh is, to be sure! When the
press jumped on his referee, he promptly wrote to
that official, expressing his boundless confidence in
him, and assuring him of his undying support. And
before the staggered press got over the one on the
point thus administered he appoints two judges to
assist the overworked referee. Now, whose leg was
being pulled?
Crawford the cricketer is engaged to a
pretty girl in Adelaide, so Australia is likely to
keep him, notwithstanding that his quarrel with
Surrey county has been honorably patched up. Ail
the same, he ought not to be allowed to play in the
projected Trumper benefit match. It is sure to be
regarded as a trial of (strength between the Austra-
lian XI and the “Six,” and Crawford—who was not
eligible for selection in the former —would give a
fictitious appearance of strength to the latter.

The damp wickets in Sydney on Satur-
day were all in favor of the bowlers, who took deadly
advantage of the fact. G. C. Willcocks (105), of Uni-
versity, scored the only century. The next best was the
72 of J. Hodgkinson, of Sydney, who was still not
out when stumps were drawn, whacked off the Middle
Harbor bowling. Eric Barbour, of University,
smacked 50 off Central Cumberland, and there the
list of batting honors ended. F. Bowden, of Gordon,
took six for 22 and one for 20—a total of seven for 4-
for the afternoon—in the match with North Sydney.
Folkard, the reliable Balmaniac, felled six Burwood
wickets for 26 runs; Stack, of University, smashed
up six Central Cumberland sticks for 54 ; Dr. Hor-
dern, of North Svdney, bagged five Gordon batsmen for
16 • and A C. Rough, of Petersham, smoothed five
Glebe wickets for 39. Naturally, _ under these cir-
cumstances, there was no very brilliant scoring, and
the top tally of the afternoon was the 250 of Uni-
versity, for 155 of which IVillcocks and Eric Barbour
were responsible. The absolutely best effort, though,
was Sydney’s 124 for two wickets, whereat Middle
Harbor and all the world wondered. Middle Harbor
had gone in first, and previous to coruscating in this
fashion, Svdney had dismissed it for 115. Naturally,
the games' made wonderful progress. North Sydney
smacked 110, dismissed Gordon for 39, and then
smacked 58 for three wickets in the second innings.
Central Cumberland left 86 for University to eclipse,
which it did by putting up 250. Redfern smacked
106, and asked, Waverley, what it thought of it.
Waverley expressed its honest opinion by hitting
107 for five wickets. Petersham dismissed Glebe for
89, and put up 40 for the loss of four sticks. Bal-
main got loose on Burwood, laying it out for 52 and
making 66 for five wickets. Paddington smashed
up Dr. O’Hara’s team for 51, and piled up 114 for
the loss of seven wickets. All of which is what the
spectators call “bright cricket.”
* * * *
Owing to the dampness of the previous
week-end, the third round of Melbourne’s pennant
cricket was crowded into about three hours’ play on
Saturday. The policy of a captain was obviously to
send in his smiters, if he had any, and whack up
a couple of hundred; then declare, and get the other
side out. Of course, the other side might boast
some swifter smiters ; but that risk had to be taken.
East rushed in to glory or a walloping, and accumu-
lated 197 for one wicket in 100 minutes. Then it
closed and set about consuming St. Kilda. The
latter declined to be consumed in the allotted time,
and when stumps were drawn had put together 90 for
four wiclcpts. The other teams either lost so manv
early wickets for so few runs, that they were forced
to play for the draw, or else left the “closing” busi-
ness so late that they had no chance of outing their
opponents. Fitzroy made 238 off South Melbourne’s
bowling; and South didn’t have a look in at the
wickets. Northcote declared against Essendon with
151 for five wickets; Essendon replied with 63 for
six wickets. Prahran hit 218 for eight wickets, and
Carlton had to be content to supply the bowling.
Collingwood made 277 for five wickets, off Rich-
mond’s bowling. North smacked 183 off Uni., and
the Students put up 68 for one wicket. And as
tzihbon might have written, thus was lost a fine op-
portunity for sundry youths to let themselves go
and shake things up, even if they were outed for
ducks and the game lost. # #
Sydney yachtsmen had a good time, and
the open boat men an exciting one on Saturday, when
the Yacht Squadron ran off the Gascoigne Cup, and
the Flying Squadron blew through two heats and a
final. Six yachts started for the Gascoigne in a
charming E.N.E. breeze of pleasant muscular de-
velopment. Culwulla 111. got the best of a magni-
ficent start, and practically led the way out through
the Heads after some excellent manoeuvring on the
part of all the fleet. Among the competitors was the
sometime Melbourne boat, Sayonara. Culwulla 111.
led round the mark at Long Reef; Sayonara, follow-
ing, carried away the top-mast, and thereby lost all
chance of the Cup. Thelma ran into second place,
closely followed by the little Petrel, after which
flashed White Wings and Ranee. After that the ex-
citement cooled down—it was merely a case of show-
ing the cleanest pair of heels, and they tore home
in the order in which they rounded the reef, Culwulla
leading the way. But when the handicaps were ad-
justed it was found little Petrel had scooped the
utensil, with Thelma second and Culwulla third.
In the open-boat section the day was to
the big boats, which were also the back-markers.
With their vaster sail surface they raked the skv
for wind—and got what was going. Some of them
raked it so thoroughly that they caught more than
they could carry, and turned turtle. In the first
heat Australian, under the old master, Christian
Webb, got away to a splendid start, and donkey-
licked everything that came against her, except Kis-
met. which would not be altogether shaken off, and
finished second. The second heat fell to Donnelly,
after a hard tussle in which there were more cap-
sizes, and several cases of gear carrying away. They
were crowding on the canvas recklessly, and had
their reward —a free wash. Arline hung on right
through, and finished second. In the final Austra-
lian and Kismet were penalised for their win in the
first heat. But a new Richmond appeared suddenly
in the shape of Nimrod, which by a daring stroke
gained about a mile on the whole fleet. After mat
it was a case of the fleet chasing Nimrod home.
The real struggle was between Kismet and Australian
for second place, and the handling of Kismet was
beautiful to see. At the psychological moment Kis-
met crowded on every possible rag. Ihe boat lifted
out of the water and blew past Australian at a ter-
rific rate. And, as they say in the classics, landed
the second side of the bacon.
* * * *
After two days of desperate struggling of
almost Bulgarian ferocity, South Sydney retained its
grin on the Dunn Challenge Shield, the outward and
visible token of the championship of Svdney Athletic
Clubs. It was the club’s third successive win. But
—tremble Tarshish! Each year sees the down-
trodden clubs making a better fight, drawing nearer
to equality and success. In 1910 South won with
to the good; in 1911 the margin dropped to
1: and last week’s victory showed the still nar-
rower gulf of 51 points between South and the next
best, East Svdney. The actual positions last week
were—South 'Sydney, 311 points; East Sydney, 26
points; University, 18 points; Police Club, 15
points; with five others picking up the crumbs that
fell from the tables of these mighty ones.
* * * -*
Sydney’s maddest sportsman is a man
who once had a friend mutilated by a shark. It hap-
pened years ago, but he goes on paying it back to
all sharks he can get hold of. His method is simple,
if not delightful. After a shark has been caught, he
is hoisted up the side of the launch, and a hook
made fast in his tail. Attached to the hook by wire
is an empty kerosene tin, which has been hermetically
sealed up; and as soon as the attachment is made
the shark is liberated. The brute can’t keep down
except by exercise of strength. Immediately he takes
things easy the buoy drags him tail first to the sur-
face, and he loses his equilibrium. Fear gets hold
of him. He chases his own tail frenziedly; and the
rest of the sharks stand aghast and remark, “What’s
up with Blue-nose?” Then B.N. gets a dark sus-
picion that there is a conspiracy against him, and he
runs amok in circles, snapping and gashing every-
thing that comes in his way, till he is exhausted,
when up goes his tail again. By this time the Shark
Legislative Council has held a session, and decided
that this sort of conduct is un-British, or something
to that effect, and they fall on B.N. and tear him
to pieces, and get badly mauled in the process. Oc-
casionally a wiser shark comes to the surface and
bashes his tin frenziedly on the water, battering it
in. Once the “sport” caught a shark that had two
hooks still fastened in his tail, so he marooned him
in a harbor pond till he could make proper arrange-
ments for his adequate decoration, which, when it
arrived, turned out to be a barrel attached to the
hook by a strong chain. That shark went mad and
died of sheer fright in about 32 minutes after being
let loose. It’s a weird joke!

Most Young' Men
And those who feel young, prefer occasion-
ally to have a touch of oddness about their
shoes, and that does not necessarily mean
freakish footwear; but just enough depar-
ture from the monotonous and conventional

Being charged with the duty of assuring the maximum of comfort, at the
least possible expense, she appreciates the advantages of a perfect lighting
system, which is absolutely safe and clean.
SHE KNOWS
that the installation of a gas service means the saving of an untold amount of
work and worry, and, more than anyone else, realises the benefits of having
a gas stove and bath-heater always ready to help her through the continuous
round of household duties.
The Immense Saving Effected by
is alone a very important factor, for it is far cheaper than any known lighting
agent, while—being non-explosive and non-poisonous—there is no need to be
constantly on one’s guard against accidents.
THE SIMPLICITY IN THE CONSTRUCTION OF THE MACHINE
assures reliability, and obviates the necessity of any skilled attendance.
The Company send their own Experts to install the system, and no payment
is asked until the Customer gives his certificate as to satisfactory service.
