Established 30 Years.
To vary the monotony of things it is proposed to
supply J. Bull witli an Australian play. T rank (Jerald
is on the lookout for a Fogtown playhouse to accom-
modate “On Our Selection.” The idea is to send over
the whole show, from the heroine to the horse’s col-
lar.
“Haresfoot”: It is a pleasure for me to hear that
Sprague, the son of Dolores Drummond, is an emi-
nent theatrical architect. I once acted with Dolores
when she played Martha Gibbs in "All That Glitters
is Not Gold,” which Thespians know well. It must
be very gratifying for a lady older than Ellen Terry
to have a golden son to lean on. Dolores was long
known on the Melbourne stage as Dolly Green. She
supported Jefferson, but- not in leading parts, and
maybe some will remember her as May Fielding, in
“The Cricket on the Hearth.” I have a dim memory
of seeing her act the hero in “Gil Bias,” also Fairy
Corallina in a pantomime. As with Mrs. Vezin,
Dominick Murray, Sheil Barry, and others who
went from Australia to England, the English people
found more talent in her than Australia did. She
was regarded here as mediocre. I remember her
just as pleasant, but nothing more. She astonished Aiis- :
tralians, even her friends, by the great English hit
she made as Ilortense, the French maid, to Jennie
Lee’s Jo. Really, Dolly was almost as much talked
about as Jennie. Her husband, Drummond, was a
member of the brilliant company which sunnorted
the Sisters Joey and Adelaide Gougenheim (Geoghe-
gan) at the Melbourne Princess's. Other members
were Emily Glyndon, Rogers, H. N. Warner, G.
Fawcett (Rowe), and Jimmy Milne. Boucicault’s
“Irish Heiress” and Planche’s “Extravaganza c 1
Fortunio” were the chief successes.
“Vulcan”: London Palace Theatre was the excep-
tion to the rule of general theatrical slump in Bull-
dom last season. The profits for 12 months ending
June were £23,826, which, after sundry liberal pro-
visions, panned out a dividend of 20 p.c. This result was
largely due to items on the bill like “Mdlle. Chic,” a
sketch by Gaby Deslys, the one-time best girl of
Mr. Manoel Portugal, now out of a job. It scandal-
ised the Fogtown press, and raised loud yells for the
censor. The piece is one of the bedroom order,
wherein the lady ihetamorphoses into a more or less
(mostlv more) state of Eve before a dressing table,
and afterwards dances “The Gaby Glide.” This
according to the Daily Express, is a species of;
Turkey Trot - cum - Grizzly Hug - cum - Apache
Dance, which “rightly belongs to the night cafes of
Montmartre,” and is described as sheer indecency.t
The item wasn’t submitted at George Rex’s recent
“command” performance. Probably the lady ob-
jected, on religious grounds, to the necessary Sunday
rehearsal.
One Max Goldberg, an English melodramatist, has
had the nerve to build another four-act play round
Sweet Nell, and to call it “Nell Gwynne of Old
Drury.” It makes Rochester the villain,, and actor
Betterton the comic relief, and gives Nell g falsely-
accused parent for whose sake she sacrifices herself
to the amorous king!
Apropos the gorgeous Opera House, built in Lon-
don by the N’York Jew, Oscar Hammerstein, there
are revelations about the ructions behind the scenes j
while the owner was running the disastrous show to
attenuated audiences. His prima donna, Felicia
Lyne, says he was so rude to her that she had to
strike him on the face with a copy of “Faust.”
Hammerstein says he can stand a lot, but he will
not stand that, so he. has issued a writ for £ 20,000 .
against Felicia. : And Felicia is getting in early by
giving het impressions of Hammerstein, with more
vigor even that she is alleged to have put into the
blow.
Charles Manners, who has been running the
“Moody-Manners opera company” in England, has
issued the prospectus of the “United Empire Grand !
Opera, Ltd.” Capital £12,500, in 100.000 half-
crown shares. Object, to produce opera in English
throughout the Empire on which the sun never sets
and the tax p-atherer never goes to bed. The com-
pany, says Charles (who is himself the owner of
one of the deenest coal-cellar or coal-scuttle voices
ever heard) will be “constituted for three years only,
as such a short period will afford a suitable oppor-
tunity to consider the effects of its operations. The
shareholders can then decide whether the profits shall
be devoted to the development of the scheme, or
whether the capital shall be returned, plus a bonus
derived from the balance in hand at the time of
winding up.” If this writer could be sure of that
balance, he would risk a modest 2s. 6d. of his own
in this Imperial operatic venture.
“Haresfoot”: How many know that Strauss’s
“Fledermaus,” from which “The Nightbirds” is
adapted, was performed in Australia? Few, I war-
rant. Camille Dubois was in the cast. She was the
wife of the Hon. Wyndham Stanhope, and her mar-
riage had been a London sensation. Another cele-
brity who played in the old show was present-day
producer Henry Bracy.
“N.L.”: Notice that the old folk-stories are being
effectively used by the biograph fiend. A recent in-
stance was based on the tradition (versified by Leigh
Hunt, and pumped into many of us at school) of a
test devised by a French court dame of the period of
Henry 11. The iladv, as most people have forgotten,
threw a glove into a den of lions, and dared one
of her lovers to rescue it. “Nellie the Lion-tamer,”
recently screened in Australia, similarly throws a
rose. Another adaptation from old story is that in
which a biogranh child detects daylight burglars at
work. When they vamoose she hangs on to their
motor-car and scatters bran along the road so that
they may be tracked, as Hop o’ My Thumb scattered
first pebbles and then breadcrumbs. In fact, the
kid’s a sort of Jemima Hop o’ My Thumb.
The drama draws steadily closer to vaudeville. In
London just now Gladys Cooper plays Beauty in
“Everywoman” nightly at one theatre; and the
young girl in the last act of the U.S. success, “Mile-
stones,” at another theatre —just as George Robey
dashes from one hall to another every evening in
quest of a crust, fizz and sundries. Talking of
“Milestones,” it is the success of the season in Eng-
land, and has travelling companies out everywhere.
“K.V.G.”: Re the conversion to righteousness of
George Edwardes, of comic opera, or rather musical
comedy, fame (B. 24/10/T2). Edwardes is not a
nephew of the late Michael Gunn, of Dublin. He is
a cousin, and his theatrical experience was gained
principally at the old Opera Comique, and the Savoy,
under the aegis of D’Oyley Carte, with whom Michael
Gunn was at that time in partnership. John Gunn,
of Clarke, Meynell and Gunn, who died some three
years ago in Sydney, was a nephew of Michael
Gunn’s, Not that it matters a cuss; but there it
is,
THE “KNOCKABOUT” GIVES A RECIPE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How do we “go”?
Say you don’t know!
Not less than three calls.
Re-calls and re-calls,
Each bloomin’ show !
How do we score?
■Hear the big roar
Start when my pard here
Wallops me hard here —-
Biff ! On the jor !
Oh, what a shout!
Ten teeth drop out.
Then with my stick, I.
Havin’ a quick eye,
Fetch him a clout!
Why the big shriek?
Not hard to stek—
With ruddy make-up
I quickly fake up
lllood on each check !
That’s the thing—gore !
That's where we score:
That proves we’re hurtiu’
Each, other ! Curtain !
“Encore! Encore!”

Twelve Minutes
from Central.
Victoria Park Racecourse —situated
at Zetland —is only i 2 minutes from
Central Railway Station and affords a
pleasant rendezvous for lovers of the turf.
The grounds are extensive, and the
many flower-beds —now in full bloom —
make a most pleasing picture* Every
detail tending- to increase the comfort
and enjoyment of Victoria Park patrons
has been attended to —the splendidly-
appointed Grandstand equalling that of
any course in Australasia.
There are retiring and Sitting
Rooms for the use of Ladies —the
sanitary arrangements being modern in
every respect.
Part of the Ground Floor of the Paddock Stand
is occupied by a well-furnished Bar —the remainder
reserved for Dining Rooms, seating 200 guests.
A feature of Victoria Park racing is that all races
are run in one heat —there are no divisions. This
arrangement ensures keen, well-contested racing, and
allows visitors to return home in good time.
Next fleeting: November 20
FIRST RACE 2 P.M.
Look out for page on Victoria
Park Cup, November 28.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will

THE BETRAYER’S CRIME.
On a bridge stood a desperate couple i •, ,

P.F H : You have a bigger front than the

If you took a Plank o/ Wood
about 5 feet long, 9 inches wide, and an inch and a half thick; and
you drilled two hundred and sixteen holes in it, each hole an inch
and a quarter deep, and a quarter of an inch in diameter; hammered
two hundred and sixteen steel pins into those holes, each pin slightly
larger than the hole it was driven into; and then hung a weight of
about thirty tons upon those pins—what result would you expect
in a short time ? Would you expect the pins to remain firm and
tight ?
For one thing, each pin is tightly jammed into its hole, and
thus, acting like a wedge, tends to burst the plank asunder.
For the next thing, the tremendous strain on the pins makes
them like so many levers, ripping the plank apart lengthwise.
But suppose, in addition, some man comes along regularly and
twists those pins backwards and forwards with a tool.
They won’t be long in getting loose, wiil they ?
Now, what if that plank, instead of being wood, were made of
solid, tough, homogeneous iron ; and the pins are merely put into
the holes, not hammered in? The thirty-ton strain, or even double
or treble that strain, will not have much effect, will it?—and,
seeing that the pins are case-hardened steel working in smooth
metal bearings, the aforesaid man may twist them about a few
thousand times without materially loosening them.

Fig. 2.—-A nearer view of the wooden wrest-plank of the piano
shown in Fig. 1. The first layer of the wrest-plank has been
removed. Note how the timber has split under the strain of
the wires. This is an average wooden wrest-plank, neither
better nor worse than is usually found in imported pianos. All
pianos with wooden wrest-planks are, of course, subject to the
above fault, which cannot be remedied except at great expense.

Now, all this applies to pianos.
The wooden plank, with its two hundred
and sixteen wedge-levers, is in imported pianos,
because neither British nor foreign manu-
facturers have as yet invented a thoroughly
practical metal wrest-plank.
The cast-iron wrest-plank, or plate, with
its steel pins, is in all Beale High-Grade
Pianos/because Beale & Co. are the first piano-
makers to invent a proper, reliable and effective
metal wrest-plate, a part of the usual iron frame.

will serve the purposes of those who would like always to have a reliable
instrument with them, but who find it neither expedient nor desirable to be
encumbered with heavier apparatus.
The V.P.K. is so diminutive that it fits easily into the average waistcoat
pocket, so accurate and reliable that the expert will revel in its use, so
simple that the beginner will experience no difficulty whatsoever in securing
successful results at the first attempt.

game shooting, nothing can beat the Winchester 1906
Repeater. It shoots three cartridges of different sizes,

The Bulletin’s “Trypliena” of yore,
who was converted into the Lady of
Doughty, has been telling a Canadian in-
terviewer that the Australians makes plea-
sure their pursuit, even as Canadians chase
the almighty dollar. But they are “not a
nation of idlers,” she hastened to explain.
Their “native gaiety of heart, and an almost
gypsy-like spirit of care-free content and
happiness” is the result of a healthful liver
due to a sufficiency of toil. “Nothing,” said
Tryphena to the historian —“nothing is
greater proof of the existence of workers
than the fact that the Labor party is now in
power, and that under Sir George Reid
changes have been already effected in the
Commonwealth tending to the increase of
wages, with, however, a consequent advance
on the price of food and other products.”
Which astounding testimonial to the politi-
cal achievements of George Reid, together
with the “with, however,” reference to
food, and a subsequent declaration of be-
lief in “women property-holders having the
franchise, indicates that The Bulletin’s
former penlady is now viewing the universe
through English pince-nez glasses. She
and her own particular member of the
House of Commons returned from Canada
to London last month, with the intention of
leaving in November for Australia.
The stylish brand of lady typist is a glad
but distracting sight, especially on Satur-
day mornings when she has an afternoon
appointment in view. Then she swims hap-
pily to her plain key-thumping job in garden-
party rig, confident that she will stun the
boss and male staff with her splendor. But
if the severe lead of a London firm is fol-
lowed, this sort of joy will be ruled out, and
plain duds on the tailor-made model worn
in place of alluring chiffons, transparent
necks and hobble skirts. Also, the immense
hat will be seen no more, bangles and ear-
rings will be disqualified, and puffed hair,
high heels and buckled shoes be considered
a sort of bid for dismissal. Presumably
these vanities are considered to be destruc-
tive to the working capacity of the male
staff, and the emotions of their female rela-
tives. As a general rule, the sniffs of the
boss’s consort, the manager’s wife, and the
head clerk’s missus when visiting the office
are quite undeserved. The boss furtively
curses the irritating jingle of his steno-
grapher-queen’s bracelets (which he did not
give her), and wishes she would pay less
attention to her hair and more to her work ;
the oueen herself is glad to get out of his
dejir. sing company: the his last
letter is dictated; and the staff, having had
various rows with her, and knowing her as
well as if she was sister to all of them,
scoffs at her best clothes, and wonders idly
who her bloque is. Once in a thousand
times an unmarried clerk offers her his name
and half his salary. Then she becomes one
of the office wives, and, on her occasional
visits, frowns Jin a qurious, disapproving way
at the bent head a fid blouse-buttons of the
new girl. Women' are suspicious creatures.
t
A country club will presently be batched
at Gisborne, to serve as an out-of-town ren-
dezvous for motorists and golfers. The
younger set are objecting to the chosen situ-
ation, on the ground that there are no nice
walks in the vicinity. The objection, how-
ever, has been turned down, as the place is
intended purely as an amusement depot, and
not as an engagement ground. A man will
be able to take his missus to the club, and
the missus everywhere is mightily pleased
about it. But the innovation is regarded
dubiously by a number of male brutes. Still,
in the matter of the membership list, the
men are the more tolerant; it is the women
who are lodging protests by the dozen
against the admittance of the other cat. The
name of Linlithgow has been written on the
scroll of patrons. I believe the locality lin-
gers fondly in his memory on account of a
fall down a dam.
It is about time the white nations fixed
up a firm understanding to discourage the
occasional nigger potentate who leaves his
native swamp and goes travelling in search
of a white wife to boss his harem. A coal-
black East African prince is now strolling
through America, accompanied by body-
guard, valet and interpreter, publishing the
specifications of the lady he desires. In a
country which knows and dislikes unfried
niggers, he is liable to need his bodyguard
at any moment. But a lot of women, from
star actresses downwards, suffer from
hysterics, so the rich savage should be firmly
repressed. An interesting sidelignt on this
Love’s pilgrimage is that it apparently did
not include the Fatherland, though it ex-
tended over England and France. I don’t
know whether he was discouraged by the
looks of the German girls or of the German
Government. More likely to be the latter
than the former.
Ever since the brief, original cable came
through, I have been waiting patiently for
some further particulars about the escape
of that foreign baroness from a lunatic asy-
lum by means of an air balloon —presumably
one of the hot-air variety used by the para-
chutist showman. Compared to a balloon,
an elephant is an easy thing to smuggle and
conceal; consequently, I cannot understand
how the baroness managed things. Even
supposing that it was carted into the asy-
lum grounds for her, inflating a gasbag as big
as a suburban church is one of the most
troublesome jobs possible; and holding it
whilst in process of inflation generally pro-
vides occupation for a wide circle of helpers.
Then a balloon in the air with someone
hanging to it is always liable to attract
attention; and if the attached passenger is
suspected of being a lunatic, or if the bag
floats out of a place where lunatics are
numerous, the amount, of interest excited is
likely to be stupendous. Anyhow, the fact
that a lady goes up attached to a balloon is
a good indication that she is not a common
groundling; and is, therefore, worth arrest-
ing or interviewing immediately she drops
back to earth. I want the particulars of
that escape story more than a new race hat
—and that is saying a heap.
Yours affectionately,
JONANNA.

THE DRAPERS OF QUEENSLAND.
BRISBANE.
IN
BANANA LAND
Governor Macgregor, on presenting a pic-
ture of certain royalties to a State school:—
■ There were some curious stories connected witli
the name of Queen Victoria. When she was pre-
sented for baptism the Emperor of Russia undertook
to be sponsor, and made a condition that if he
were to be godfather the child should be called Alex-
andnna, and she was so baptised. The Regent, after-
wards George IV., then suggested the name Victoria
should be added. They would see that the latter
name was continued down in the royal family, and
the young lady they noticed on the picture was
named Victoria Alexandrina Alice Marv The
name selected for her was Mary, so they would
know her as the Princess Mary. He did not know
how far they were taught history in the schools, but
it was an important study. .. .
Most important, in fact. Indeed, one doesn’t
know how Young Australia can possibly
expect to push, along in the world without
giving grave consideration to such tremem
dous matters as those kindly mentioned by
his Excellency.
C. G. Corten, who died the other day in
Brisbane, was’ something of a pioneer. * He
didn’t arrive with the first ship, or fight at
Eureka, or dig the first nugget at Gympie,
but shortly after he arrived in Queensland
from Cape Colony in 1875 he fixed upon
Cawarral, which leans up against Rock-
hampton, and grew tobacco. He erected
drying rooms, and Jiis first harvest yielded
him five tons of finished “smoke.” A
tobacco factory was a curio in those days,
and men went far out of their way to “see it.
Since then, the district has grown back-
wards, and now it hasn’t any factory at
all. Later, Corten tried the same business
at Toowoomba, and finally retired to while
away his time in inventing things. The
market has one or two of liis ideas about
its counters now. It would have had an-
other. only Corten died and left the job
unfinished.
A TWINGE IN HIS SOUTH-EAST
TENTACLE.
Capital was so sensitive that it did not matter
what part of the world abused its rights, a feeling of
insecurity immediately followed. —Solemn warning of
a returned Sydneyite from Europe.
Times have changed, and archbishops have
changed with them. His Grace of Brisbane
lately acted as a sort of spruiker for an
impending tea-meeting, holding forth as fol-
lows : “This will be a real knife and fork
meal—not a sandwich and sausage roll
affair.”
Charming extract from the report of Gov-
ernment Analyst Henderson: —
Out of two million gallons of (alleged) milk sup-
plied annually to Brisbane and suburbs, 62,400 gal-
lons were water of a dubious quality. With sd. per
quart as the average price, this represents £ 5200
paid by the public for water, of which less than
one-twelfth was recovered in fines.... Not only is
the lacteal fluid poisoned with dirty water, but the
legally “pure” milk is itself dirty enough to cause
a large loss of infant life much larger than the loss
caused by mere adulteration.
You have at least this choice in Brisbane:
you can either let your baby be poisoned
by dirty adulterated milk, or by dirty non-
adulterated milk. In the former case, you
buy water for milk; in the latter, you pur-
chase many more disease germs and a higher
rate of mortality. On the whole, the dis-
graceful case seems to pan out in favor of
the adulterated article.
ARTHUR MORGAN, Lieutenant-Governor
and President of Leg. Council (Q.).
Tom Welsby, M.L.A., who is a brewery
chairman among a, host of other things,
wants to make Prohibition prohibit, or else
have the thing thrown' overboard as a fraud.
“Prohibition areas” were proposed in the
new Liquor Bill; and when Welsby couldn’t
block them, he moved that, when such an
area was established by vote, nobody should
be allowed to therein “make, brew, distil,
import, carry, keep,Jhave possession of, con-
sume, buy, deliver, sell, supply, barter or
otherwise dispose of any liquor.” But Digby
Denham wasn’t carrying things to that logi-
cal conclusion; so his Home Secretary threw
it out with a plaintive appeal to the brewery
chairman to be serious.
With the loss of Port Curtis the Queens-
land Labor party comes down to 26 in a
House of 72, thus giving Denham a clear
majority of 20 as against eight in the pre-
ceding Parliament. Of course, a party that
is so confident of its hold on the country
that it can afford to get rid pf 25 of its
members at various times will view the
tripling of the Conservative majority with
perfect equanimity. Probably, also, the next
general election will be ushered in by a
fresh general strike, ,to the great joy of the
intransigent wing of Labor and the lively
exultation of the lurking Liberals.
A Criminal Appeal Bill is on its way
through the Bananaland Legislature. Like
the N. S. Wales meastire, it is closely
modelled on the Act which has been such
a success in the Cold Land. Accused will
have the right to appeal on a question of
law. or of fact, or of 'mixed law and fact—
subject, of course, to the Court granting
him permission. The water in the beer, as
one might say, from the bona-fide criminal’s
standpoint, is that, as a result of appealing,
he is as liable to have his*sentence increased
as he is to have it lessened or wiped out
altogether. The experience of Britain goes
to show that Hell hath no fury like the
malefactor who has had his demand for a
rebate of sentence not merelv scorneu, but
met with the cruel words: “Take 18 months
extra for luck.”
Gypsy Blood.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The long white road is calling me,
The plains and uplands green,
The open, lawless sky o’erliead.
The distant silver sheen.
The old wild life is wooing me,
And all my pulses set —
Hot tides at flood —to those far fields
Of freedom and regret.
With soft, kind arms you draw me near;
White love, your eyes are blue,
And to the dark of mine they ask.
Were all our vows untrue?
Ah, vain, that look of sad reproach,
You know, e’er we were wed,
The reckless gypsy in my blood
Slumbered, but was not dead.
With passion’s strength you hold me fast,
Your dear eyes wild with pain,
While, through the world a vagrant wind
Whistles its mad refrain.
It calls me like a living thing,
Its songs my senses load.
Oh, hold me, stay my feet, dear God—
They’re aching for the road !
QUEENSLAND.
A. GORE-JONES.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, November 9,. 1912.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—-
Wulliam Macgreegor, locum tenens for
George V., entertained the University pro-
fessors and Mrs. professors, plus an un-
limited quantity of student, at Fernberg
yesterday as a sort of thanksgiving service
on the close of term. A heavy thunderstorm
won the tennis games, so the majority of the
guests turned to billiards, boiled tea, cigars,
scandal and cakes and ale generally. It
was a pleasant, homely afternoon, and the
bright, smiling countenances of aide Foxton
and secretary Bytli were like the twin
moons that light Queen Frivol’s throne in
Paradise.
The races on Saturday attracted large
flocks of the fluttering Gamble Moth to the
Flame of Chance, and great was the scorch-
ing of financial wings. One well-known
Society butterfly was perfectly white-hot.
She has more than a passing interest in a
speedy animal, and, on “information from
the stable,” scurried round the lawn putting
her pals wise to the trainer’s alleged state-
ment that the gee-gee hadn’t a whatsitsname
chance. And how did the band play when
the brute romped home first!
Morag Jackson, a daughter of the well-
known medical Jackson, has acceded to the
matrimonial request (by cable) of Claude
Roe, who is at present at Oxford. The
plucky man is a son of the present Director
of Education, and erstwhile head of the
Brisbane Boys’ Grammar School.
Mary Proctor, the little astronomer-lady,
who gives a lecture here next Wednesday,
is making hosts of friends. She lunched
with the Macgregor chieftain on 'Tuesday,
and good impressions were apparently
mutual. The Chiel will preside at her
lecture.
The Muffs, an amateur dramatic club,
have taken the plunge into professionalism.
With Walter Tliurlow as ringleader, they
appeared as “Black and White Pierrots,” on
Guy Fawkes night. It was a reasonably
good show, which is no faint praise in these
days of stage duffers.
At Lylia Fegan’s concert in the- South
Brisbane Technical College Hall last Tues-
day, most of the musically-inclined of Bris-
bane were congregated. The overcrowding was
due to a rumor (which proved to be correct)
that the lady would play Romeo to Miss
Alice Crowther’s Juliet. Later in the even-
ing, Miss Fegan resumed feminine garb (or
some of it), and became Cleopatra. There
wasn’t any Antony, but there were eight
maids-in-waiting. Mrs. Gilbert Wilson (ex-
quisitely frocked and in perfect voice),
Mr. W. Crisp, and Mr. W. E. Sclilusener
were also in the programme; and Miss
Lilian Clancey coaxed accompaniments
from the piano.
Dr. Lilian Cooper and Miss Bedford gave
addresses to a packed meeting of the Na-
tional Council of Women last night. The
Dr.-lady is hot and strong on the suffragette
question, having been, inoculated while in
England, and the conclusion she arrives at
is that the militant window-wallopers and
disorderly shriekers should be given the
rights they demand. For myself, Thargo-
mindah, I’d sooner stick to a woman’s sure
privileges than chance woman’s doubtful
rights. I don’t know that we’d like them
when we got them. All the same, Miss
Cooper’s paper was interesting and clever.
Miss Bedford described institutions she had
noticed in her travels which might be of
benefit in Australia. The Women’s Ex-
change, in Sap Francisco, which gives edu-
cated women d chance of earning their own
crust and butter, and city playgrounds for
jthe kiddies, were the main contents of her
box of observations.
Willard House, where the National Coun-
cil of Women holds its meetings, is just
opposite the Anglican Cathedral, and last
night was the night for the bellringers to
practice. They didn’t overlook it. Three
times the lecturer was obliged to cease read-
ing her paper, and two deputations were
chased into the night, one to grapple with
the bell nuisance, and the other to beg, bor-
row or steal another hall. The latter p t arty
was eventually successful, but by the time
it returned the jangling of the bells was a
curse of the past, so the meeting was not
compelled to go elsewhere to deliberate on
how the country is to be Saved.
Mr. and Mrs. Penleigh Boyd, who are
honeymooning in Italy, will most likely re-
turn to Australia next April. Mrs. Phillips
Fox, who chaperoned Miss Anderson before
her marriage, had a portrait of Penleigh
Boyd in the Paris (Autumn) Salon.
Major Tuson, son of an old officer of the
Union Bank in Queensland, was married in.
London lately to the widow Lowe, the daugh-
ter of a family which wears a hyphen half-
way through its name. The Tusons are still
represented hereabouts by the banker’s
widow, who owns quite her share of this little
bit of Australia, majah is, or was, in
the 16th Lancers.
Yours in a scatter of ink,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Riders of locally-assqinbled cycles, built of the
famous B.S.A. fittings, have again practically swept
the board in the big Australasian road races held
this season. They secured fastest times and cham-
pionships each in the Beverley-Perth (116 miles),
.E. M. Gray; Victorian Gycle Traders’ (100 miles),
G. W. Bell; Goulburn-Sydney (131 miles), A. C.
Walcott ; Burra-Adelaide (110 miles), Don Kirkham ;
Dunlop Gold Oup race (116 miles), 0. Snell; and
Timaru-CHristchurch (112 miles), H. Henderson.
They also secured first, with one exception, second
and third, and the bulk of the specials in each race.
Altogether, B.S.A. riders won prizes value over
£lOOO in the above races alone, wonderful testimony
to the speed qualities and staunchness of their
mounts.*
Mr. A. G. Hales was born in Australia in 1860,
but has lived for a great many years in England,
where he is well known on account of the bril-
liant work lie did as War Correspondent for the
London Daily News in South Africa, Macedonia,
and during the Russo-Japanese War. He has lec-
tured throughout England, America, South Africa and
Australia. Besides being a poet and a novelist, Mr.
Hales has followed mining for years, and has trav-
elled extensively in search of sport and adventure.
Few brain workers have led such an active and
strenuous life, and it is therefore interesting to
learn that Mr. Hales is a great admirer of the well-
known tonic food, Sanatogen. He writes: “I have
used Sanatogen and find myself considerably im-
proved in general health. I am working late and
long under considerable pressure, and find that my
brain benefits by every tin of this nerve tonic that I
take; it revitalises a man and is not a drug. No
brain worker should be without it.”*
An official return just tabled shows that
the Queensland Government spent £5520
last year on lawyers, of which sum A.-G.
Tom O’Sullivan received £llO7 and J. W.
Blair £257 —quite a fair cut for two
eminent legislators.' Verily, loyalty brings
its own reward.
THE SOUL OF WIT.
“ What is the best after-dinner speech you ever
heard, John? ”
“ Allow me, I’ll pay.’”
The Brisbane Telegraph, generally re-
puted to be an all-wool Wowser organ, is
exceedingly bitter against the Denham Gov-
ernment’s Liquor Bill—the pet measure of
Brisbane Wesleyanism. The Telegraph
urges a broad view and an ignoring of fad-
dists, an attitude which precisely corres-
ponds with that of Mr. Bung himself. Beer
has no lack of potent missionaries.
Rockhampton had a rather unique gath-
ering the other day, when an enthusiast —
Andrew Small, of Clermont —mustered his
shipmates of the Eutopia. The Eutopia
was the first immigrant ship to call direct to
Keppel Bay. where in November, 1862 —50
years ago—it landed 324 new-cliums. After
a lapse of half a century, 23 of these cld
inhabitants gathered and told each other
what gay young dogs they once were. By
the way, the Eutopia was beaten out to
Central Queensland parts by the Persia,
which arrived in 1861 with 454 “chummies”
aboard. It, however, went to Port Curtis.
Rockhampton thereupon petitioned vigor-
ously, and during the next half-hundred
years most things have come its way instead
of going to the rival, Gladstone.
“Hugh Enden” discovers a family vault:
Sixty years ago there was a place in Central
Queensland called Gladstone. It consisted of 30
men, a dozen or so women, a few cows, a fine, harbor
and a dog. The place is still there, with its general
features unchanged, though a few graves adorn its
cemetery and Mary has ceased to call the cattle home
from across the salt-pans. The dog is dead also.
There was a street in Gladstone called Gondoon-
street. With a few shops and half a dozen groggeries
it’s the old place still. But its name has changed.
It is now Gonbung-street.
Gladstone will always be the same. The dead hand
holds it tight. Trade is dead, the water in its harbor
is dead. Now and again the hearse rattles over its
cobbles to the cemetery. Nothing else moves.
The North Coast railway runs through the town,
and every train brings a load of people who. when
they gaze on the ruin and think what it might have
been, shed bucketsful of salt tears. The Government
provides the buckets.

ABRIDGED PROSPECTUS
McLucas Gold Mines
(NO LIABILITY).
CAPITAL £lO,OOO in 40,000 Shares of ss.
each, of which 8000 Shares are Fully Paid
Up and 32,000 Shares are Contributing, pay-
able Gd. per Share on Application, the bal-
ance of 4s. Gd. is payable by way of calls as
may be required, but not to exceed Two
Pence per Share per month.
McLUCAS GOLD MINES (locally
known as Blaekfellow Reef) are situated at
the head of Pikes Creek, 25 miles N.W. from
Dalveen Railway Station, containing 20
acres leasehold. Crushings have given as
high as Boz. per ton, with an average of
3oz. Bdwt. for 200 tons. This is
verified by recent Assays giving Boz. Bdwt.
per ton. About 3000 tons mixed quartz have
been raised and stacked at the mine. A
Report and Survey, by Mr. F. W. Thomson.
Mining Surveyor and Engineer, is attached
to the Prospectus obtainable from members
of Stock Exchanges, or E. Steele, Secretary
pro tem., Ruthven Street, Toowoomba. The
Provisional Directors are: Messrs. D.
Sheahan, Sen., Stanthorpe ; and G. Critten-
den, R. Rosenstengel, J. Sunderland, H. C.
Pointer and G. Feldmann, of Toowoomba.

PRIVATE SUBDIVISIONS. SALE
To FARMERS, GRAZIERS, WHEAT-
GROWERS, and OTHERS.
“CARUMBI ESTATE,”
About 30,000 ACRES of First-class
MIXED FARMING, WHEAT, and
SHEEP Country.
In Farms From About
300 to 800 Acres,
ON LONG TERMS OF TEN YEARS.
WS. KEAST, Auctioneers, 608-10
• Collins Street, Melbourne, have
received instructions from the vendors,
to
SUBDIVIDE and SELL PRIVATELY,
THAT MAGNIFICENT PROPERTY,
“CARUMBI ESTATE,”
Containing 30,000 ACRES, situated
in the FAMOUS BLAND COUN-
TRY, on the new line of railway
from STOCKINBINGAL t o
FORBES, and close to the main
line between SYDNEY and MEL-
BOURNE.
The property consists of fine
MIXED FARMING, WHEAT
and LAMB-RAISING country,
subdivided into fifty-four farms,
from about 300 to 800 acres (the
homestead with about 3000
acres). The whole of the coun-
try is suitable for wheat, oats,
and lucerne, and 200 acres of
well-established lucerne is now
growing without irrigation.
IMPROVEMENTS.
The estate is in perfect order, and about 20,000
acres have been grubbed lOin. deep, and cleared
ready for the set plough. Sufficient timber and fire-
wood have been left on each block. About 600 acres
of pine have been left on the property to provide
timber for building purposes, and arrangements can
be made with the vendors accordingly.
FENCING.
The fencing is all in first-class order. Most of the
subdivisional fencing is new. Only the best materials
and netting have been used; and all posts and
strainers and gate-posts are sunk to exceptional
depths. Inspection will prove this, and will show
that most of the farms are* already fenced.
WATER.
It is certainly one of the very best watered proper-
ties in New South Wales. In addition to the Bland
River, the Narraburra Creek, and other creeks, there
are, perhaps, more tanks than on any similar
property. Most of these tanks are new, and their
holding capacity undoubted. The capacity runs up
to over 10,000 yards, and provision for water is
made on each farm now offered.
HOMESTEAD BLOCK, 3000 ACRES.
Special attention of stud breeders and others is
drawn to this. The improvements are nearly all
new, and constructed of brick. They are too numer-
ous to detail here, but mention may be made of the
commodious ten-roomed residence with exceptionally
wide verandahs. Large brick office and store, the
convenient and well-planned men’s quarters (also in
brick), machinery-shed, stables, stock yards, wool-
shed (with large brick dip), shearers’ quarters, gar-
dener’s cottage (brick), and piggeries.
REMARKS.
The timber is principally box and pine, and out
of the whole estate it is estimated that only about
200 acres are unsuitable for farming. The climate
is salubrious, soil is first-class, and everything is in
perfect order. The estate has been so subdivided as
to give a chance to every class of settler, and the
desire of the owners is to make it one of th<\ best
settlements in New South Wales.
LIBERAL TERMS, of £1 per acre down, and bal-
ance up to TEN YEARS.
Further particulars apply
W. S. KEAST, auctioneers, 608-10 Collins Street,
Melbourne; or
Our representative, D. THOMPSON, Carumbi Es-
tate, Stockinbingal, N.S.W., who is on the Coota-
rnundra and Stockinbingal Telephone Exchange.
Solicitors—Messrs. Williams and Matthews, Queen
Street, Melbourne.

WILL OF ANDREW INKSTER, deceased, late of
Yodda Valley, Papua, and afterwards of Ingle-
burn, in the State of New South Wales. WHEREAS
by his Will dated the 15th day of November 1909
Andrew Inkster (otherwise known as Andrew L.
Inkster) who died on the twenty-eighth day of July
One thousand nine hundred and ten gave and be-
queathed all his estate to his mother sisters and
brother to be divided equally amongst those still
living. AND WHEREAS Letters of Administration
with the said Will annexed were granted to
FREDERICK WALLIN by the Supreme Court of
New South Wales in its Probate Jurisdiction on the
second day of August One thousand nine hundred and
eleven, NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that all
creditors and other persons having claims against the
estate of the said Andrew Inkster whether under the
said Will or otherwise are required to send in their
claims to the undersigned' on or before the thirty-
first day of March One thousand nine hundred and
thirteen in default whereof the said estate will be
distributed amongst the parties entitled thereto with-
out regard to any claims of which no notice has been
given.
WILLIAM ALFRED GILDER,
Proctor, 92 Pitt Street,
Sydney, N. S. Wales.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound mag-ni-
ne l, to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
a '" e - one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain oft' them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend . _
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever . you . wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
T' le price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also ail
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on i trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at, once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t deiay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you

Scalp, Body, Hands, Limbs Covered
with Scales. Could Not Stand Tor-
ture. Cured by Cuticura Remedies.
“About three years ago I was attacked
with a very severe itching and burning all
over my body and finally my skin broke
gf
until my body looked like
a piece of raw meat. I did
believe that I could not
stand the torture any
longer, while all the time
I was using the salve and
the wash ordered by my
physician without relief.
When I was advised to go
to a skin specialist I went
with no better results. My
body was covered with
large white scales, with
scales on my hands, arms
and lower limbs. In about one week my
scalp was covered with scales which looked
like dandruff but became worse each day
until at the end of another week the scales
were as large as on a fish. The itch in my
head was unbearable. My hair was coming
out in combfuls until it looked so bad I was
ashamed to go on the street.
“I used tar soap for a shampoo but it
took no effect. At the end of three weeks

Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. I
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

The Inky Way.
“Laborite”: Australian writers who try to better
their working conditions are familiar with the threat
that “selected instead of original matter” will be
used, and that they will thereby be bumped, bashed,
or chucked. Now comes J. H. Catts. According to
Catts (B. 31/10/’l2), the matter written by his sub-
editor, with a view to making the paper look like a
paper, “was never asked for, and it wouldn’t affect
the sale of a single paper if it were dropped out, and
selected, instead of original, matter were inserted
in its stead.” This country is so cursed by thievish
prints thfet perhaps one more wouldn’t matter much;
but what must we think of a “Labor” politician
with sufficient hide to dream of dropping a chief
employee’s original work for the everlasting clip?
The political platform on which Catts gains any
eminence he may possess advocates that the earth
should be made a decent place for workers, and if
there’s a harder worker than a man who has to do
the chief part towards filling a fair-sized weekly, I’d
like to know where he is. Mr. Catts was present,
and spoke at various sittings of the N. S. Wales
Labor conference of 1911, which conference passed
more than one motion favoring the support by the
Labor movement of Australian writers and artists.
If he feels that Australian writers are so useless and so
easily to be done without, why didn’t he arise at
that time and point out that Labor didn’t want
writers? I’d like to hear this advocate of cheap
clipped matter give his opinion on the Australian
shopkeeper who offered “selected” instead of original
boots for sale. Yet, as a fact, there isn’t much dif-
ference between boots that are the product of suc-
cessful burglary and the average “lifted” newspaper
matter.
* * * *
“T.C.8.”: N.S.W. country pressmen who did the
Sydney Harbor trip with Speaker Willis the other
day were shocked to their last whisker to discover
the sort of man he really is. The keeper of order
unbent and told the Inky Wayback that “he never
smiled when in the chair, though he sometimes
laughed when he vacated it.” I. Wayback has now
returned to the bush with a fresh idea to mix with
his Liberalism. He has discovered that Willis is
merely a polite gentleman guilty of the crime of re-
garding himself as having a mission to put down
political larrikinism of the Fitzperry and Cohen-
wood sort.
* * * *
Australian newspaper scribes who would like to
write “M.J.” after their names —“Master of Jour-
nalism”—must emigrate to the beautiful City of
Cork, in the South of Ireland. Cork University Col-
lege has just established a Faculty of Journalism,
in which a degree may be gained after a three-year
course. But, alas, “the Irish language is compul-
sory for both degree and matriculation exams.,” so
the average journalist will Do Without It, and sign
himself D.W.I. if he wants to. And D.W.I. will
probably do him just as much good.
* * * *
From London: —Cardinal Moran still showers
“copy” upon the sphere which he formerly inha-
bited. A new volume of the “Catholic Cyclopedia”
has just been published in N’w York and London.
It contains a lengthy article, signed by the Car-
dinal, on Dr. Peter Talbot, Archbishop of Dublin in
the 17th century, who passed through stormy times
and died a prisoner in Dublin Castle. This new
Cyclopedia has also an article on the Archdiocese of
Sydney by Monsignor O’Haran. It is nearly all
about the Cardinal and what he did in Sydney.
“He consecrated 14 bishops, ordained 500 priests,
dedicated more than 500 churches and professed
5000 nuns.” O’Haran might have added, “And
made 50,000 Wowsers mighty uncomfortable.” Of
course the biographer claims for the Cardinal many
things that I’ll have to ask Dill Macky about the
next time we meet at the Gaiety.
* * * *
“Sir Octopus”: Apropos of the late Tom Beasley,
manufacturer of topical verses; bespoke goods a
specialty. About a quarter of a century ago, when
the old tub Brightside was about the most interest-
ing feature of the Manly ferry service, Tom had
charge of the refreshment ioint on the pier. He
could not put two words together nearly as well as
he could make a sandwich. A little later he became
a mummer under Gen. Booth, and at the Salv’army
Theatre was wont to tell how he was saved. When
I met him organising in the West I could scarcely
credit either eyes or ears. Several times I heard
and saw him at work, and he could sway a mob
wonderfully. “Where did we meet last?” he asked
me one night. “When you were blithering to the
accompaniment of a big drum on the Corso,” I re-
sponded. “Ah, that’s where I got the necessary con-
fidence,” he said. “I never could spout before.”
Booth’s brigade lost a rare fish when it let Beasley
slip through its fingers. He was a specialist in con-
version, if the Salvs. had only known it.
* * * *
The Australian Journalists’ Association has de-
cided to publish an inter-State craft paper. It will
be named The Australian Journalist, and the first
issue is clue towards the end of November. Publi-
cation will be monthly ; the size will be 16 pages;
and the circulation well over 1000 —probably well
over 1500.

BABBLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Often I seek excuses for a verse;
For verse means lucre, lucre means a meal;
Meals are the best excuses to rehearse
The movements moral processes reveal.
For moral processes are like bargees,
Threading the alimentary canal,
Bringing a noble purpose to your knees,
Bricks to your brains, and fuel there withal.
I said “excuses,” but I meant a theme,
To make a babble over Thought’s dry rocks;
And now I am embarked upon a stream
Where runs no current, but about the locks.
Which is the better, there may be some doubt,
A torrent rushing in a stony bed,
Saving some sinners from the ills of drought;
Or water-highways, bringing beggars bread.
So, for a bread and water rhyme at last;
Fuel’s for fools, philosophers require
Only the elements to break their fast,
Wind for their wisdom, sunshine for their fire.
Wind to excite their wits and bear the seed
Into their neighbor’s patch; the sun to rouse
Those common properties the neighbors need
To fill the udders of their milking cows.
Softly! The bread and water’s turned to milk!
Food and utility confound us all;
Cloth and philosophy give way to silk,
Sauces and luxury—and then, the fall!
Pharaoh constructs canals —the railway comes;
Olives and bees make honey, wax and oil;
Scotch engineers make work, and Egypt hums
With Cooks and Cockneys, tickets, toys and toil.
So, let me drowse beside my mountain stream.
Bother my neighbors’ cows! Perdition catch
The Scotch surveyor, who would chain my dream
And plant a station in my poppy patch!
And now for dinner. Are the lentils done?
And are the wine-flasks full, the olives good?
Throw wide the window open to the sun!
The rhyme of life begins and ends in food!
MAJORCA.

MAN AND WIFE CURED
SUFFERED FROM LIVER DISORDER AND BILIOUSNESS: DROWSY,
OUT-OF-SORTS. AND TORTURED BY CHEST PAINS.
Bile Beans are the Best
Spring Medicine.
At this season the human system needs toning-uj I
and putting in thoroughly healthy condition. If this’
is not done, the body, through lack of vitality to stand
the altered climatic conditions, may fall a victim to
one or other of the complaints that endangered the
health of Mr. E. P. Lambert, of 7 Denham Street,
Sydney, who, sneaking also on behalf of his wife, says:
“Myself and wife were both victims of biliousness and
indigestion. Often, while working on the wharf,
vomiting fits and bilious attacks would completely
prostrate me, and while these bouts lasted all strength left
me. Terrible chest pains intensified my suffering; my
liver was sluggish, which caused me to feel drowsy and
thoroughly worn out, while there was always an unpleasant
taste in my mouth. My wife suffered in a like manner
with wind round the heart; she was reduced to an awful
state, and her health completely shattered. Both of us
tried various remedies, but all our efforts were in vain.
“At last my wife procured a supply of Bile Beans, and we
both started taking them with verv gratifying results, a.
few doses relieved us both of the awful pains and sickly
sensations. The bilious attacks grew less and finally ceased, lassitude
disappeared, and vitality and energy took its place. A full course of Bile
Beans completely cured us, and now we are both able to eat practically
anything, whereas before we were only able to partake of a very limited
diet.
“My daughter has also derived much benefit from Bile Beans, which are
a splendid liver medicine which no home should be without.”
f Nature’s Remedy—Purely Vegetable.)

sluggish organs to natural activity, purify the blood, and end all weakness. Bile
Beans arc so gentle—yet searching—that they can be given to the most delicate
youngster. Girls just entering womanhood will prevent much unnecessary pain and
suffering by taking a short ■course of Itile Beans. A bar should be kept in every

The Jamboree.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’T ONGSIDE the Desert track that
I i leads up North from Broken ’lll,
Unsightly in the glarin’ ’eat, we found
the bones o’ Bill.
’E wasn’t good to gaze upon, that lonesome
human shell,
But neither men nor manners keep upon the
Roof of ’Ell !
We yanked him fin a saddle-front, we dragged
’im many a mile
(An’ once or twice I could ’a’ swore I seen
ole William smile!)
We bore ’im on the saddle-front, ’is rags an’
bones a-rub,
Until we came, at set o’ sun, to “Piker”
Doolan’s pub.
Ole “Piker” didn’t look surprised ; ole “Piker”
didn’t stare ;
’E simply said: “It seems to me that it is
’ardly fair
To yank along a Rechabite—a sober looker-
on.
Whose thirst ’as passed before the Lord;
whose spendin’ days are gone !”
“Oh ! stow yer gaff, for ’Eaven’s sake,” young
Jack McCluskey said.
“The man that drinks your whisky best, ’e
drinks it when Vs dead ;
For then ’e don’t wake up to see wild devils
red and blue,
Nor squirmin’ snakes —nor ask the Lord to
bring a curse on you.”
Seven men was in our party, an’ the seventh
man was Bill,
When seven most outrageous thirsts we
started out to fill.
L’pon the counter Bill we propped, ’longside
a. cask o’ beer,
(An’ once or twice I could ’a’ swore I ’eard
ole William cheer !)
Ole Bill looked disappointed like when we our
poisons named ;
For not to drink when others drink is cause
to be ashamed !
But ’e, ole cuss! ’e didn’t know ’ow wise ’is
mates could be:
We simply said: “Ole William, ’ere, shall
’ave ’is jamboree!”
Though Bill was dead, an’ passed away, an’
ready for the vault—
We knew, as well as anyone, it wasn’t Wil-
liam's fault.
For any man is apt to die; but, when he
passes out,
Why should ’e ’urry to the Land of Ever-
lastin’ Drought?
Six rounds o’ drinks we ’ad (our necks
seemed filled with burnin’ lime !) -
An’ right in front of Bill we set his whisky
ev’ry time !
For Bill a drinkin’ comrade was —an’ when
a comrade dies.
Why should ’e be a temp’rance crank —or
guzzle with the flies?
Then Mulga Mick grabs ’old o’ Bill, an’
cutely found an’ felt
A most auspicious spongy wad inside the
corpse’s belt.
Then from a little chamois bag ’e pulls a
fiver out—
“No Jimmy Woodsers ’ere,” sez ’e; “it’s
William’s turn to shout!”
Ole Bill, what time ’e lived on earth, asked
not to be excused—
When ’e was called to join the boys ’e never
once refused!
An’ now because ’is breath was gone, the
tongue ’e lied with, still,
Why should the curse o’ temperance fall,
’eavy upon Bill?
Yes, ’e who dies must grimly bear one long,
tremendous thirst;
For when Vs shoved beneath the sand ’e’s
settled from the first!
’E’s simply got to keep the pledge, an’ stay
for ever dead- —
While all the time ’is bosom churns are sliik-
kered over’ead !
So “Piker” fills ’em up again—not “Piker’s”
round, by ’arf!
(An’ once or twice 1 could ’a’ swore I ’eard
ole William larf!)
“Four-poun’-thirteen,” sez Mulga Mick;
“just see your change is right—
An’, while you stay in ‘Piker’s’ pub, please
keep it out o’ sight.”
The money in the chamois bag Mick well an’
truly placed.
An’ clasped the belt once more around the
corpse’s wizened waist.
An’ then we drank—an’ drank again—until
the dawn o’ day :
And’ for each seventh round o’ drinks the
dead wan ’ad to pay!
We put the drink in front of him. (I
could ’a’ swore ’e said:
“If this was mine two davs ago I wouldn’t
now be dead !”)
But though we drank, an’ smoked an’ fought,
an’ danced, an’ raved, an’ swore.
No man had ever seen ole Bill so sensible
before!
’E was a right-yus citizen — a sort o’ moral
law.
Who never cursed the company, or punched
it on the jaw :
’E never used a swearin’ word, nor yet a
bottle flung,
At someone’s ’ed, when repartee too per-
sonally stung.
’E kept ’is tongue profoundly still; not once
did ’e remark
That Doolan was a ’ighwayman, a vulture,
’ or a shark ;
An’ neither did ’e curl ’is lip, an’ snap the
sneer o’ shame,
To swear that not a man of us went by ’is
father’s name.
No matter what we said or did, an’ thought
we good or ill,
It mattered not a single jot—r’twas all the
same to Bill!
An’ when Kinneally told ’im straight to go
an’ boil ’is ’ead,
’E never sassed—but just remained most
beautifully dead !
We threw the dice an’ dealt the cards, with
each man’s chance the same —
An’ Bill (God rest ’is sinful soul!), ’e played
in ev’ry game!
We sang the old Bush choruses that made
the rafters ring, ,
(An’ once or twice I could a swore I eard
ole William sing !).
We found ten fivers more on Bill, but ’id
them well away —
For ’im to grease ole Peters palm on Resur-
rection Day! ,
An’ we ’ad quids, an’ many quids—an quids
enough to burn ;
But it was only etiquette for Bill to buy in
turn.
We scattered all the fiends of ’Ell ; we let
the Devil loose;
But Bill ’e was a credit to that spirit ous
caboose! ■
An’ though at times 1 could a swore Rood
luck” from ’im I ’eard,
Six men in Doolan’s shanty swore ’e never
spoke a word!
We drank until the devils grew to twice as
many more,
An’ snakes an’ allergators somersaulted on
the floor! _
Jim’s wantegong got into ’olts with “Boko’s”
santerpee—
But Bill contemptuously eyed the whole
menagerie !
We seen the blazin’ Desert sun light up the
brassy skies;
But Bill, ’e never pulled the blinds, an’
never blinked ’is eyes.
’E never ’ad a pick-me-up (just whisky neat,
instead!),
Ah, ’e drinks Doolan’s whisky best who
drinks it when ’e’s dead!
Then one by one the devils went —an’
Jimmy’s wantygong,
With “Boko’s” wrestlin’ santerpee, an’ Jack
McCluskey’s song.
A hush came o’er the pubbery, an’ for a
little while
The only thing that cheered the scene was
“Piker” Doolan’s smile.
But Bill, ’e ’ad no aches nor pains; ’e sat
there just as calm
As one who in the sanctu’ry partakes of
Gilead’s balm.
But ’e was cold to gaze upon, that solemn
sentinel.
Ah, was that night ’is Purgat’ry—or was it
’Ea.ven —or ’Ell?
But what it was ’twas left to us, ’is mourn-
ful friends, to guess :
We only knew that William knew no dreary
loneliness !
We did our best to cheer ’is soul towards
the Golden Stairs —•
Per medium of Doolan’s stock of alcoholic
wares.
Next day we yanked ’im further on, ’is rags
an’ bones a-rub ;
For no man. be ’e drunk or dead, is safe at
“Piker’s” pub.
An’ Bill, ’e was our trusted mate —an’ when
a comrade dies.
Why should ’e fatten “Piker’s” purse, or
guzzle with the flies!

’Tween Drinks.
A VIRTUOUS DECEPTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
‘‘"IVT" O, Crawford,” said Patrick Macka-
-1 hoy, “woman is not m.y pet aver-
sion. Travel and observation have
served to convince me that there is nothing
so like a man as a woman. It’s somewhat
to the credit of the Mackaboy strain, that I
have foregathered in bars with the. ribald
through long, hot summers, and still retain
a lively respect for my little sister.
“Fact is, MdVleekin, the next sex doesn’t
get a fair deal from the habitual potter.
When the wine’s in the woman’s outed. In
the bar-room conversazione Sissie is either a
bad joke or an object of smouldering resent-
ment. If I accepted the judgments handed
out to me here in sheaves, I would be a
stern advocate of the policy of keeping
women in crates.
“As it is, being superior to my surround-
ings, Anson, and immune to the corrupting
influence of evil associations, Cootie, I can
still look myself souarely in the eye, and in-
sist that when woman’s wholly bad she’s just
about half as bad as man.
“Not that I haven’t been jolted off my
pedestal by the sweetest girl in the world
in my regular turn,” said the barman with
a touch of melancholy. “Looking back on
a long and spotted career I recall with some
satisfaction various occasions when the min-
istering angel socked me in the epiglottis
good and hard. I bear no ill-will. In later
years, with keener judgment, enhanced by
time and study, I cheerfully admit it was
coming to me —every hook and jab.”
“There was Dorris,” Patrick Mackaboy
sighed and changed the subject, showing a
bright spurt of coin-manipulation with a
gingei nut.
“About Dorris?” said MeMeekin. “Cut
out the palm mystery. Re the girl Dorris,
when and where? How and why? With
date and place, pertinent details, and such
moral reflections as are positively unavoid-
able—go!”
“Dorris wore a white dress. Her face was
as white. She was sitting in a white enam-
elled wicker chair at a white table, under a
tree clotted with white bloom. Her hair was
Dale gold, and her eyes were two black lairs
in the driven snow —lairs of the devil of
discontent.
“I was an invalid, too. Spots that had
haunted my general outlook for months sud-
denly begot a lean, red imp with spiked knees
and a spinal ridge like a horned toad. I was
supplementing my worries as a barman, mak-
ing a big effort to be an author of sorts at
the time. The doctor said neurasthenia, and
recommended mountain resorts and total ab-
stinence from midnight oil and carousings
with the Muse.
“The place was swung high in the ethereal
blue, on a beetling range. It was surrounded
by cloud-capped towers and gorgeous palaces
of primordial granite. I could welter here in
precipices and peaks and other scenic effects,
but let’s to business.
“To Sweet Springs came many pilgrims,
nerve cases, convalescents, cranks, honey-
mooners, dyspeptics, valetudinarians of all

HOW TIRED I AH!”
No one knows the suffering some women quietly endure. They think it is
only natural for them to have pain in the back, throbbing headaches, dizzy spells,
rheumatic twinges, irregular heart, and to feel tired and worn out before the day is
half through. And so they bear up as best they can, in patience and without com-
plaint, never thinking they can be cured, never suspecting the kidneys as the cause
of their illness.
But it is not right for women to be always ailing, and the best proof of this is
given in the outspoken statements of Sydney women who have been made well, and
kept well, by Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, the great kidney and bladder medicine
for men and women, old and young. Doan’s Pills gently heal the tired, overworked
kidneys, and when the kidneys are well they take out the liquid poisons which cause
the above symptoms, as well as bladder disorders, gravel, dropsy, rheumatism,
lumbago, and wasting away.
TWO
LASTING SYDNEY CURES.
Mrs. S. May, 48 Newlancl Street, Waverley, Sydney,
says: “It is now 13 years since I told you that Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills had cured me of backache and
rheumatism, and gave you permission to publish this
fact for the benefit of other sufferers. I can say
now that my cure was a perfect one, and I have been
well all that time. I had contracted a severe chill
across the loins, and for a couple of years suffered
with lumbago-like pains in the small of my back. I
also had a touch of rheumatism. But Doan’s Back-
ache Kidney Pills cured me 13 years ago, and I am
still quite free of every symptom of kidney complaint.
I have recommended this great kidney remedy to
numbers of sufferers, and they all endorse my good
opinion of it.”
Mrs. A. B. Bruce, “Omapere,” Broughton Road,
Artarmon, Sydney, says: “I suffered for a long time
with severe pains in my back. They were so bad that
I could scarcely move about. Rest was out of the
question. I would be tossing about all night, moaning
with the pain. I could not bend my back, and I had
to neglect my work. I was really very ill, and would
be confined to bed for days at a time. I saw Doan’s
Backache Kidney Fills advertised as a cure for back-
ache. and procured a bottle. After a few doses I got
relief, and then gradually I got better, until I was
quite cured. This did not happen yesterday, but two
vears ago, and lam still well.” Five years later Mrs.
Bruce says: “Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills cured me
of backache seven years ago, and I have been free of
this ailment ever since.”

sorts, shades and sects, including gaunt,
shifty men, shaking off those monstrous in-
firmities that haunt the whiskers of the
misguided sinner who has tried to put down
the accursed drink in one titanic gulp.
“My sympathy for Dorris was something
in the nature of a dissipation. I indulged my-
self in it by the hour. She had an elder
sister on guard, and Dorris’s trouble was a
fixed conviction that the world was against
her. She imagined herself an object of ab-
horrence to man and animal, and had a
piquant idea that if she went abroad in the
streets the pack would get after her.
“It was understood that Miss Dorris
Creeling was progressing favorably. In a
month’s time she might be in a position to
give a glowing testimonial to Sweet Spring
with special mention of the balmy mountain
air and the easterly outlook. Meanwhile she
haunted the great garden, cowering in cover,
shunning everyone, even the faithful sister
Vvho kept watch and ward at a distance.
“Sympathy springs from man’s conscious-
ness of his own abilities. I, too, had had
moments when I began to have glimpses of
myself as a useless by-product, and to specu-
late on the probability of the populace throw-
ing clods at me. I, too, had had twinges that
caught up the strings of my neck and turned
my heart to a cup of tears, realising that
nowhere in the wide, wide world was there
a gentle breast offering as a resting-place for
a head like mine.
“Yes, to a sound man it is nauseous
bathos, but if you are sick with the right
kind of sickness those feelings make hell. So
I yearned towards Dorris Creeling. Anson,
you are wrong. You would be naturally,
There was nothing of that in my yearnings.
.1 would have yearned similarly towards a
cat in distress or an anguished camel. It
simply meant that I was so tuned to com-
passion you couldn’t trail a finger across my
cords without producing a symphony.
“Anyhow, being sorry for the poor girl left
me no time to be sorry for myself, and
within a fortnight I was a young blood again,
careless of the world’s opinion, capable of
looking over Windon’s Slip without feeling
an inward prompting to hurl myself into
the absurd, dotted, theatrical landscape half
a mile below, and fit to put up 10 two-
minute rounds with any censorious middle-
weight who might be moved to cast asper-
sions on the fair fame of the clan Mackaboy.
“I had walked much in the garden, won-
dering earnestly what a strapping young man
might do for a damsel in distress, afflicted
with a horror of the sons of Adam. Gentle-
men, I have an air. You may have noticed
it. I am in the nature of an unintentional
deceiver, an unwitting take-down. When I
cast aside the imnlements of trade and appur-
tenances of office, and walk abroad, nobody
is content to take me for an ambidextrous
pot-jostler or toss-pot merely. A barrister, if
you like, but never a barman. It is no fault
of my own. Observe, I have nothing up my
sleeve. Nothing is done by me to heighten
the delusion, but, on the other hand, I take
no steps to correct the popular error.
“It was akin to the above at the hospice
in the hills. Sweet Springs took me at my
face value, and I had obsequious service and
general respect. The common idea seemed to
be that Patrick Mackaboy was a man of
beans, whereas, towards the finish, Patrick
had not the wherewithal to put down a bran
plot in a comfit box.
“I ma y mention that among those present
was one Horace Canborne, whom I recog-
nised as a frequent client of mine in many
places. He was there to shake off the fag-
end of an old and recurrent delusion con-
cerning a cock ostrich, two blue pelicans, a
Jew lizard, a horned owl, one four-legged
stool, a dugong, two pink dodoes and a sculp-
tured tortoise, all on one rope, which pro-
cession would pass down his throat innne-
diately it caught him bending. Horace
might have put the proprietary and guests
right concerning my inferior status, but it
did not seem to have occurred to him as a
judicious course, and he and the drabble of
passive resisters, who were drinking ginver-
beer and telling each other how the craving
for stimulants had at length been perma-
nently overcome, treated me with fine, old-
fashioned courtesy that should have pro-
voiced suspicions in this amiable bosom.
“Things stirred at Sweet Springs when
what might have been a cat-fight in a clothes-
bag arrived in the dining-room one pleasant
afternoon. Presently the disturbance re-
solved itself into. a woman under the stress
of powerful emotion.
“ Dorris Creeling!’ screamed the woman,
‘Windon’s Slip !’
“If anybody remained to argue it was not
Pat Mackaboy. In about seven minutes I
was at Windon’s Slip, with 40ft. of half-inch
Manila. Already a clamorous congregation
was on the spot, mingling tears, entreaties,
prayers, expostulations, and vain noise. And
down at the bottom of the abrupt
slope, beyond which was Eternity, stood
Dorris Creeling, with her body against
the granite, and her feet against a ledge that
was scarcely more than a ripple on the slimy
rock.
“Past the slope was the happy land, far,
far away. A few had gone that route. You
slid the slope, and then shot into space,
bringing up—long after earthly cares had
ceased—in timbered country so far down,
that the gums looked like bracken in a
cleared paddock ; and that 20ft. slope was so
precipitous that the girl stood almost upright
against the face of it, turned half-side on, her
smudged-in, cavernous eyes lifted to the
people above.
“The elder Miss Greeling, prostrate at the
brink, held by many hands, pleaded in a
long scream of utmost suffering. Her sister
had escaped from her, and precipitated her-
self down the Slip. That she was not now
in the lowlands was the closest thing to
a miracle that has come my way yet.
“The rope was about my waist, and four
reliable honeymooners were on the other end.
I was going over when Dorris spoke :
“ ‘lf you come near me I shall jump!’
she said.
“I did not doubt her for a moment. You
understood at once it was advisable to try
other means. Lying on the brink, I lowered
the looped rope.
“The girl was looking into my eyes. She
pushed herself out from the granite face.
‘I wish to die,’ she said,
“The movement sickened me. I jerked
the rope back, and remained, looking down
at Dorris, while her sister raved entreaties
that scathed your bones like an emery wheel,
“My bright idea came to me from Heaven,
or elsewhere. I ordered them to get the
sister away. I commanded everyone into
the background, with a burst of magnificent
arrogance, and then, lying half over the
abyss, I talked to the girl below.
“ ‘Dorris,’ I said, ‘for God’s sake, Dorris,
listen to me. I love you. I love you with
all my soul. I don’t want anyone in this
world but you. Don’t do this dreadful
thing. I love you. You hear me? You
know what I am saying to you. I love you.’
“It’s sloppy stuff to spoon out here and
now before a gang of hard-doers with
whipped-beer in their ignoble whiskers, but
I did it well then. ’Twas a bluff, but I put
everything good that God had given me into
it, pleading like an archangel, and the girl’s
strange shadowed eyes became luminous as
I talked.
“ ‘Life is not terrible,’ I said, ‘if you can
care for one who would die for you. My ex-
istence, too, has been empty. Oh, you
sweet, white woman, do not rob me of my
new hope. Come back to me. I love ’>’ou.
Come back to me!’
“She never took her eyes off mine, and
little by little I dropped the noose nearer,
pleading with a passion that was like mad-
ness. ‘lf you die, dear one,’ I said, ‘there
can be no life for me. Save me. Save me.
I ask no more but that you will live.’ Lord
knows all I said. There was a sort of fine
frenzy on me, but as I raved, the girl thrust
one arm after the other through the noose,
and let it fall about her body, and ten
seconds later she was safe in her sister’s
arms.”
“Well?” inquired Anson after a pause.
“I deemed it expedient to leave Sweet
Springs two days later.”
“There’s a denouement. Let’s have it.”
“It seems my friend Horace Canborne, in-
spired by a sense of unwholesome humor,
had given it out on the occasion of my first
appearance that I was Patrick Mackaboy,
of The Bar, letting it be inferred that The
Bar was a run on the Darling worth next
but one to a million, and his palsied follow-
ing of renovated sots backed up and spread
the merry jape.”
“Well?”
“Oh, nothing. That is, scarcely anything
worth mentioning. A month later Miss
Dorris Greeling, the well-known, favorite
light operatic artiste, having quite recovered
from her recent indisposition, and being of
sound mind, acting under a misconception
inspired by Horace Canborne’s little fiction,
set going an action for breach of promise
claiming two thousand pounds damages.
Defendant was an impecunious barman, one
Patrick Mackaboy.”
EDWARD DYSON.
At Watson’s Bay.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“IT MT' ENT to Watson’s Bay lately to stay
\ \ till next day with a friend. Rose
up early next morning and took a
walk. The scenery is chiefly composed of
rocks. There is not much to boast about in
the way of rich forest raiment on any of the
points and peninsulas around the Harbor,
but at Watson’s Bay Nature, as if tired of
pretence, shows you her bones without a
stitch of clothes on them. Wondered how
anybody, with a choice in the matter, could
elect to live theie. Iheie is no suif bathing
as there is at Manly, and the hotels don’t
open till 7 a.m. and close when they please,
